% ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
%  ABSTRACT
% ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\chapter*{Abstract}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Abstract}

\textbf{Background.}

Low-dose paediatric chest radiography reduces carcinogenic radiation burden but produces noise-corrupted images that can conceal diagnostic findings. Existing deep learning denoisers achieve high pixel fidelity yet provide no measure of reconstruction confidence, leaving clinicians without a signal to distinguish reliable from uncertain image regions.

\textbf{Objective.}
This dissertation designs, implements, and systematically evaluates the Pathology-Preserving Variational Autoencoder with Hybrid Transformer (PP-VAE-Hformer), a probabilistic denoising framework that simultaneously recovers image quality and produces calibrated, per-pixel uncertainty maps suitable for direct use in the paediatric reporting workflow.

\textbf{Methods.}
A Foi Poisson-Gaussian degradation pipeline ($\alpha{=}0.03$, $\beta{=}0.005$) simulated clinically realistic quantum noise at three severity levels on 5,216 training images from the Kermany paediatric chest radiograph dataset. A 19-arm ablation study (16 principal arms A–P, plus three loss-substitution fine-tuning extensions Q, R, S) evaluated the independent and combined effects of eight loss terms, L2, L1, Charbonnier, heteroscedastic Negative Log-Likelihood (NLL), Multi-Scale Structural Similarity Index Measure (SSIM), Focal Frequency Loss (FFL), Sobel edge supervision, and VGG perceptual loss, together with three Kullback-Leibler (KL) divergence annealing schedules (linear, cyclical, free-bits) and two-stage loss-substitution fine-tuning. All 19 arms, five retrained baseline architectures (DnCNN, IRCNN, FFDNet, DRUNet, SwinIR), and two extended architectural comparisons (NAFNet and SCUNet, retrained from scratch under identical noise conditions) were evaluated on 624 held-out test images using Peak Signal-to-Noise Ratio (PSNR), SSIM, Feature Similarity Index (FSIM), Learned Perceptual Image Patch Similarity (LPIPS), and calibration metrics.

\textbf{Results.}
The optimal deterministic arm (Arm~A, $\mathcal{L}_2$) achieved $34.618 \pm 1.071$~dB PSNR, exceeding all five retrained baselines, Block-Matching and 3-Dimensional Filtering (BM3D) ($30.716$~dB), and a Palette-style conditional diffusion model ($13.52$~dB at 200 epochs, an honest negative finding demonstrating that supervised architectures are substantially more parameter-efficient under constrained training budgets). The best NLL arm (Arm~D, NLL+SSIM+FFL) achieved $33.435$~dB PSNR, $+0.64$~dB above DnCNN, while simultaneously producing calibrated aleatoric uncertainty maps at a cost of $1.2$~dB relative to the MSE baseline. Cyclical KL annealing prevented posterior collapse, closing a $1.10$~dB gap versus linear warmup and enabling VAE arms to reach statistical parity with CNN baselines (Arm~H vs IRCNN: $d{=}{-}0.101$, n.s.). Concurrent SSIM and Sobel edge supervision caused a $-1.747$~dB interference collapse (Arm~M), a novel empirical finding with direct implications for composite loss design. Two-stage loss-substitution fine-tuning achieved the best perceptual quality in the study (Arm~R: LPIPS~$0.1226$, FSIM~$0.9332$).

\textbf{Conclusions.}
PP-VAE-Hformer demonstrates that calibrated per-pixel uncertainty quantification can be added to a state-of-the-art denoising architecture at negligible reconstruction cost, and that the choice of KL annealing schedule, not model capacity, is the critical design variable for probabilistic image restoration. The resulting uncertainty maps provide a directly actionable clinical signal consistent with the ALARA dose-optimisation mandate.

% ============================================================

\chapter{Introduction}
\label{chap:intro}

% ============================================================
\section{Clinical Context: Radiation Burden in Paediatric Imaging}
\label{sec:intro:clinical}
% ============================================================

When a clinician orders a chest radiograph for a sick child, two competing harms are set in motion simultaneously. The radiation required to form the image carries a small but measurable lifetime cancer risk; yet reducing that radiation to protect the child degrades image quality, risking a missed or delayed diagnosis. This dissertation is about resolving that tension through computation.

Multiple large cohort studies confirm that diagnostic radiation is directly carcinogenic in children at doses encountered in routine clinical care \cite{mathews_cancer_2013, hauptmann_brain_2023, bosch_de_basea_gomez_risk_2023, smith-bindman_hematologic_2025}. Children are disproportionately radiosensitive: their rapidly dividing progenitor cells carry higher intrinsic radiation sensitivity, and their longer remaining lifespans provide a multi-decade window for oncogenic mutations to progress \cite{national_research_council_health_2006, pearce_radiation_2012}. Under the Linear No-Threshold (LNT) model endorsed by the International Commission on Radiological Protection (ICRP) and the Biological Effects of Ionizing Radiation (BEIR) VII report \cite{international_commission_on_radiological_protection_2007_2007, national_research_council_health_2006}, any dose carries finite risk. Every unnecessary repeat acquisition ordered because a low-dose image was too noisy to interpret is therefore a preventable harm, as is every escalation from chest radiography to Computed Tomography (CT), which imposes a bone marrow dose approximately 1,400 times higher \cite{smith-bindman_hematologic_2025}. Any computational enhancement algorithm must accordingly preserve pathological findings faithfully: a system that produces visually acceptable images while erasing genuine disease imposes the same diagnostic penalty as no enhancement at all. The full epidemiological evidence is reviewed in Chapter~\ref{chap:lit}.

Global radiological practice is governed by the ALARA (As Low As Reasonably Achievable) principle, which requires imaging dose to be kept as low as possible while preserving diagnostic utility \cite{international_commission_on_radiological_protection_2007_2007}. The \textit{Image Gently} campaign \cite{strauss_image_2009} and professional Diagnostic Reference Levels operationalise this mandate clinically. Reducing dose without sacrificing quality requires advanced computational reconstruction, the subject of this dissertation.

% ============================================================
\section{The Image Quality--Dose Trade-Off}
\label{sec:intro:tradeoff}
% ============================================================

Reducing dose is achieved by lowering the tube current-time product (mAs, milliampere-seconds) or peak kilovoltage (kVp), which proportionally reduces photon fluence and precipitates a non-linear increase in image noise \cite{borges_noise_2020}. Radiographic noise is signal-dependent, characterised by a mixed Poisson-Gaussian model \cite{foi_practical_2008, borges_noise_2020}. If $y(x)$ represents the true signal intensity at pixel $x$, the measured noisy intensity $z(x)$ is modelled as \cite{foi_practical_2008}:

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:intro:noise}
z(x) = y(x) + \eta_P(y(x)) + \eta_G(x)
\end{equation}

$\eta_P(y(x))$ is signal-dependent Poisson quantum noise and $\eta_G(x)$ is signal-independent Gaussian electronic read-out noise. The total noise variance at pixel intensity $y$ is affine in signal \cite{foi_practical_2008, borges_noise_2020}:

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:intro:variance}
\sigma^2(y) = \alpha y + \beta
\end{equation}

where $\alpha$ governs signal-dependent Poisson noise and $\beta$ the signal-independent electronic noise floor.\footnote{A relationship is \emph{affine} in a variable $y$ if it takes the form $f(y) = ay + b$: a linear scaling term plus a constant offset. The variance equation $\sigma^2(y) = \alpha y + \beta$ is affine because two physically distinct contributions are present simultaneously: a Poisson term $\alpha y$ whose magnitude grows with signal intensity, and a constant electronic read-out noise floor $\beta$. This is fundamentally different from additive white Gaussian noise (AWGN), for which $\alpha = 0$ and variance is uniform everywhere in the image. The heteroscedastic noise model matters clinically: bright bone regions have higher absolute pixel variance than dark lung fields, so a loss function that treats all pixels equally implicitly tolerates more error in diagnostically critical low-signal regions.} As dose decreases, the signal $y(x)$ falls and the image enters photon starvation\footnote{\emph{Photon starvation} occurs when the X-ray photon flux reaching the detector falls so low that quantum statistical fluctuations dominate the signal. At normal dose, the large number of photons averaged per pixel makes Poisson noise negligibly small relative to the signal; as dose falls, the relative noise $1/\sqrt{N}$ grows as the photon count $N$ per pixel decreases. Below a critical flux the noise overwhelms anatomical contrast, producing the characteristic mottled ``salt-and-pepper'' texture of a severely under-exposed radiograph \cite{borges_noise_2020}.}: Poisson fluctuations become visible and $\beta$ begins to overwhelm the signal \cite{foi_practical_2008}.

% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\subsection{Understanding Spatial Frequency in a Medical Image}
\label{sec:intro:tradeoff:frequency}
% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

Before examining the clinical consequences of low-dose noise, it is essential to introduce a concept that underpins the entire subsequent argument: \textit{spatial frequency}. Any image can be decomposed into frequency components via the Fourier transform \cite{rahaman_spectral_2019}: \textit{low-frequency} components correspond to regions of gradual brightness change (large anatomical structures, overall organ shapes), while \textit{high-frequency} components correspond to rapid pixel-to-pixel brightness changes (sharp edges, fine vascular detail, interstitial reticulations).

\textbf{Why low-dose noise lives in the high-frequency range.} When a sparse Poisson process deposits photons randomly onto the detector, the resulting speckle pattern creates rapid, irregular pixel-to-pixel brightness fluctuations: the signature of high-frequency noise \cite{foi_practical_2008}. Critically, however, the most diagnostically important pathological features in a chest radiograph also exist in the high-frequency range: the sharp margin of a consolidating pneumonia, the razor-thin pleural line of a pneumothorax, and the fine interstitial reticulations of viral infection \cite{wang_high-frequency_2020}. This creates the fundamental denoising dilemma: low-dose noise and clinically vital pathology overlap almost completely in the high-frequency region of the image spectrum. Any algorithm that simply suppresses high frequencies will remove both the noise and the diagnosis simultaneously.

This frequency-domain perspective is central to understanding why every existing class of reconstruction algorithm fails in a predictable way, and motivates the frequency-sensitive composite loss developed in this dissertation.

% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

The Contrast-to-Noise Ratio (CNR) quantifies the clinical impact of noise: the Rose criterion requires a CNR of at least 5.0 for reliable lesion detection \cite{rose_sensitivity_1948}. As dose falls, CNR drops below this threshold for low-contrast findings such as early pneumonic consolidation or a fine pleural line, increasing false-negative and false-positive rates \cite{bhadra_hallucinations_2021}. Several post-acquisition computational denoising techniques have been developed to recover diagnostic quality without additional radiation exposure.

\textbf{Why uncertainty quantification is a clinical requirement, not an optional extra.} Even a high-quality denoised image carries a clinical limitation: if the model outputs only a single clean-looking reconstruction with no indication of how confident it is, the clinician has no way to know whether a given region of the image has been faithfully restored from the original data or whether the model has filled in detail it guessed from training images of typical anatomy. This distinction matters most in the lung regions with the lowest signal-to-noise ratio, precisely the dark pleural angles and retrocardiac spaces where pathology is easiest to miss. Huang et al.\ \cite{huang_review_2024} identify the absence of calibrated uncertainty as the single largest barrier to clinical adoption of Artificial Intelligence (AI) image analysis models: clinicians cannot assess when to trust a model's output without some measure of that output's reliability. Gillmann et al.\ \cite{gillmann_uncertainty_2021} demonstrate that spatially resolved per-pixel confidence maps, indicating which specific regions of the reconstructed image are most trustworthy, are more clinically actionable than a single scalar confidence score for the whole image. This dissertation therefore requires its denoising model to produce not only a denoised image but also a pixel-level uncertainty map alongside it.

% ============================================================
\section{Existing Approaches and Their Limitations}
\label{sec:intro:existing}
% ============================================================

The history of image denoising can be traced through three successive generations, each building on the limitations of what came before. Classical statistical filters (BM3D, NLM, Total Variation) require no training data but impose hand-crafted priors that over-smooth fine pathology. The deep learning era opened with discriminative CNNs (DnCNN, DRUNet) and later hybrid Transformer architectures (SwinIR, Hformer, NAFNet, SCUNet), which learn directly from data but have relied throughout on pixel-wise losses that blur fine structural detail and produce no uncertainty output. Generative approaches (GANs, Diffusion models) synthesise visually sharp results but introduce hallucination risks that are clinically dangerous. Each generation resolves the failures of the previous one while introducing new limitations of its own.

A key milestone within the Convolutional Neural Network (CNN) generation is \emph{residual learning} \cite{he_deep_2016}: rather than training a network to directly output a clean image, DnCNN \cite{zhang_beyond_2017} trains a network to predict the \emph{noise} itself and subtract it from the input. Formally, if $z$ is the noisy input and $y$ is the clean target, the network learns a mapping $\mathcal{F}(z)$ such that $\hat{y} = z - \mathcal{F}(z)$. This residual formulation is equivalent to inserting an identity skip connection from input to output, so that the network only needs to learn what to \emph{remove} rather than what a clean image looks like. Residual learning remains the structural principle in every descendant architecture, including the candidate models of this dissertation.

A unique constraint of paediatric chest X-ray (CXR) denoising is that true paired (noisy, clean) training data cannot ethically be acquired: no ethics committee would sanction supra-diagnostic re-irradiation of a child for a training set. The Kermany collection \cite{kermany_identifying_2018}, acquired at clinical doses, therefore serves as the clean reference, with noise added computationally via the Foi Poisson-Gaussian model \cite{foi_practical_2008}.

The subsections below examine each method family in detail.

% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\subsection{Traditional Computational Methods}
\label{sec:intro:existing:classical}
% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────

Classical approaches, Non-Local Means (NLM) \cite{buades_non-local_2005}, Total Variation (TV) regularisation \cite{rudin_nonlinear_1992}, wavelet thresholding \cite{donoho_ideal_1994}, and BM3D \cite{dabov_image_2007} (applied after Generalised Anscombe Transform\footnote{The \emph{Generalised Anscombe Transform} (GAT) \cite{makitalo_optimal_2013} is a variance-stabilising transformation. Because Poisson noise has variance proportional to signal intensity, different pixels in the image have different noise levels, and classical denoising methods that assume constant noise cannot be applied directly. The GAT maps the data through a square-root-like function so that the transformed variable has approximately unit variance everywhere, converting heteroscedastic Poisson-Gaussian noise into the additive white Gaussian noise that methods such as BM3D require. The transform is inverted after denoising. Its limitation is that the noise parameters $(\alpha, \beta)$ must be accurately known in advance: when they are estimated from the image itself, miscalibration degrades denoising performance \cite{borges_noise_2020}.} to stabilise Poisson variance), exploit hand-crafted statistical priors and can suppress broadband Gaussian noise. However, they lack the semantic intelligence to distinguish high-frequency quantum noise from high-frequency diagnostic structures (consolidation margins, fine vasculature, interstitial reticulations), resulting in over-smoothing that erases pathology \cite{rudin_nonlinear_1992}. Crucially, none offer uncertainty quantification, and GAT-based methods degrade further when calibration parameters $(\alpha,\beta)$ are unknown \cite{borges_noise_2020}.

% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\subsection{Deep Learning: Supervised Convolutional Neural Networks}
\label{sec:intro:existing:cnn}
% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────

Supervised CNNs (DnCNN \cite{zhang_beyond_2017}, U-Net \cite{ronneberger_u-net_2015}, RED-CNN \cite{chen_low-dose_2017}) learn a direct mapping from degraded input to clean output, efficiently suppressing global noise. Their fundamental limitation is the pixel-wise Mean Squared Error (MSE) / Mean Absolute Error (MAE) loss: minimising MSE drives the network to the conditional mean of all plausible clean images (``regression to the mean''\footnote{\emph{Regression to the mean} in the denoising context describes the tendency of a network trained with MSE loss to output the pixel-wise conditional expectation of all plausible clean images consistent with a given noisy input. Because different valid clean images differ most in their high-frequency content, sharp edges that could plausibly lie slightly to the left or right, fine textures that vary in phase, averaging them together produces a systematically smoother image than any individual realisation. This is a mathematical consequence of minimising $\mathbb{E}[\|y - \hat{y}\|^2]$, not a failure of optimisation: the MSE-optimal predictor is the conditional mean, and the conditional mean blurs fine structure \cite{zhao_loss_2017}.} \cite{zhao_loss_2017}), which mathematically eliminates high-frequency texture and fine structural variance. This blurring is compounded by the \textit{spectral bias} (Frequency Principle \cite{rahaman_spectral_2019, xu_frequency_2020}) of gradient-based training: networks preferentially learn low-frequency components first, systematically under-recovering the sharp margins of consolidating pneumonia and fine interstitial reticulations that carry the diagnostic signal \cite{wang_high-frequency_2020}. Deterministic CNNs also provide no uncertainty output, precluding safe deployment in safety-critical paediatric imaging.

% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\subsection{Deep Learning: Generative Adversarial Networks}
\label{sec:intro:existing:gan}
% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────

Generative Adversarial Networks (GANs) \cite{goodfellow_generative_2014} address CNN blurring via an adversarial discriminator that penalises perceptually unrealistic outputs (pix2pix \cite{isola_image--image_2017}, WGAN-VGG \cite{yang_low-dose_2018}), producing visually sharp reconstructions. Their clinical safety hazard is \textit{hallucination}: the generator may fabricate anatomically plausible but diagnostically false structures (false lung nodules, erased consolidations) because it is incentivised only to fool the discriminator, not to faithfully preserve pathology \cite{bhadra_hallucinations_2021}. This renders pure GAN frameworks medico-legally indefensible in paediatric imaging. Mode collapse (convergence to a small set of outputs regardless of patient anatomy) is an additional failure mode \cite{goodfellow_generative_2014}.

% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\subsection{Deep Learning: Diffusion Models}
\label{sec:intro:existing:diffusion}
% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────

Currently representing the state-of-the-art in generative modelling, Denoising Diffusion
Probabilistic Models (DDPMs) have been rapidly adapted for medical image reconstruction
\cite{ho_denoising_2020, song_score-based_2021}. Diffusion models learn to generate data
by iteratively reversing a Markovian diffusion process,\footnote{A \emph{Markov process} is one in which the next state depends only on the current state, not on any earlier history. In a diffusion model, a \emph{forward} Markov chain corrupts an image by adding a small, fixed amount of Gaussian noise at each of $T \approx 1000$ discrete steps until the image is indistinguishable from pure Gaussian noise. The model learns to run this chain in reverse: starting from noise and iteratively removing a little noise at each step, conditioned on the noisy image input. Because each reversal step requires one full forward pass through a large network, inference at $T=1000$ steps is three orders of magnitude slower than a single-pass CNN \cite{ho_denoising_2020}.} gradually denoising a pure Gaussian
noise matrix into a structured image guided by complex latent representations
\cite{ho_denoising_2020}.

Diffusion models (DDPMs \cite{ho_denoising_2020}) achieve the highest perceptual quality of any generative approach but have two critical clinical barriers: (i) iterative inference requires 100--1,000 sequential forward passes, precluding real-time deployment; and (ii) when trained on datasets dominated by healthy anatomy, the strong diffusion prior ``hallucinates health'': pathological input images are forcibly denoised back towards normal anatomy, actively erasing disease features \cite{kazerouni_diffusion_2023, pfaff_no-new-denoiser_2024}. 

% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\subsection{From Building Blocks to PP-VAE-Hformer: How the Proposed Framework Synthesises All Prior Contributions}
\label{sec:intro:existing:synthesis}
% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────

Each prior generation contributes a distinct capability that the next generation builds upon. Critically, PP-VAE-Hformer (architecture studied in this dissertation) is not a sixth competing family, it is a deliberate synthesis of the best contribution from every prior approach, assembled to address the specific clinical constraints of paediatric CXR denoising.

\textbf{From classical methods}: the insight that images have structured statistical regularity (self-similar patches, smooth regions) is encoded not in a hand-crafted filter but in the learned features of the Hformer encoder.

\textbf{From residual CNNs}: the residual learning principle \cite{he_deep_2016}, train the network to predict and subtract noise rather than directly output a clean image, is preserved at every convolutional layer of the HybridBlock's ResBlocks. Skip connections from encoder to decoder additionally allow fine local detail to bypass the bottleneck compression entirely, preventing information loss.

\textbf{From Transformer architectures}: multi-head windowed self-attention \cite{liu_swin_2021} gives each spatial region the ability to gather context from its neighbours, recovering anatomical structures that span regions larger than a fixed convolution kernel. The Hformer backbone \cite{zhang_hformer_2023} alternates CNN and attention processing in each HybridBlock, combining the local precision of convolutions with the global context of attention.

\textbf{From generative models}: the insight that uncertainty is clinically meaningful, that a model should not produce a single confident output when the input data is ambiguous, is formalised by inserting a Variational Autoencoder (VAE) bottleneck \cite{kingma_auto-encoding_2014} into the Hformer.\footnote{A \emph{Variational Autoencoder} (VAE) \cite{kingma_auto-encoding_2014} is a probabilistic extension of a conventional autoencoder. Rather than compressing the input to a single fixed vector, the encoder outputs a probability distribution, a mean and a variance, over a lower-dimensional \emph{latent space}. The network then \emph{samples} from this distribution to produce a latent code, which the decoder uses to reconstruct the image. Because each forward pass draws a different sample, running the model multiple times on the same input produces slightly different outputs. The spread of those outputs across many runs directly measures how uncertain the model is. The \emph{reparameterisation trick} \cite{kingma_auto-encoding_2014} makes this stochastic sampling differentiable, so the network can be trained end-to-end with standard gradient descent.} The VAE enables two types of uncertainty to be measured simultaneously: \emph{aleatoric uncertainty}\footnote{\emph{Aleatoric uncertainty} is irreducible: it reflects genuine randomness in the data itself, here the quantum shot noise of the X-ray photon flux, which no amount of additional training data or model capacity can eliminate. It is captured by a learned per-pixel variance head $\hat{\sigma}^2_a$ on the decoder output.} (irreducible noise inherent in the X-ray acquisition) and \emph{epistemic uncertainty}\footnote{\emph{Epistemic uncertainty} is reducible: it reflects the model's own ignorance about under-constrained regions, and in principle decreases with more data. It is estimated by running the VAE stochastic forward pass $K=20$ times and measuring the pixel-wise variance across the resulting reconstructions \cite{kendall_what_2017}.} (reducible model uncertainty about under-constrained regions), following the Kendall and Gal \cite{kendall_what_2017} decomposition. Neither pure CNNs, Transformers, nor standard deterministic architectures can produce these outputs. Standard VAEs used alone for denoising produce blurred outputs because their training objective (the ELBO\footnote{The \emph{Evidence Lower BOund} (ELBO) is the VAE training objective. It decomposes as: $\mathrm{ELBO} = \mathbb{E}_{q_\phi(z|x)}[\log p_\theta(x|z)] - D_{\mathrm{KL}}(q_\phi(z|x)\,\|\,p(z))$. The first term rewards faithful reconstruction; the second, the KL divergence, penalises the encoder for producing a posterior that deviates from the standard Gaussian prior. When the likelihood uses a fixed isotropic Gaussian (as in plain VAEs), this reduces to unweighted MSE, producing the same blur problem as CNN L2 training. PP-VAE-Hformer replaces this with the heteroscedastic NLL, SSIM, and FFL terms, eliminating the standard blur pathway.}) collapses to unweighted MSE. PP-VAE-Hformer resolves this by replacing the ELBO's pixel-wise MSE with a composite loss (heteroscedastic NLL, MS-SSIM, Focal Frequency Loss) that specifically targets high-frequency pathological detail, while carefully scheduling the KL divergence weight to prevent posterior collapse.\footnote{\emph{Posterior collapse} occurs when the KL penalty overwhelms the reconstruction signal early in training, causing the encoder's posterior to converge to the prior regardless of the input image. Once collapsed, the latent code carries no patient-specific information and the model outputs the same generic image for every patient. PP-VAE-Hformer prevents this via cyclic KL annealing \cite{fu_cyclical_2019} and a free-bits lower bound on the KL term.}

The result is an architecture that uses CNNs for local texture precision, Transformers for global anatomical context, residual connections for information preservation, and a VAE bottleneck for uncertainty quantification, with a loss function designed around the specific physics of low-dose paediatric radiograph noise rather than the natural image statistics of existing benchmarks.

Table~\ref{tab:intro:comparison} provides a comparative synthesis of these five methodological
families across the dimensions most relevant to paediatric clinical deployment and Figure~\ref{fig:architecture_evolution} depicts on the evolution of the different variants for denoising task of medical images.

    & The VAE component alone (without the composite loss and KL schedule) would blur outputs \cite{bredell_explicitly_2023}; cyclic KL annealing and heteroscedastic NLL jointly prevent this.

\end{landscape}
% ============================================================
\section{Critical Analysis of Literature, Research Motivation, and Gap Identification}
\label{sec:intro:gap}
% ============================================================

The existing literature reveals four interrelated gaps that this dissertation addresses simultaneously.

\textbf{Gap 1: Lack of paediatric specificity.} Existing denoising evaluations target natural images or adult CT; none benchmark on paediatric CXR at clinically relevant resolution with a Poisson-Gaussian noise model \cite{laborie_scoping_2026, yang_medmnist_2023}.

\textbf{Gap 2: No multi-constraint probabilistic framework.} No prior work combines heteroscedastic NLL, structural similarity, and frequency-domain regularisation within a single VAE denoising objective for medical imaging \cite{bredell_explicitly_2023, zhao_loss_2017}.

\textbf{Gap 3: Absence of calibrated pixel-level uncertainty.} Deterministic denoisers produce a single point-estimate reconstruction with no confidence signal, leaving clinicians unable to distinguish image regions where the network is reliably recovering the true signal from those where it is extrapolating under high noise or distributional mismatch. Huang et al.\ \cite{huang_review_2024} identify this as the primary barrier to clinical adoption of medical image analysis models: without uncertainty estimates, clinicians cannot assess when to trust model outputs. Kabir et al.\ \cite{kabir_neural_2018} survey neural network prediction-interval methods and show that heteroscedastic mean-variance estimation (the NLL loss) is the most principled single-pass approach, yet it has not been applied to paediatric CXR denoising. Gillmann et al.\ \cite{gillmann_uncertainty_2021} further demonstrate that spatially resolved per-pixel uncertainty maps, rather than scalar confidence scores, are the most actionable form for radiologists examining specific anatomical regions. VAE-based uncertainty estimation, which naturally separates epistemic (model) and aleatoric (data) components following the Kendall \& Gal \cite{kendall_what_2017} decomposition, has not been evaluated for paediatric CXR denoising with calibration metrics that establish clinical trustworthiness.

\textbf{Gap 4: No explicit frequency-domain regularisation.} The spectral bias of gradient descent \cite{rahaman_spectral_2019, xu_frequency_2020} systematically suppresses high-frequency diagnostic content (vessel margins, consolidation boundaries) in all existing denoisers. No paediatric CXR framework applies a Focal Frequency Loss \cite{jiang_focal_2021} to correct this directly \cite{durall_watch_2020}.

Two uses of the word ``smooth'' appear in this dissertation and must be distinguished. \emph{Over-smoothing} refers to spatial blurring of the reconstructed output image: the suppression of high-frequency anatomical detail (vessel margins, consolidation boundaries, fine reticulations) caused by MSE/L2 objectives minimising the expected squared error, which drives predictions toward the pixel-wise conditional mean and thereby eliminates fine-structure variance. This is the undesirable outcome motivating the composite loss design. \emph{Smooth transitions} in the latent space refers to the continuity of the encoder's mapping $q_\phi(z|x)$: nearby latent codes decode to visually similar images, making interpolation and Monte Carlo sampling meaningful. This is an algebraic property of the latent manifold, enforced by KL regularisation, and is orthogonal to the spatial resolution of individual reconstructed outputs. Improving latent continuity does not cause spatial over-smoothing; it is the mechanism that enables calibrated epistemic uncertainty maps.

The proposed framework, PP-VAE-Hformer, directly addresses each of these gaps. In brief, it inserts a Variational Autoencoder probabilistic bottleneck into a hybrid CNN-Transformer encoder-decoder backbone and trains it with a four-term composite loss combining heteroscedastic negative log-likelihood, structural similarity, focal frequency regularisation, and a KL divergence term. This design is introduced conceptually in Section~\ref{sec:intro:contributions} and specified in full mathematical detail in Chapter~\ref{chap:methods}. Table~\ref{tab:intro:gaps} summarises the gap-to-solution mapping; the primary literature underpinning each gap is reviewed in depth in Chapter~\ref{chap:lit}.

\begin{table}[htbp]
  \centering
  \caption{Gap synthesis in medical image enhancement. Each identified limitation maps to a
  specific design decision in the proposed PP-VAE framework.}
  \label{tab:intro:gaps}
  \small
  \begin{tabularx}{\textwidth}{
    >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{2.8cm} 
    >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{2.2cm} 
    >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}X 
    >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}X
  }
    \toprule
    \textbf{Identified Limitation} &
    \textbf{Evidence Base} &
    \textbf{Clinical Consequence} &
    \textbf{Proposed Solution (PP-VAE)} \\
    \midrule
    Hallucination of anatomical structures
    & \cite{bhadra_hallucinations_2021, kc_sfrc_2024}
    & False-positive diagnoses; erroneous surgical or therapeutic interventions.
    & Avoid pure adversarial objectives; rely on constrained, regularised probabilistic decoders (VAE). \\
    \midrule
    Loss of subtle pathology (over-smoothing)
    & \cite{bredell_explicitly_2023, zhao_loss_2017}
    & False-negative interpretations; failure to detect early-stage pneumonia or interstitial disease.
    & Integrate edge-preservation ($\mathcal{L}_\mathrm{Edge}$) and structural similarity ($\mathcal{L}_\mathrm{SSIM}$) constraints directly into the VAE loss. \\
    \midrule
    Blur-induced error in VAE latent modelling
    & \cite{bredell_explicitly_2023}
    & Concealment of sharp anatomical margins; degraded diagnostic feature extraction.
    & Reformulate VAE reconstruction loss incorporating spatial and frequency-domain penalties without violating the ELBO. \\
    \midrule
    Lack of paediatric CXR specificity
    & \cite{kermany_identifying_2018, laborie_scoping_2026, strauss_image_2009}
    & Models generalise poorly to the unique anatomy, pathology, and low-dose constraints of children.
    & Train and validate exclusively on the full Kermany 2018 paediatric CXR dataset at 256\,$\times$\,256 pixels \cite{kermany_identifying_2018}. \\
    \midrule
    Absence of uncertainty quantification
    & \cite{kendall_what_2017, ovadia_can_2019}
    & Clinicians cannot ascertain algorithmic confidence, leading to over-reliance or blind distrust.
    & Exploit the VAE stochastic latent space $q_\phi(z|x)$ to generate pixel-level epistemic uncertainty maps. \\
    \midrule
    \textbf{Absence of explicit frequency-domain regularisation}
    & \textbf{\cite{rahaman_spectral_2019, xu_frequency_2020, durall_watch_2020}}
    & \textbf{Systematic under-recovery of high-frequency pathological detail due to spectral bias; visually plausible but spectrally distorted reconstructions.}
    & \textbf{Add a Focal Frequency Loss term ($\mathcal{L}_\mathrm{Freq}$) that directly penalises discrepancies in the image frequency spectrum \cite{jiang_focal_2021}.} \\
    \bottomrule
  \end{tabularx}
\end{table}

% ============================================================
\section{Research Aims and Objectives}
\label{sec:intro:aims}
% ============================================================

This dissertation designs, implements, and evaluates a Pathology-Preserving Variational Autoencoder (PP-VAE-Hformer) for low-dose paediatric CXR denoising, with four objectives:

\begin{enumerate}
  \item \textbf{Degradation Modelling:} Apply a Poisson-Gaussian degradation pipeline \cite{foi_practical_2008} at three calibrated severity levels spanning the clinical dose range.

  \item \textbf{Architecture and Objective Design:} Construct a composite loss (NLL, MS-SSIM, FFL, KL) and evaluate each component via a 16-arm ablation study (Arms A--P), directly addressing the spectral bias \cite{rahaman_spectral_2019} and calibration gaps identified above.

  \item \textbf{Pathology Preservation Validation:} Compare PP-VAE-Hformer against five retrained baselines (DnCNN, IRCNN, FFDNet, DRUNet, SwinIR) and two state-of-the-art (SOTA) extensions (NAFNet, SCUNet) on the full Kermany paediatric CXR dataset \cite{kermany_identifying_2018} at $256\times256$ pixels.

  \item \textbf{Uncertainty Quantification:} Evaluate pixel-wise aleatoric and epistemic uncertainty maps using Expected Calibration Error, establishing whether the outputs are clinically trustworthy \cite{kendall_what_2017}.
\end{enumerate}

% ============================================================
\section{Contributions of This Work}
\label{sec:intro:contributions}
% ============================================================

PP-VAE-Hformer replaces the standard pixel-wise loss with a four-term composite objective and inserts a VAE probabilistic bottleneck into an Hformer encoder-decoder backbone. A well-organised latent bottleneck is one in which nearby latent vectors correspond to visually and semantically similar images (continuity), and in which latent dimensions capture independent sources of variation (disentanglement); KL regularisation enforces this structure by constraining the encoder posterior $q_\phi(z|x)$ toward a standard Gaussian prior. The composite loss (full specification in Chapter~\ref{chap:methods}) combines: (i) the heteroscedastic NLL\footnote{\emph{Heteroscedastic} means the variance is not constant but varies spatially. A heteroscedastic negative log-likelihood (NLL) loss treats the decoder output as a Gaussian with a learned, pixel-specific variance $\hat{\sigma}^2_a(x)$: $\mathcal{L}_\mathrm{NLL} = \frac{1}{2}\sum_x \left[\frac{(y_x - \hat{\mu}_x)^2}{\hat{\sigma}^2_{a,x}} + \log\hat{\sigma}^2_{a,x}\right]$. This has two effects: (i) pixels predicted with high uncertainty contribute less to the gradient, so the model is not penalised for noise it cannot eliminate; and (ii) the model is incentivised to predict accurate variances because overestimating $\hat{\sigma}^2_a$ is penalised by the $\log$ term. Crucially, this simultaneously trains both the reconstruction mean $\hat{\mu}$ and the aleatoric uncertainty map $\hat{\sigma}^2_a$ from a single loss term \cite{kendall_what_2017}.} \cite{kendall_what_2017}, predicting per-pixel aleatoric uncertainty $\hat{\sigma}_a$; (ii) multi-scale SSIM\footnote{\emph{Structural Similarity} (SSIM) \cite{wang_image_2004} compares two image patches along three axes: luminance (mean intensity ratio), contrast (standard deviation ratio), and structure (normalised cross-correlation). A value of 1.0 indicates perfect match; 0.0 indicates no structural similarity. \emph{Multi-Scale} SSIM (MS-SSIM) \cite{wang_multiscale_2003} repeats this comparison after progressively downsampling the image, so that both fine-scale texture errors and coarse-scale structural distortions are penalised. As a loss term, $1 - \mathrm{MS\text{-}SSIM}$ penalises the network when its output is structurally dissimilar to the clean reference, complementing the per-pixel NLL by capturing spatial correlations that per-pixel losses ignore.} \cite{wang_multiscale_2003}, penalising blurred structural regions; (iii) Focal Frequency Loss\footnote{The \emph{Focal Frequency Loss} (FFL) \cite{jiang_focal_2021} operates in the Fourier frequency domain rather than the pixel domain. It computes the element-wise squared difference between the Fourier spectra of the predicted and target images, weighted by a matrix that assigns higher importance to frequency components where the current model performs poorly. Because spectral bias causes gradient-based training to learn low-frequency components first, the high-frequency components corresponding to sharp edges and fine pathological texture are systematically under-trained. FFL adaptively redirects the training signal towards these neglected frequencies at each update step, complementing the spatial-domain losses without requiring any architectural change.} \cite{jiang_focal_2021}, adaptively correcting high-frequency spectral errors; and (iv) KL divergence \cite{kingma_auto-encoding_2014}, cyclically annealed to enable Monte Carlo epistemic uncertainty estimation. The combined objective is:

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:intro:totalloss}
\mathcal{L}_\mathrm{total} =
\mathcal{L}_\mathrm{NLL}
+ \beta\,\mathcal{L}_\mathrm{KL}
+ \lambda_{\mathrm{SSIM}}\,\mathcal{L}_\mathrm{SSIM}
+ \lambda_{\mathrm{FFL}}\,\mathcal{L}_\mathrm{FFL}
\end{equation}

The 16-arm ablation (Arms A--P) systematically varies loss combinations, KL schedules, and architectural components. Three loss-substitution fine-tuning extensions (Arms Q, R, S) show that two-stage training from a converged L2 checkpoint achieves the best perceptual quality (Arm~R: LPIPS~$0.1226$, FSIM~$0.9332$) without retraining from scratch. The framework is the first systematic comparison of SOTA denoising architectures on paediatric CXR at $256\times256$ pixel resolution, directly addressing Laborie et al.'s \cite{laborie_scoping_2026} finding that 18 of 20 AI models degrade on paediatric populations. Each input produces three outputs: reconstructed mean $\hat{\mu}$, aleatoric uncertainty map $\hat{\sigma}^2_\mathrm{a}$, and epistemic uncertainty map $\hat{\sigma}^2_\mathrm{e}$ via $K=20$ Monte Carlo VAE samples.\footnote{\emph{Monte Carlo sampling} estimates a quantity by averaging over many independent random draws. In the VAE context, the encoder outputs a distribution rather than a deterministic vector, so each forward pass draws a different latent code $z^{(k)} \sim q_\phi(z|x)$. Running $K=20$ such passes and decoding each produces $K$ slightly different reconstructions. Their pixel-wise mean gives the reconstruction estimate $\hat{\mu}$, and their pixel-wise variance gives the epistemic uncertainty $\hat{\sigma}^2_e$: if all $K$ reconstructions agree tightly, the model is confident; if they spread widely, the model is uncertain. The $K=20$ choice balances statistical stability against inference cost; pilot experiments showed that increasing to $K=100$ altered epistemic maps by less than 1\%.}

% ============================================================
\section{Dissertation Roadmap}
\label{sec:intro:roadmap}
% ============================================================

Chapter~\ref{chap:lit} reviews the epidemiological evidence, architecture evolution, loss function design, and uncertainty quantification literature. Chapter~\ref{chap:methods} specifies the noise model, architecture, and 16-arm ablation. Chapter~\ref{chap:results} presents quantitative and qualitative results for all 19 arms and five baselines. Chapter~\ref{chap:discussion} interprets findings and addresses limitations. Chapter~\ref{chap:conclusion} summarises contributions and future directions.

% ============================================================
% CHAPTER 2 — LITERATURE REVIEW
% ============================================================
\chapter{Literature Review}
\label{chap:lit}

This chapter synthesises the evidence and technical background that motivates and situates PP-VAE-Hformer, covering: the epidemiological case for paediatric dose reduction; the Kermany dataset; the evolution of denoising architectures; loss function design; uncertainty quantification; AI generalisation failure in paediatric radiology; and evaluation metrics.

% ============================================================
\section{The Clinical Case for Paediatric CXR Denoising: Radiation Risk as Direct Justification}
\label{sec:lit:radiation}
% ============================================================

Children who receive medical imaging carry a quantifiable, dose-dependent cancer risk. Four large independent cohort studies now provide direct observational evidence at doses encountered in routine care.

% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\subsection{The Epidemiological Evidence Base}
\label{sec:lit:radiation:evidence}
% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

The LNT model \cite{international_commission_on_radiological_protection_2007_2007, national_research_council_health_2006} predicts linear cancer risk with dose and no safe threshold. Four large cohort studies have replaced inference with direct observation. Mathews et al.\ \cite{mathews_cancer_2013} followed 680,000 Australian children for up to 24 years after CT, finding an incidence rate ratio of 1.24. The EPI-CT consortium (948,174 patients, nine countries) found an Excess Relative Risk (ERR) of 1.27 per 100\,mGy for brain tumours \cite{hauptmann_brain_2023} and 1.96 per 100\,mGy for haematological malignancies with elevated risk detectable at bone marrow doses as low as 10--15\,mGy \cite{bosch_de_basea_gomez_risk_2023}. Smith-Bindman et al.\ \cite{smith-bindman_hematologic_2025} followed 3.7 million children across the USA and Canada; their findings are summarised in Table~\ref{tab:lit:dose}.

\begin{table}[H]
  \centering
  \caption{Radiation dose and attributable cancer risk by imaging modality in children.}
  \label{tab:lit:dose}
  \small
  \begin{tabular}{l c c c}
    \toprule
    \textbf{Modality} &
    \textbf{Mean bone marrow dose (mGy)} &
    \textbf{ERR per 100 mGy} &
    \textbf{Attributable cancer risk} \\
    \midrule
    Chest radiograph & 0.01 & --- & 0.03\,\% \\
    Chest CT          & 2.8  & 2.54 & 6.0\,\% \\
    Head CT           & 13.7 & 2.54 & 25.9\,\% \\
    Abdominal CT      & 10.4 & 2.54 & 21.4\,\% \\
    \bottomrule
  \end{tabular}

  \vspace{0.01em}
  {\footnotesize \textbf{Note:} The contrast between chest radiography and head CT (a factor
  of over 800 in attributable risk) illustrates why preventing unnecessary escalation from
  X-ray to CT is clinically meaningful. ERR = excess relative risk. Source: Smith-Bindman 
  et al.\ (2025), \textit{N Engl J Med}, 392(1):44--56.}
\end{table}

The 1,400-fold dose difference between a chest radiograph (0.01\,mGy) and a head CT (13.7\,mGy) is the key clinical fact: preventing escalation to CT is the single most effective radiation protection mechanism available \cite{smith-bindman_hematologic_2025}. ERR estimates are consistent across all four studies (1.3--2.5 per 100\,mGy depending on cancer type), confirming that diagnostic radiation causes cancer in children at doses encountered in routine care.

% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\subsection{Why Preventing Imaging Escalation Is the Key Mechanism}
\label{sec:lit:radiation:escalation}
% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

A chest radiograph at 0.01\,mGy carries near-zero per-exam cancer risk. This makes it the safest modality, but only if the image is diagnostically adequate. When an inadequate low-dose radiograph prompts escalation to chest CT, the bone marrow dose rises 280-fold; escalation to head CT raises it 1,400-fold \cite{smith-bindman_hematologic_2025}. Children with chronic conditions (cystic fibrosis, congenital heart disease) accumulate tens to hundreds of CXRs across childhood \cite{johnson_cumulative_2014}, so each inadequate image compounds into a higher lifetime risk. Effective denoising eliminates this dilemma by making low-dose images diagnostically complete without repeat acquisition or escalation.

Lovrenski et al.\ \cite{lovrenski_esr_2025} on behalf of the European Society of Paediatric Radiology (ESPR) codified a three-tier hierarchy in 2025: chest radiograph and lung ultrasound (LUS) as initial tools, CT reserved for complex cases. The proposed framework directly operationalises this pathway: it maximises clinical decisions within the lowest-radiation tier.

% ============================================================
\section{The Kermany Paediatric Chest X-Ray Dataset}
\label{sec:lit:kermany}
% ============================================================

The Kermany et al.\ \cite{kermany_identifying_2018} collection comprises 5,856 anterior-posterior chest radiographs from children aged 1--5 years, labelled as normal or pneumonia (bacterial/viral). The full-resolution collection is used at $256\times256$ pixels rather than the $28\times28$ MedMNIST \cite{yang_medmnist_2023} derivative\footnote{Initial experiments of the dissertation were conducted in the low-resolution domain of this dataset, PneumoniaMNIST; see Appendix section~xxx for more details on the preliminary results.}, because diagnostically relevant high-frequency structures (pleural edges, consolidation margins) are absent at low resolution. Dataset partitioning, preprocessing, and training split are detailed in Section~\ref{sec:methods:dataset}.

Figure~\ref{fig:lit:kermany:cxr} shows representative images from the three classes. Each exhibits radiological features specific to the paediatric age group (1--5 years) that are systematically absent from adult CXR training sets: a prominent anterior mediastinal opacity from the thymus (which involutes by age three to four), a cardiothoracic ratio of up to 55\% (vs.\ the 50\% adult threshold), horizontally oriented ribs reflecting the barrel-chested configuration of young children, and proportionally elevated diaphragm domes \cite{laborie_scoping_2026}. Bacterial pneumonia presents as lobar or segmental consolidation --- a homogeneous airspace opacity that is spatially localised (commonly right lower or right upper lobe in children) and radiographically distinct from the parenchyma \cite{world_health_organisation_classification_2020}. Viral pneumonia presents as bilateral perihilar interstitial infiltrates: diffuse, hazy, reticular opacities symmetrically involving both lung fields without discrete lobe boundaries \cite{kermany_identifying_2018}. These two patterns require different denoising fidelity: consolidated regions require sharp boundary preservation (lobar margins carry diagnostic specificity); interstitial regions require fine texture preservation (fine reticulation carries severity information). A denoiser that over-smooths either pattern changes the diagnosis.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\linewidth]{figures/lit_cxr_comparison}
\caption[Representative paediatric chest radiographs from the Kermany dataset (Normal, Bacterial, Viral)]{%
  Representative anterior-posterior chest radiographs from the Kermany 2018 test set
  \citep{kermany_identifying_2018}, one image per class. Yellow arrows (Normal): key
  anatomical features specific to paediatric CXR that differ from adult imaging ---
  prominent thymus shadow (normal, involutes by age~3--4), large cardiac silhouette
  (cardiothoracic ratio $\leq$55\%), horizontal ribs, and costophrenic angles.
  Red arrows (Bacterial): lobar consolidation in the right lower zone; homogeneous
  airspace opacity with a sharp medial border.
  Blue arrows (Viral): bilateral perihilar interstitial infiltrates; diffuse fine
  reticular haziness without discrete lobe boundaries.
  All three classes are represented in the 5,856-image dataset used for training and evaluation.
  Adult AI models trained on NIH ChestX-ray14 or similar adult datasets are not calibrated
  for these paediatric anatomical features \citep{laborie_scoping_2026}.}
\label{fig:lit:kermany:cxr}
\end{figure}

% ============================================================
\section{The Evolution of Image Denoising Architectures}
\label{sec:lit:architectures}
% ============================================================

Table~\ref{tab:lit:architectures} and Figure~\ref{fig:architecture_evolution} trace the evolution from classical to state-of-the-art deep learning denoisers. Each class is evaluated against the requirements of paediatric CXR denoising.

% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\subsection{Classical Methods}
\label{sec:lit:architectures:classical}
% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

Classical methods (BM3D \cite{dabov_image_2007}, NLM \cite{buades_non-local_2005}, TV \cite{rudin_nonlinear_1992}) exploit statistical self-similarity without semantic understanding. BM3D achieves 28.57\,dB on BSD68 at $\sigma=25$ but over-smooths the fine interstitial reticulations and vessel margins that carry diagnostic information about respiratory infection \cite{rudin_nonlinear_1992, dabov_image_2007}.

% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\subsection{Convolutional Neural Networks}
\label{sec:lit:architectures:cnn}
% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

DnCNN \cite{zhang_beyond_2017} established residual learning for blind Gaussian denoising, achieving 29.23\,dB on BSD68 at $\sigma=25$. RED-CNN \cite{chen_low-dose_2017} adapted the U-Net architecture to low-dose CT, preserving fine structures via skip connections. DRUNet \cite{zhang_drunet_2021} (32.7M parameters, noise-conditioned) set the CNN ceiling at 29.48\,dB. All CNN denoisers share the pixel-wise MSE limitation detailed in Section~\ref{sec:lit:losses:pixelwise}: training on squared pixel error destroys the sharp margins of consolidating pneumonia by driving the output toward the average of all plausible clean images.

% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\subsection{Transformer and Hybrid Architectures}
\label{sec:lit:architectures:transformer}
% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

The idea of combining an autoencoder (encoder-decoder) structure with both CNN and Transformer components in a single medical image processing architecture was pioneered by Chen et al.\ \cite{chen_transunet_2021} in TransUNet, which replaced the U-Net encoder's convolutional layers with a Transformer, using the U-Net's skip connections to preserve spatial detail. This established the template that subsequent image restoration architectures would follow. SwinIR \cite{liang_swinir_2021} applied Swin Transformer windowed self-attention to image restoration, achieving 29.50\,dB on BSD68 at $\sigma=25$. Hformer \cite{zhang_hformer_2023}, with only 1.65M parameters, alternates convolutional residual blocks with lightweight Transformer attention and serves as the backbone for PP-VAE-Hformer. Xu et al.\ \cite{xuLowdoseComputedTomography2025} proposed a related hybrid (StoBlock+RDN+attention) for LDCT, demonstrating broad applicability of local-dense plus global-context processing, but with no uncertainty output. NAFNet \cite{chen_nafnet_2022} achieved 29.52\,dB on BSD68 by replacing non-linearities with simple gating, showing architectural simplicity can match complexity. SCUNet \cite{zhang_scunet_2023}, the current ceiling at 29.55\,dB, combines Swin Transformer and convolutional branches in each block and includes Poisson noise in its training synthesis. Table~\ref{tab:lit:architectures} summarises benchmark results across all methods.

\begin{table}[H]
  \centering
  \caption{PSNR (dB) on BSD68 greyscale denoising: classical methods to state of the art.}
  \label{tab:lit:architectures}
  \small
  \begin{tabular}{l c c c c c c}
    \toprule
    \textbf{Method} & \textbf{Year} & \textbf{Tier} & \textbf{Loss} &
    \textbf{$\sigma=15$} & \textbf{$\sigma=25$} & \textbf{$\sigma=50$} \\
    \midrule
    \multicolumn{7}{l}{\textit{Classical methods (no training data)}} \\
    BM3D           & 2007 & Classical     & n/a         & 31.73 & 28.57 & 25.62 \\
    WNNM           & 2014 & Classical     & n/a         & 31.37 & 29.41 & 25.87 \\
    \midrule
    \multicolumn{7}{l}{\textit{Tier 1, CNN: Basic residual}} \\
    DnCNN          & 2017 & CNN-basic     & \textbf{L2} & 31.73 & 29.23 & 26.23 \\
    IRCNN          & 2017 & CNN-P\&P      & \textbf{L2} & 31.63 & 29.15 & 26.22 \\
    \midrule
    \multicolumn{7}{l}{\textit{Tier 2, CNN: Explicit noise conditioning}} \\
    FFDNet         & 2018 & CNN-$\sigma$  & \textbf{L2} & 31.63 & 29.19 & 26.29 \\
    \midrule
    \multicolumn{7}{l}{\textit{Tier 3, CNN: Non-local and frequency-domain}} \\
    N3Net          & 2018 & CNN-nonlocal  & \textbf{L2} & 31.78 & 29.30 & 26.39 \\
    MWCNN          & 2018 & CNN-wavelet   & \textbf{L2} & 31.86 & 29.41 & 26.53 \\
    FOCNet         & 2019 & CNN-math      & \textbf{L2} & 31.83 & 29.38 & 26.50 \\
    RNAN           & 2019 & CNN-attn      & \textbf{L2} & 31.86 & 29.44 & 26.48 \\
    \midrule
    \multicolumn{7}{l}{\textit{Tier 4, CNN: Deep and conditioned (ceiling)}} \\
    DRUNet         & 2021 & CNN-deep      & \textbf{L2} & \textbf{31.91} & \textbf{29.48} & \textbf{26.59} \\
    \midrule
    \multicolumn{7}{l}{\textit{Tier 5, Transformer / Hybrid (current SOTA)}} \\
    SwinIR         & 2021 & Transformer   & Charbonnier & 31.97 & 29.50 & 26.58 \\
    NAFNet         & 2022 & Conv no-act.  & MAE         & 31.99 & $\approx$29.52 & --- \\
    SCUNet\textsuperscript{\dag} & 2023 & SC-hybrid & \textbf{L2} & \textbf{31.99} & \textbf{29.55} & \textbf{26.67} \\
    Hformer        & 2023 & CNN+attn      & \textbf{L2} & --- & --- & --- \\
    \bottomrule
  \end{tabular}

  \vspace{0.4em}
  {\footnotesize \textbf{Notes:} Higher PSNR is better; bold indicates best in tier.
  Performance gains come primarily from architectural innovation; the field has relied on
  L2 or Charbonnier loss throughout. Sources: all numbers from Liang et al.\ (2021, Table~5)
  unless noted. \textsuperscript{\dag}SCUNet numbers from Zhang et al.\ (2023, Table~1).
  Hformer did not report BSD68 results in the original publication.
  Results for PP-VAE-Hformer are reported separately in Chapter~\ref{chap:results} and are
  not included here because they were obtained on a different domain (Kermany paediatric CXR,
  Poisson-Gaussian noise) and are not directly comparable to the AWGN BSD68 benchmark above.}
\end{table}
% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\subsection{Deterministic versus Probabilistic Encoders}

The encoder-decoder architectures reviewed above are all \emph{deterministic}: the encoder maps each input to a fixed latent point $\bar{z} = f_\phi(x)$, with no constraint on the distribution of $\bar{z}$ across the training set. A \emph{convolutional autoencoder} (CAE) minimises only the reconstruction loss $\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{recon}}$; the latent space is therefore unconstrained, with no guarantee that similar inputs map to similar codes or that interpolation between codes produces meaningful images.

A \emph{variational autoencoder} (VAE; Kingma \& Welling, 2014~\cite{kingma_auto-encoding_2014}) changes this by adding a KL divergence regulariser $D_{\mathrm{KL}}[q_\phi(z|x) \| p(z)]$ that constrains the encoder to output the parameters $(\mu_\phi(x), \sigma^2_\phi(x))$ of a Gaussian distribution $q_\phi(z|x) \approx \mathcal{N}(\mu_\phi, \sigma^2_\phi)$, from which the latent code $z$ is stochastically sampled via the reparameterisation trick $z = \mu_\phi + \sigma_\phi \odot \varepsilon$, $\varepsilon \sim \mathcal{N}(0,I)$. This single change produces three consequences not available in a CAE: (i)~a continuous interpolable latent space, so that similar inputs reliably map to nearby codes; (ii)~generative capability, allowing novel images to be synthesised by sampling $z \sim p(z)$ and decoding; (iii)~principled epistemic uncertainty estimation via Monte Carlo sampling of $q_\phi(z|x)$, which is the mechanism PP-VAE-Hformer exploits for calibrated per-pixel uncertainty maps.

PP-VAE-Hformer extends the VAE by adding a heteroscedastic decoder head that simultaneously predicts a per-pixel mean $\hat{\mu}$ and aleatoric variance $\hat{\sigma}^2_a$, enabling it to model the signal-dependent Poisson-Gaussian noise and provide a data-reliability map alongside the reconstruction. This separation of epistemic and aleatoric uncertainty following the Kendall \& Gal~\cite{kendall_what_2017} decomposition is the key distinction from all comparison architectures in Table~\ref{tab:lit:architectures}.

% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\subsection{Why Architecture Alone Cannot Bridge the Gap}
\label{sec:lit:architectures:plateau}
% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

Table~\ref{tab:lit:architectures} reveals a critical pattern. The BM3D-to-DnCNN step (28.57 to 29.23\,dB) reflects a genuine paradigm shift to data-driven learning. But the subsequent twelve years of architectural innovation from DnCNN (2017) to SCUNet (2023) add only 0.32\,dB in total: each of six distinct architectural ideas (N3Net, MWCNN, FOCNet, RNAN, DRUNet, SwinIR) contributes 0.02--0.07\,dB. The trajectory is flat.

One of the reasons this dissertation hypothesizes is that throughout this progression, the loss function has never changed. Every method uses L2, MAE, or Charbonnier, all pixel-wise objectives that treat each pixel independently and make no provision for signal-dependent Poisson noise. Blau and Michaeli \cite{blau_perception_2018} proved that any pure distortion-minimising objective (like MSE, or MAE) is Pareto-dominated by methods that also account for perceptual quality, meaning that optimising for both simultaneously involves an inherent trade-off between them. Zhao et al.\ \cite{zhao_loss_2017} showed in 2017 that replacing L2 with a composite structural loss yields 0.5--1.0\,dB improvements without any architecture change, yet this finding was not adopted by any subsequent architecture in Table~\ref{tab:lit:architectures}.

The spectral bias of gradient descent \cite{rahaman_spectral_2019} compounds this: networks learn low-frequency components faster, systematically under-recovering fine anatomical margins regardless of architecture. Only a loss function that explicitly penalises high-frequency errors, such as the Focal Frequency Loss \cite{jiang_focal_2021}, can directly address this. The loss function is the unexploited lever.

% ─── Cross-Cutting Limitations Table ─────────────────────────────────────────
\begin{table}[H]

    \toprule
