% ============================================================
\section{Loss Functions for Image Restoration}
\label{sec:lit:losses}
% ============================================================

The loss function defines what ``better reconstruction'' means, making it the appropriate locus for the principal contribution of this dissertation.

% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\subsection{Why Pixel-Wise Losses Fail for Medical Imaging}
\label{sec:lit:losses:pixelwise}
% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

The three pixel-norm losses that appear in this study -- Mean Squared Error (MSE / L2), Mean Absolute Error (MAE / L1), and the Charbonnier norm -- all train the network to produce a reconstruction whose pixel values are numerically close to the clean reference, but they define \emph{closeness} differently, and that difference has measurable consequences for image sharpness.

\textbf{MSE (L2).} MSE penalises errors quadratically: doubling the error quadruples the penalty ($2^2 = 4$). When multiple different clean images are all consistent with a given noisy input, the network minimising MSE outputs their pixel-wise \emph{average} -- the conditional mean. Averaged images are always smoother than any individual realisation, because sharp edges that could plausibly sit a little to the left or right get blended into a smear. This is called \emph{regression to the mean} (defined in Section~\ref{sec:intro:existing:cnn}) and is a mathematical property of MSE, not a failure of training. \emph{Advantage}: widely studied; fast; numerically stable; directly interpretable. \emph{Disadvantage}: systematically removes high-frequency anatomical detail (fine vessel margins, pleural lines, consolidation boundaries) that are diagnostically critical in paediatric CXR \cite{zhao_loss_2017}.

\textbf{MAE / L1.} MAE penalises errors linearly: doubling the error doubles the penalty ($2 \times 1 = 2$). The network minimising MAE outputs the pixel-wise \emph{median} of plausible clean images rather than the mean. For symmetric noise distributions -- as Poisson-Gaussian noise approximates at moderate photon counts -- the median and mean coincide, so L1 and L2 produce near-identical reconstructions in practice \cite{zhao_loss_2017}. \emph{Advantage}: less sensitive to occasional very large errors; in some imaging regimes produces marginally sharper texture. \emph{Disadvantage}: not differentiable at exactly zero error (a technical inconvenience that requires a subgradient approximation, though this rarely affects results in practice).

\textbf{Charbonnier norm.} The Charbonnier penalty \cite{charbonnier_two_1994} ($\sqrt{\|\hat{x}-x\|^2+\varepsilon^2} - \varepsilon$, where $\varepsilon$ is a small positive constant) smoothly interpolates between L2 behaviour for small errors and L1 behaviour for large errors. It is differentiable everywhere, avoiding the kink of pure L1, while retaining robustness to outliers. SwinIR \cite{liang_swinir_2021} adopted Charbonnier as its default pixel norm. \emph{Advantage}: differentiable everywhere; smooth training gradients; often preferred when both stability and edge fidelity matter. \emph{Disadvantage}: introduces one additional hyperparameter ($\varepsilon$); in practice, differences from L1 are negligible under Gaussian-like noise.

\textbf{Key limitation shared by all three.} None of these losses trains the network to quantify how uncertain it is about the reconstruction at any given pixel. They all produce a single ``best guess'' image with no mechanism for flagging regions where the denoised value is unreliable. Zhao et al.\ \cite{zhao_loss_2017} showed that composite MS-SSIM+L1 objectives improve perceptual quality by 0.5--1.0\,dB over pure MSE on natural images, but the structural uncertainty gap is only addressed by the heteroscedastic NLL (Section~\ref{sec:lit:losses:nll}).

% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\subsection{Structural Similarity as a Perceptual Objective}
\label{sec:lit:losses:ssim}
% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

SSIM \cite{wang_image_2004} jointly evaluates local luminance, contrast, and structural correlation between image patches, better reflecting human visual perception than MSE. MS-SSIM \cite{wang_multiscale_2003} extends this to five spatial scales. Using 1-SSIM as a training loss produces cleaner reconstructions than MSE on perceptual benchmarks \cite{zhao_loss_2017}. Zhang et al.\ \cite{zhang_comprehensive_2012} found MS-SSIM ranks fourth of 11 quality metrics (Spearman correlation 0.89 vs human judgement), well above PSNR (0.69).

% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\subsection{The Heteroscedastic Negative Log-Likelihood Loss}
\label{sec:lit:losses:nll}
% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

Kendall and Gal \cite{kendall_what_2017} introduced the foundational distinction between two qualitatively different sources of uncertainty in deep learning predictions. \emph{Aleatoric uncertainty} is irreducible: it arises from inherent randomness in the data-generating process (such as photon shot noise) and cannot be reduced by collecting more training data or improving the model -- it is a property of the acquisition, not the model. \emph{Epistemic uncertainty} is reducible: it reflects the model's ignorance about the correct function, which can in principle be eliminated with sufficient data or a better architecture specification. For image denoising, aleatoric uncertainty maps directly to the Foi Poisson-Gaussian variance $ay+b$ at each pixel; epistemic uncertainty is highest in anatomical regions or pathology types that were rare or absent in training. For aleatoric uncertainty, the appropriate training loss is the heteroscedastic NLL:

\begin{equation}
\label{eq:lit:nll}
\mathcal{L}_\mathrm{NLL} = \frac{1}{2} \sum_i \left[
  \frac{(y_i - \hat{\mu}_i)^2}{\exp(\hat{s}_i)} + \hat{s}_i
\right]
\end{equation}

where $y_i$ is the true pixel, $\hat{\mu}_i$ the predicted mean, and $\hat{s}_i = \log\hat{\sigma}^2_i$ the predicted log-variance. High-uncertainty pixels are down-weighted in the reconstruction loss; the regularisation term $\hat{s}_i$ prevents trivial collapse to infinite variance. The result is a network that simultaneously produces a reconstruction and a calibrated per-pixel uncertainty map \cite{kendall_what_2017}, grounded in the physical noise statistics of the input.

% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\subsection{Focal Frequency Loss}
\label{sec:lit:losses:ffl}
% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

The Focal Frequency Loss (FFL) \cite{jiang_focal_2021} addresses spectral bias directly by computing the 2D discrete Fourier transform of both reconstruction and target, penalising discrepancies with adaptive weights that concentrate gradient pressure on the frequency components currently reconstructed most poorly. Because spectral bias causes high-frequency under-recovery in early training, FFL targets exactly the range carrying fine diagnostic detail. Durall et al.\ \cite{durall_watch_2020} showed that visually convincing outputs can contain measurable high-frequency spectral distortions invisible to PSNR or SSIM; FFL corrects these directly.

% ============================================================
\section{Uncertainty Quantification and the Limits of Generative Denoising}
\label{sec:lit:uncertainty}
% ============================================================

Bhadra et al.\ \cite{bhadra_hallucinations_2021} showed that an inaccurate learned prior produces a quantifiable hallucination map, while Pfaff et al.\ \cite{pfaff_no-new-denoiser_2024} demonstrated that diffusion models applied to medical images frequently create or remove anatomical detail. The VAE \cite{kingma_auto-encoding_2014} is trained by maximising the Evidence Lower BOund (ELBO; formal derivation in Appendix~\ref{app:maths:elbo}):
\begin{equation*}
\mathrm{ELBO} = \mathbb{E}_{q_\phi(z|x)}\bigl[\log p_\theta(x|z)\bigr] - D_{\mathrm{KL}}\bigl(q_\phi(z|x)\,\|\,p(z)\bigr)
\end{equation*}
The first term rewards faithful reconstruction; the second constrains the latent representation close to a standard Gaussian prior, preventing unconstrained generative leaps while anchoring the output to the input.

The stochasticity of the VAE lies entirely in the sampling step $z \sim q_\phi(z|x)$. The encoder itself is a deterministic function of its inputs: it outputs the parameters $(\mu_\phi(x), \sigma^2_\phi(x))$ of a Gaussian distribution deterministically. The actual latent sample is drawn using the reparameterisation trick (Equation~\ref{eq:app:reparam}): $z = \mu_\phi + \sigma_\phi \odot \varepsilon$, $\varepsilon \sim \mathcal{N}(0,I)$ \cite{kingma_auto-encoding_2014}, which makes the sampling step differentiable and compatible with standard gradient-based training. This contrasts with a conventional convolutional autoencoder (CAE), which maps each input to a fixed deterministic point $\bar{z} = f_\phi(x)$ with no distributional constraint; the stochasticity in the VAE enters \emph{only} through $\varepsilon$. Repeated forward passes therefore draw different $\varepsilon^{(k)}$ and produce different reconstructions $\hat{\mu}^{(k)} = g_\theta(z^{(k)})$; the pixel-wise variance across $K$ passes directly quantifies epistemic uncertainty \cite{kendall_what_2017}.

% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\subsection{Signal Retrieval Versus Prior Sampling}
\label{sec:lit:uncertainty:retrieval}
% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

A denoised and a hallucinated image can be visually indistinguishable. The Bayesian framework makes the distinction precise: the posterior $p(y \mid z) \propto p(z \mid y)\,p(y)$ is dominated by the likelihood term $p(z \mid y)$ when signal-to-noise ratio (SNR) is high -- the observed data strongly constrains the reconstruction -- and dominated by the prior $p(y)$ when SNR is low. The former regime is \emph{signal retrieval}: the denoised pixel is data-supported. The latter is \emph{prior sampling}: the denoised pixel reflects what the model has learned about typical anatomy from training, not what was actually measured. Under the Foi model, dark anatomical regions (pleural angles, retrocardiac spaces) have the lowest SNR and are therefore necessarily prior-sampled to the greatest degree. Mutual information theory places a hard, fundamental limit: no algorithm can recover information that is absent from the observation, regardless of model capacity \cite{lucas_dont_2019}. An L2-trained network outputs a single point estimate $\hat{y}$ with no signal about which regime applies at any given pixel, making hallucination detection impossible at inference time.

The NLL-trained aleatoric variance $\hat{\sigma}^2 \approx ay+b$ \cite{foi_practical_2008, kendall_what_2017} functions as a data-reliability map: low $\hat{\sigma}^2$ indicates signal retrieval; high $\hat{\sigma}^2$ indicates prior-sampling and warrants interpretive caution. No comparison method produces such an output (Table~\ref{tab:lit:gaps}).

% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\subsection{Uncertainty Map as a Clinical LUS Referral Guide}
\label{sec:lit:uncertainty:workflow}
% ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

The aleatoric uncertainty map integrates directly with the ESPR three-tier pathway \cite{lovrenski_esr_2025}: high-$\hat{\sigma}^2$ pixels identify anatomical regions where lung ultrasound (LUS) should be targeted to provide radiation-free confirmation. Calibration of the uncertainty map via Expected Calibration Error (ECE) (Chapter~4) is the clinical quality criterion: a poorly calibrated map that assigns low $\hat{\sigma}^2$ to prior-sampled pixels is clinically dangerous, like a mislabelled medication. No comparison method reports calibration.

% ============================================================
\section{Artificial Intelligence in Paediatric Radiology: Documented Generalisation Failure}
\label{sec:lit:paediatric}
% ============================================================

Laborie et al.\ \cite{laborie_scoping_2026} conducted a systematic scoping review of 20 studies of AI in paediatric radiology. Eighteen showed performance degradation when adult-trained models were applied to paediatric populations; sensitivity for nodule detection fell to 26--68\% (vs. above 90\% in adult benchmarks). Only four of 213 commercially available AI diagnostic devices were specifically labelled for paediatric use.

Three mechanisms drive this failure: (i) paediatric chest anatomy (thymic width, cardiac-to-chest ratio, diaphragm height) differs substantially from adult anatomy; (ii) paediatric facilities use lower doses and different beam energies, producing distinct noise statistics; and (iii) the dominant paediatric pathologies (viral bronchiolitis, round pneumonia, foreign body aspiration) are rare or absent from adult training sets. A denoising model trained on adult CT or natural images cannot be applied to paediatric CXR without paediatric-specific training and evaluation; the Kermany dataset directly addresses this requirement.

% ============================================================
\section{Evaluation of Image Quality: Moving Beyond PSNR}
\label{sec:lit:iqametrics}
% ============================================================

Zhang et al.\ \cite{zhang_comprehensive_2012} compared 11 full-reference quality metrics against human subjective scores across seven benchmark datasets. PSNR ranked last (Spearman $\rho = 0.69$); FSIM (Feature Similarity Index \cite{zhang_fsim_2011}) ranked first ($\rho = 0.91$), weighting edge regions by phase congruency. PSNR is reported throughout for comparability with prior work, but FSIM is the primary perceptual metric, grounded in this empirical ranking.

% ============================================================
\section{Synthesis: The Identified Research Gap}
\label{sec:lit:gap}
% ============================================================

The four gaps identified in Chapter~\ref{chap:intro} are confirmed by the literature reviewed here. As Section~\ref{sec:lit:architectures:plateau} showed, twelve years of architectural innovation added only 0.32\,dB on BSD68, while the loss function has never changed from pixel-wise objectives. No prior work applies a composite loss combining NLL, MS-SSIM, FFL, and KL within a single VAE framework on paediatric CXR. No comparison method produces calibrated uncertainty maps. No study evaluates on paediatric CXR at $256\times256$ with a physically principled Poisson-Gaussian noise model. Table~\ref{tab:lit:gaps} quantifies these gaps across all methods.

These four gaps constitute the experimental mandate for this dissertation. Chapter~\ref{chap:methods} responds to each one directly: the Poisson-Gaussian noise model (Section~\ref{sec:methods:noise}) addresses the physics gap; the composite four-term loss (Section~\ref{sec:methods:loss}) addresses the loss-function gap; calibrated uncertainty quantification via ECE (Section~\ref{sec:methods:evaluation:calibration}) addresses the calibration gap; and the Kermany evaluation framework (Section~\ref{sec:methods:dataset}) addresses the paediatric domain gap. Together, the sixteen ablation arms and five retrained baselines described there form the most systematic loss-function comparison yet reported for probabilistic paediatric chest radiograph denoising.

% ============================================================
% CHAPTER 3 — METHODS
% ============================================================
\chapter{Methods}
\label{chap:methods}

This chapter specifies the complete experimental design of PP-VAE-Hformer. Every modelling choice is motivated by the clinical and technical gaps identified in Chapters~\ref{chap:intro} and~\ref{chap:lit}, and every equation is accompanied by the physical or statistical interpretation that connects it to those gaps. The chapter covers, in order: the 16-arm ablation design; the Kermany dataset and preprocessing pipeline; the Foi Poisson-Gaussian noise simulation; the hybrid CNN-Transformer architecture and probabilistic bottleneck; the four-term composite loss function; the training protocol; the multi-metric evaluation framework; and the computational environment. Readers primarily interested in results may consult Table~\ref{tab:methods:loss_summary} (loss configurations for all 16 arms) as a reference map and proceed to Chapter~\ref{chap:results}.

\section{Study Design Overview}
\label{sec:methods:overview}


This study evaluates PP-VAE-Hformer through a controlled ablation experiment.\footnote{An \emph{ablation study} is a controlled experiment in which one component of a complex system is selectively removed or substituted while every other element remains fixed, isolating that component's causal contribution to measured performance. The term originates in neuroscience, where physical lesioning of brain regions established their functional role. In machine learning, ablation is the primary method for establishing which architectural choices and loss terms genuinely explain observed performance differences, rather than correlating with confounders such as parameter count or training compute \cite{meyes_ablation_2019}.} Sixteen arms were defined, each differing only in the loss function\footnote{The \emph{loss function} (also called the objective function or training criterion) is a scalar-valued function that the training algorithm minimises by adjusting the network's parameters. Its gradient at each training step defines the direction in which parameters move. The choice of loss function therefore determines not only what the network is optimised to produce, but also which network components receive a meaningful gradient signal: an MSE loss provides no gradient for an uncertainty head, while the heteroscedastic NLL (Section~\ref{sec:methods:loss:nll}) simultaneously trains both the reconstruction mean and the per-pixel variance \cite{kendall_what_2017}.} or a specific architectural choice (probabilistic vs.\ deterministic latent space, KL schedule, activation function), with all other aspects -- dataset, backbone, optimiser, epochs, evaluation -- held constant. This ensures observed performance differences are attributable to the specific component under test \cite{zhang_beyond_2017, liang_swinir_2021}.

\paragraph{Why 16 arms rather than exhaustive enumeration.}
The loss function space considered here includes five binary components: NLL (vs.\ MSE/L1), SSIM, FFL, edge supervision, and perceptual loss. Exhaustive enumeration of all $2^5 = 32$ binary combinations, further crossed with three KL schedules, two activation functions, and three pixel-norm variants, would yield over 500 distinct conditions -- each requiring 9--10 A100 GPU-hours, totalling more than 4,500 GPU-hours. This is beyond the available compute budget and would not produce interpretable conclusions (too many degrees of freedom for 624 test images to distinguish). The 16-arm design is therefore hypothesis-driven: each arm tests a specific, scientifically motivated question (does NLL improve calibration? does FFL recover high-frequency content? does cyclic KL prevent posterior collapse?) and is ordered cumulatively so that each arm adds exactly one component to the previous arm, isolating its causal contribution. This design follows Meyes et al.\ \cite{meyes_ablation_2019}: ablation is most informative when structured as a sequential, one-component-at-a-time comparison rather than a grid search.

The 16 arms fall into five groups, summarised in Table~\ref{tab:methods:loss_summary}. Arms~A--E form the core cumulative sequence (Table~\ref{tab:methods:arms}): Arm~A is the MSE baseline, each subsequent arm adds one component (NLL, SSIM, FFL, VAE bottleneck), and Arm~E is the initial PP-VAE-Hformer proposal. Arms~F--H extend the VAE comparison by testing three KL annealing schedules. Arms~I--K investigate L1 versus L2 loss and hybrid NLL+L1 objectives. Arms~L--N add structural regularisation terms (Sobel edge loss, VGG-16 perceptual loss) to the NLL framework. Arms~O--P ablate Parametric Rectified Linear Unit (PReLU) activations, with Arm~P combining the best-performing loss terms and VAE schedule.
\begin{table}[htbp]
  \centering
  \caption{Core five experimental arms (A--E): the primary cumulative ablation sequence. Arms~F--P extending this design are described in Table~\ref{tab:methods:loss_summary}.}
  \label{tab:methods:arms}
  \small
  \begin{tabular}{c p{3.8cm} c p{4.5cm}}
    \toprule
    \textbf{Arm} & \textbf{Loss components} & \textbf{VAE} & \textbf{Contribution tested} \\
    \midrule
    A & MSE only                                 & No  & Baseline (replicates DnCNN / Hformer) \\
    B & NLL                                      & No  & Heteroscedastic uncertainty loss \\
    C & NLL + SSIM                               & No  & Structural fidelity term \\
    D & NLL + SSIM + FFL                         & No  & Frequency-domain spectral penalty \\
    E & NLL + SSIM + FFL + KL (linear)           & Yes & Probabilistic bottleneck \& epistemic uncertainty \\
    \bottomrule
  \end{tabular}

  \vspace{0.3em}
  {\footnotesize \textbf{Note:} All other settings (architecture depth, optimiser, learning rate,
  dataset, training duration) are identical across all 16 arms. VAE = variational autoencoder bottleneck active.
  NLL = negative log-likelihood; SSIM = structural similarity; FFL = focal frequency loss;
  KL = Kullback--Leibler divergence.}
\end{table}
Four sequential phases were executed: (1) dataset preparation and noise simulation; (2) training across all 16 arms, 5 retrained KAIR baselines (DnCNN, IRCNN, FFDNet, DRUNet, SwinIR), and 2 extended SOTA baselines (NAFNet, SCUNet); (3) quantitative evaluation on the held-out test set; and (4) calibration analysis of uncertainty estimates from NLL-trained arms. All phases used PyTorch \cite{paszke_pytorch_2019} (\texttt{scripts/train\_proposed.py}).

\begin{figure}[htbp]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\linewidth]{Experimental design.pdf} % or .png, .eps
\caption[Experimental pipeline overview]{Overview of the experimental pipeline. Clean paediatric radiographs (Kermany dataset) 
  are corrupted by Poisson-Gaussian noise ($\eta \in \{50,100,200,300\}$) and processed 
  by PP-VAE-Hformer. The model is trained across 16 ablation arms sharing an
  encoder--decoder architecture but differing in loss functions, KL schedule, and activation function.
  Outputs include a denoised image $\hat{\mu}_y$ (all arms), an aleatoric
  uncertainty map $\log\hat{\sigma}^2_a$ (NLL-trained arms B--P, excluding I/J), and an epistemic uncertainty
  map $\hat{\sigma}^2_e$ via Monte Carlo sampling (VAE arms E, F, G, H, P)}
\label{fig:methods:pipeline}
\end{figure}

%============================================================
\section{Dataset}
\label{sec:methods:dataset}

\subsection{Kermany Paediatric Chest Radiograph Collection}
\label{sec:methods:dataset:kermany}

The dataset used in this study is the Kermany paediatric chest radiograph collection (described in Section~\ref{sec:lit:kermany}): 5,856 anterior-posterior chest radiographs from children aged one to five years, acquired under routine clinical conditions \cite{kermany_identifying_2018}. Table~\ref{tab:methods:dataset} summarises the final train/validation/test partition. The following describes the preprocessing applied in this study.

Images were resized to $256\times256$ via bilinear interpolation,\footnote{Bilinear interpolation computes each output pixel as the weighted average of the four nearest neighbours in the source image, with weights proportional to proximity in both the horizontal and vertical directions. It avoids the blocking artefacts of nearest-neighbour resampling and the ringing introduced by higher-order bicubic kernels, while preserving the overall intensity structure required for correct Poisson-Gaussian noise simulation at the target resolution.} converted to single-channel float tensors, and normalised to $[0,1]$. No augmentation was applied, as it would alter the intensity statistics required by the Poisson-Gaussian noise model. The 624-image official test partition was held out throughout training. Of the remaining 5,232 images, 785 (15\%, seed 42) formed the validation set and 4,447 the training set. Class labels were unused: the denoising task is self-supervised.

\paragraph{Prevention of data leakage.} \emph{Data leakage}\footnote{\emph{Data leakage} occurs when information from the evaluation set is used, directly or indirectly, to make decisions during training. If a model is allowed to see test-set images during training, or if hyperparameters are optimised by repeatedly checking test-set performance, the model's apparent performance on the test set will be artificially inflated and will not reflect how the model performs on genuinely unseen patients. Preventing leakage is therefore a fundamental requirement of trustworthy machine learning evaluation in medical settings.} is prevented at three levels. First, the 624-image official test partition was partitioned before any model code was written and was never accessed during training, hyperparameter selection, or architecture design. Its results are reported once at the end of each experiment. Second, the 785-image validation set was used exclusively for checkpoint selection (selecting the training epoch with the best validation PSNR); it played no role in loss function design, learning rate choice, or any other decision that could cause optimistic bias, and to prevent any confounding that would arise from hyperparameter tuning in the comparison of loss terms. Third, Poisson-Gaussian noise was generated on-the-fly at each training iteration using a freshly drawn random seed, so no model ever sees the same noisy image twice and no noisy realisation from the test or validation set can be memorised. The random seed for the train/validation split (seed 42) is fixed and reported for full reproducibility, allowing independent groups to reconstruct the identical partition.

\begin{table}[htbp]
\centering
\caption{Dataset partition summary. Class labels were available but not used during
training or evaluation; the denoising task requires only clean images as targets.}
\label{tab:methods:dataset}
\begin{tabular}{lrl}
\toprule
\textbf{Split} & \textbf{Images} & \textbf{Role} \\
\midrule
Training & 4,447 & Model parameter updates \\
Validation & 785 & Checkpoint selection (never trained on) \\
Test & 624 & Final evaluation only (never seen during training) \\
\midrule
\textbf{Total} & \textbf{5,856} & \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\subsection{Justification of Dataset Choice}
\label{sec:methods:dataset:justification}

The Kermany collection was selected because its images carry clear diagnostic class labels (Normal, Bacterial Pneumonia, Viral Pneumonia) that serve as ground truth for evaluating pathology preservation, and because it is one of the most widely used large-scale publicly available paediatric CXR datasets with real clinical acquisitions from children aged one to five -- precisely the population at highest radiosensitivity \cite{smith-bindman_hematologic_2025} and the domain where adult-trained models fail \cite{laborie_scoping_2026}. At $256\times256$, images retain diagnostically relevant anatomy while remaining computationally tractable. No additional ethical approval was required, as this study uses a pre-existing publicly available dataset with no new data collection.

% ============================================================
\section{Noise Simulation}
\label{sec:methods:noise}

\subsection{Physical Basis: the Poisson-Gaussian Noise Model}
\label{sec:methods:noise:model}

As established in Section~\ref{sec:intro:tradeoff}, radiographic noise comprises two physically distinct components: signal-dependent quantum (Poisson) noise and signal-independent detector read-out noise. The Foi et al.\ \cite{foi_practical_2008} model expresses the total noise variance at a pixel with expected intensity $y$ as:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:methods:foi}
\mathrm{Var}(z \mid y) = a \cdot y + b
\end{equation}
where $a \geq 0$ is the Poisson gain parameter and $b \geq 0$ the Gaussian read-out variance. This \emph{affine} variance relationship (see Section~\ref{sec:intro:tradeoff}) predicts that dark regions (pleural spaces, lung bases) are substantially noisier than bright regions (aerated parenchyma), confirmed with $R^2 > 0.99$ against real CCD sensor data \cite{foi_practical_2008}.

\subsection{Implementation}
\label{sec:methods:noise:implementation}

Noise was generated on-the-fly: a fresh independent sample was drawn for every image in every batch, so the model sees a different noisy realisation of each clean image on every training epoch and generalises across the entire noise distribution rather than memorising a fixed noisy dataset. This approach also means training pairs are effectively infinite in number -- a substantial regularisation benefit for a model with over 1.65 million parameters trained on 4,447 clean images.

The noise was generated using a Gaussian approximation to the Poisson term, following
the same approach described by Foi et al.\ \cite{foi_practical_2008}:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:methods:noise_impl}
\tilde{x} = x + \varepsilon, \qquad
\varepsilon \sim \mathcal{N}\!\left(0,\; a \cdot \max(x, 0) + b\right)
\end{equation}
where $\tilde{x}$ is the simulated noisy image, $x \in [0,1]$ is the clean image, and
$\varepsilon$ is pixel-wise independent Gaussian noise with variance equal to the affine
function of local intensity. The $\max(x, 0)$ clamp prevents negative variance at very dark
pixels. This approximation is valid when the expected photon count per pixel is substantially
greater than one, a condition satisfied across the noise levels and intensity ranges used in
this study \cite{foi_practical_2008}.

\subsection{Noise Level Presets}
\label{sec:methods:noise:presets}

Three calibrated presets span the clinical dose range (Table~\ref{tab:methods:noise_presets}). During training, the preset was sampled uniformly per batch; evaluation used each preset separately.
\begin{table}[htbp]
\centering
\caption{Foi noise presets used in the experiments.}
\label{tab:methods:noise_presets}
\begin{threeparttable}
\begin{tabular}{lcccc}
\toprule
\textbf{Preset} & \textbf{$a$} & \textbf{$b$} & \textbf{Eff.\ $\sigma$ at $\bar{y}=0.5$} & \textbf{Approximate clinical context} \\
\midrule
Low   & 0.01 & 0.002 & $\approx 0.074$ ($\sigma \approx 15$) & Near-standard dose \\
Mid   & 0.03 & 0.005 & $\approx 0.122$ ($\sigma \approx 25$) & Reduced dose \\
High  & 0.08 & 0.010 & $\approx 0.204$ ($\sigma \approx 50$) & Ultra-low-dose \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\begin{tablenotes}
\footnotesize
\item Effective $\sigma$ is computed as $\sqrt{a \cdot 0.5 + b}$, evaluated at mean image intensity $\bar{y} = 0.5$, providing a single-number summary comparable to the additive Gaussian noise levels used in natural-image denoising benchmarks (e.g.\ $\sigma = 15$, 25, 50 in BSD68).
\end{tablenotes}
\end{threeparttable}
\end{table}

Unlike additive Gaussian benchmarks (fixed $\sigma$ per image), the Foi model produces spatially heterogeneous noise: the per-pixel variance $ay + b$ varies continuously across the image according to local intensity.\footnote{Spatially heterogeneous noise means the noise level is not the same everywhere in the image. In a Poisson-Gaussian radiograph, the lung parenchyma (high $y$) carries strong Poisson noise with variance $\approx ay$, while bone cortex (low $y$) is dominated by the Gaussian floor $b$. A denoiser trained with a spatially uniform loss function (MSE, L1) implicitly assumes constant noise variance and therefore systematically under-regularises high-noise regions and over-smoothes low-noise regions. Only the heteroscedastic NLL loss (Equation~\ref{eq:methods:nll}) correctly accounts for this per-pixel variance by auto-weighting pixels inversely proportional to their predicted uncertainty.} This spatial heterogeneity is precisely what the heteroscedastic loss (Arms B--E) is designed to model, and what makes the per-pixel uncertainty map clinically interpretable as a data-reliability signal.

% ============================================================
\section{Model Architecture}
\label{sec:methods:architecture}

\subsection{Overview}
\label{sec:methods:architecture:overview}

The proposed model, PP-VAE-Hformer, is a U-Net\footnote{The U-Net \cite{ronneberger_u-net_2015} is an encoder-decoder architecture distinguished by \emph{skip connections} that concatenate feature maps from each encoder stage directly to the corresponding decoder stage. The encoder progressively downsamples spatial resolution while increasing channel depth to capture semantic context; the decoder upsamples back to the original resolution. Skip connections allow the decoder to combine high-resolution local detail (from the encoder) with the high-level abstract representation (from the bottleneck), which is critical for reconstruction tasks where fine spatial detail must be preserved. Virtually all modern medical image segmentation and restoration architectures are U-Net variants.} encoder-decoder whose stages are built from HybridBlocks, a design
adapted from the Hformer architecture for CT denoising \cite{zhang_hformer_2023}. At
the bottom of the U-Net, an optional probabilistic bottleneck based on a Variational
Autoencoder (VAE) \cite{kingma_auto-encoding_2014} introduces controlled stochasticity,
enabling epistemic uncertainty estimation via repeated stochastic forward passes. A dual
output head at the final decoder stage produces two outputs simultaneously: a denoised
image mean and a per-pixel aleatoric uncertainty map.

\input{fig_architecture_overview}




\subsection{Stem and Encoder}
\label{sec:methods:architecture:encoder}

The input is a single-channel greyscale image of size $1 \times 256 \times 256$ pixels. A
stem convolution ($3 \times 3$ filter, padding 1) maps the single input channel to 64 feature
channels, producing a $64 \times 256 \times 256$ feature map. This initial projection step is
a standard design choice that transforms raw pixel intensity into a richer feature
representation before the main encoder begins \cite{zhang_hformer_2023}.

The encoder consists of three downsampling stages. At each stage, a sequence of
HybridBlocks processes the feature map at the current spatial resolution, and then a
strided convolution halves the spatial resolution and doubles the number of channels. After
three downsampling stages the spatial resolution is $32 \times 32$ pixels and the channel
count is 512. The feature map at the input to each downsampling operation is saved as a
skip connection to be used in the corresponding decoder stage. The channel and spatial
progression is summarised in Table~\ref{tab:methods:channels}.

\begin{table}[htbp]
\centering
\caption{Spatial resolution and channel count at each stage of PP-VAE-Hformer.}
\label{tab:methods:channels}
\begin{tabular}{lccc}
\toprule
\textbf{Stage} & \textbf{Operation} & \textbf{Spatial size} & \textbf{Channels} \\
\midrule
Input      & Raw noisy image                & $256 \times 256$ & 1    \\
Stem       & Conv $3\times3$, stride 1     & $256 \times 256$ & 64   \\
Encoder 1  & 2 HybridBlocks + Downsample   & $128 \times 128$ & 128  \\
Encoder 2  & 2 HybridBlocks + Downsample   & $64 \times 64$   & 256  \\
Encoder 3  & 2 HybridBlocks + Downsample   & $32 \times 32$   & 512  \\
Bottleneck & 2 HybridBlocks + (VAE)        & $32 \times 32$   & 512  \\
Decoder 3  & Upsample + skip + 2 HybridBlocks & $64 \times 64$ & 256  \\
Decoder 2  & Upsample + skip + 2 HybridBlocks & $128 \times 128$ & 128 \\
Decoder 1  & Upsample + skip + 2 HybridBlocks & $256 \times 256$ & 64   \\
Output     & Dual Conv $3\times3$, stride 1 & $256 \times 256$ & 1    \\
\bottomrule
\multicolumn{4}{l}{\footnotesize{The input image is compressed by a factor of $8\times$ spatially (256 to 32 pixels) and expanded}} \\
\multicolumn{4}{l}{\footnotesize{$512\times$ in channel depth before the bottleneck. The decoder mirrors this progression.}} \\
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

The HybridBlock is the core processing unit of PP-VAE-Hformer. It applies two operations
in sequence: a convolutional ResBlock followed by a windowed self-attention block
(WindowAttnBlock). This pairing combines the complementary strengths of convolutional
and attention-based processing \cite{zhang_hformer_2023}: the ResBlock extracts precise
local texture features, while the WindowAttnBlock captures longer-range contextual
relationships that help the network understand what a region should look like given its
surroundings.

\paragraph{ResBlock.}
The ResBlock applies two $3 \times 3$ convolutions with GroupNorm\footnote{Group Normalisation \cite{wu_group_2018} partitions the feature channels into groups and normalises the mean and variance of activations within each group independently. Unlike Batch Normalisation \cite{ioffe_batch_2015}, which normalises across the batch dimension and is statistically unstable at small batch sizes, GroupNorm is batch-size independent. This property is important for medical imaging workloads, where memory constraints often limit effective batch sizes to 4--16 images, and where per-image intensity calibration is clinically meaningful.} and GELU\footnote{The Gaussian Error Linear Unit (GELU) \cite{hendrycks_gaussian_2016} is defined as $\mathrm{GELU}(x) = x \cdot \Phi(x)$, where $\Phi$ denotes the standard normal CDF. Unlike Rectified Linear Unit (ReLU), which hard-gates all negative inputs to zero, GELU applies a smooth probabilistic gate: inputs near zero are passed through with a weight that varies continuously. This produces smoother gradient landscapes than ReLU and has become the default activation in Transformer-based architectures including BERT \cite{devlin_bert_2019} and GPT. Its relevance here is that the smooth gradient landscape prevents the type of activation-function-induced gradient overflow that causes Arm~O's PReLU failure (Section~\ref{sec:results:error}).} activation, with a residual skip connection \cite{he_deep_2016}.

\input{fig_architecture_resblock}

\paragraph{WindowAttnBlock.}
The WindowAttnBlock applies multi-head self-attention within non-overlapping $8\times8$ local windows \cite{liu_swin_2021, zhang_hformer_2023}. Understanding the computation requires first understanding what attention does.

\textit{Standard self-attention.} Attention \cite{vaswani_attention_2017} transforms an input sequence of feature vectors into a context-aware output sequence. Each input position $i$ is represented by three derived vectors: a \emph{query} $\mathbf{Q}_i$, a \emph{key} $\mathbf{K}_i$, and a \emph{value} $\mathbf{V}_i$, each obtained by a learned linear projection of the input feature vector at that position. The output at position $i$ is a weighted sum of all value vectors, where the weight given to position $j$'s value depends on how similar position $i$'s query is to position $j$'s key:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:methods:attention}
\mathrm{Attention}(\mathbf{Q},\mathbf{K},\mathbf{V}) = \mathrm{softmax}\!\left(\frac{\mathbf{Q}\mathbf{K}^\top}{\sqrt{d_k}}\right)\mathbf{V}
\end{equation}
where $\mathbf{Q}$, $\mathbf{K}$, $\mathbf{V}$ are matrices stacking the query, key, and value vectors of all positions, and $d_k$ is the dimension of each key vector (the $\sqrt{d_k}$ scaling stabilises gradients for large $d_k$). The \emph{softmax} function converts raw similarity scores into non-negative weights that sum to 1 across all positions.\footnote{The \emph{softmax} function maps a vector of real numbers $\mathbf{s} = (s_1, \ldots, s_n)$ to a probability distribution: $\mathrm{softmax}(s_i) = e^{s_i} / \sum_j e^{s_j}$. Larger similarities receive exponentially more weight; this means attention is dominated by the most similar positions rather than being a simple average.} In \emph{multi-head} attention \cite{vaswani_attention_2017}, this computation is performed $h$ times in parallel with different projection matrices, and the $h$ outputs are concatenated and re-projected, allowing the model to simultaneously attend to different aspects of context (e.g.\ one head might attend to sharp edges, another to smooth regions).

\textit{Key computational trick: windowed vs.\ global attention.} Applied to a $32\times32$ feature map with $d_k = 64$, global attention requires computing the $\mathbf{Q}\mathbf{K}^\top$ product for all $(32\times32)^2 = 1{,}048{,}576$ pairs, computationally prohibitive at scale. Hformer (following the Swin Transformer \cite{liu_swin_2021}) restricts each query to attend only within a non-overlapping $8\times8$ local window. Within each window, only $(8\times8)^2 = 4{,}096$ attention pairs are computed. The $32\times32$ feature map contains $(32/8)^2 = 16$ such windows, for a total of $16 \times 4{,}096 = 65{,}536$ pairs, a $16\times$ reduction in computation at the bottleneck stage. Crucially, each feature vector is a high-dimensional channel-space representation extracted by prior convolutions, not a raw pixel value: attention is therefore comparing \emph{learned feature descriptors} of local regions, not pixel intensities directly. This gives windowed attention the ability to match anatomical texture across spatial neighbours based on what the convolutions have learned to detect, at a fraction of global attention's cost.

The block uses pre-layer normalisation\footnote{Placing the layer normalisation \emph{before} each sub-layer (attention, feedforward) rather than after it, as in the original Transformer, is referred to as \emph{pre-norm}. Pre-norm prevents gradient vanishing in deep stacks by ensuring the normalisation operates on the residual stream before the projection, and has become standard in modern deep learning \cite{xiong_layer_2020}.} and a two-layer feedforward network with GELU activation. Two HybridBlocks are stacked at each encoder and decoder stage.

\input{fig_architecture_hybridblock}

\subsection{Hyperparameter Specification and Justification}
\label{sec:methods:architecture:hyperparams}

Table~\ref{tab:methods:hyperparams} lists all architectural hyperparameters. The following explains the rationale for each key choice.

\begin{table}[htbp]
\centering
\caption{Architectural hyperparameters of PP-VAE-Hformer with justification.}
\label{tab:methods:hyperparams}
\small
\begin{tabular}{lll}
\toprule
\textbf{Hyperparameter} & \textbf{Value} & \textbf{Rationale} \\
\midrule
Base feature channels & 64 & Standard for 256$\times$256 image restoration (\cite{zhang_hformer_2023, liang_swinir_2021}) \\
Encoder channel progression & 64$\to$128$\to$256$\to$512 & Doubles per stage; matches U-Net convention \\
Number of encoder/decoder stages & 3 & 8$\times$ spatial compression; more stages would \\
 & & reduce the bottleneck below $32\times32$ \\
HybridBlocks per stage & 2 & Pilot: 1 block undertrained; 3 exceeded Graphics Processing Unit (GPU) memory \\
ResBlock kernel size & $3\times3$ & Standard for image restoration; larger kernels \\
 & & gave no improvement at extra compute cost \\
ResBlock stride / padding & 1 / 1 & Preserves spatial resolution within block \\
Downsampling conv & $3\times3$, stride 2 & Strided conv preferred over pooling \\
 & & (preserves learned feature information) \\
Upsampling (decoder) & Transposed conv $2\times2$, stride 2 & Learned upsampling preferred over bilinear \\
 & & (avoids fixed interpolation artefacts) \\
Attention window size & $8\times8$ & Following Swin Transformer \cite{liu_swin_2021}; \\
 & & smaller windows reduce context, larger exceed GPU cache \\
Attention heads & 8 & Standard for $d_k=64$ models; each head attends \\
 & & with $d_k/h = 8$-dimensional key vectors \\
VAE projection kernel & $1\times1$ & Bottleneck projection; no spatial mixing needed \\
KL log-variance clamp & $[-10,\,4]$ & Prevents variance overflow at initialisation \\
Monte Carlo (MC) sampling passes ($K$) & 20 & Pilot: $K{=}100$ changed epistemic maps by $<1\%$; \\
 & & 20 balances stability and inference cost \\
Output head kernels & $3\times3$, stride 1 & Two parallel heads; same architecture as stem \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\textbf{Why three downsampling stages?} The 8$\times$ spatial compression (256 to 32 pixels) was chosen so the bottleneck feature map is large enough to retain spatial structure (a 16$\times$16 or 8$\times$8 bottleneck discards too much spatial detail for reconstruction at 256$\times$256), while being small enough that global context can be captured within the $8\times8$ attention windows covering the entire 32$\times$32 map in 16 windows.

\textbf{Why 2 HybridBlocks per stage?} Pilot experiments with 1 block per stage produced underfitting (validation PSNR 0.4~dB below 2-block configuration); 3 blocks exceeded the SLURM A100 memory limit for batch size 16. Two blocks also exactly matches the Hformer paper's default configuration \cite{zhang_hformer_2023}, enabling direct architectural comparison.

\textbf{Why $3\times3$ convolutions throughout?} The $3\times3$ kernel has become standard in image restoration architectures because it captures the minimal local neighbourhood required for edge detection and texture characterisation, while larger kernels ($5\times5$, $7\times7$) add parameters and computation without consistent PSNR benefit at this image scale \cite{zhang_beyond_2017, zhang_drunet_2021}.

\subsection{Bottleneck and VAE Probabilistic Bottleneck}
\label{sec:methods:architecture:bottleneck}

After the three encoder stages, the feature map is at its smallest spatial footprint
($32 \times 32$ pixels, 512 channels). Two further HybridBlocks process the bottleneck
features before the optional VAE layer is applied.

For Arms A through D, the bottleneck is deterministic: the feature
map passes through the bottleneck HybridBlocks and directly into the decoder.

For Arm E, a VAE Bottleneck module is applied after the bottleneck
HybridBlocks. Unlike the original VAE \cite{kingma_auto-encoding_2014} which maps the
entire image to a single global latent vector, this VAE operates spatially on the full
$512 \times 32 \times 32$ feature map. Three $1 \times 1$ convolutions serve as projection
layers: one produces the posterior mean $\mathbf{z}_\mu$, one produces the log-variance
$\mathbf{z}_{\log\sigma^2}$, and one projects the drawn sample back into the feature space.
The reparameterisation trick \cite{kingma_auto-encoding_2014} draws a differentiable sample
from the learned posterior:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:methods:reparam}
\mathbf{z} = \mathbf{z}_\mu + \boldsymbol{\varepsilon} \odot \exp\!\left(\tfrac{1}{2}\,\mathbf{z}_{\log\sigma^2}\right),
\qquad \boldsymbol{\varepsilon} \sim \mathcal{N}(\mathbf{0}, \mathbf{I})
\end{equation}
where $\odot$ denotes element-wise multiplication. The log-variance is clamped to
$[-10, 4]$ to prevent numerical overflow during early training when the variance estimates
may be poorly initialised. During evaluation, $\mathbf{z} = \mathbf{z}_\mu$ is used without
sampling (the deterministic forward pass).

\subsection{Decoder and Dual Output Head}
\label{sec:methods:architecture:decoder}

The decoder mirrors the encoder. At each of the three upsampling stages, a transposed
convolution doubles the spatial resolution. The resulting feature map is then concatenated
with the skip connection from the corresponding encoder stage along the channel dimension,
and a $1 \times 1$ convolution fuses the concatenated channels back to the target channel
count. Two HybridBlocks then refine the fused representation.

At the top of the decoder, the feature map is back at $256 \times 256$ pixels with 64
channels. Two parallel $3 \times 3$ convolutional output heads are applied:

\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Reconstruction head} ($\hat{\mu}_y$): outputs a single-channel image
  representing the denoised estimate of the clean radiograph. This is the primary output
  used by all 16 arms.
\item \textbf{Aleatoric head} ($\log\hat{\sigma}^2_a$): outputs a single-channel map of the
  log aleatoric variance at each pixel, representing the irreducible noise that cannot be
  recovered regardless of model capacity. This head is supervised only in Arms B through E
  (where the NLL loss provides a gradient signal).
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Epistemic Uncertainty via Monte Carlo Sampling}
\label{sec:methods:architecture:epistemic}

For Arm E, epistemic uncertainty $\hat{\sigma}^2_e$ is computed at evaluation time using Monte Carlo sampling
through the VAE bottleneck. The model is put into training mode to activate stochastic
sampling, and $K = 20$ independent forward passes are run for the same input image.
Each pass draws a different random sample from the posterior
$\mathcal{N}(\mathbf{z}_\mu,\,\exp(\mathbf{z}_{\log\sigma^2}))$, producing a slightly
different reconstruction. The pixel-wise variance across the 20 reconstructions is taken as
the epistemic uncertainty map:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:methods:epistemic}
\hat{\sigma}^2_e(i) = \frac{1}{K}\sum_{k=1}^{K}\!\left(\hat{\mu}^{(k)}_y(i) - \bar{\mu}_y(i)\right)^2,
\qquad \bar{\mu}_y(i) = \frac{1}{K}\sum_{k=1}^{K}\hat{\mu}^{(k)}_y(i)
\end{equation}
A high value of $\hat{\sigma}^2_e$ at a pixel means the 20 reconstructions disagree substantially with one another there: when different noise samples from the posterior produce different outputs, the network cannot commit to a single answer based on the input data alone, and the reconstruction at that pixel is partly determined by what the model learned from training images rather than by what was actually measured in the current patient.\footnote{This interpretation is the operational form of the signal retrieval versus prior sampling framework (Section~\ref{sec:lit:uncertainty:retrieval}). High $\hat{\sigma}^2_e$ identifies precisely the prior-sampling regime: pixels where the 20 reconstructions diverge are pixels where the decoder is extrapolating from the learned prior rather than anchoring to the observed data. Low $\hat{\sigma}^2_e$ with high $\hat{\sigma}^2_a$ indicates a different situation: the noise is high (aleatoric) but the model consistently agrees on the reconstruction -- meaning the model is confident even though the data is noisy. The two maps are therefore complementary, not redundant.} Clinically, this is the
signal identified in Section~\ref{sec:lit:uncertainty:retrieval}: pixels where
$\hat{\sigma}^2_e$ is large are candidates for targeted lung ultrasound review, because
the denoised image value at those locations is not reliably supported by the observed
radiograph \cite{lovrenski_esr_2025}.

% ============================================================
\section{Loss Functions}
\label{sec:methods:loss}

\paragraph{How a loss function is computed, conceptual overview.}
A loss function measures, numerically, \emph{how wrong} the model's output is at each training step, so that the training algorithm knows in which direction to nudge the model's parameters. Figure~\ref{fig:methods:loss_diagram} illustrates the general principle: the model receives a noisy input image and produces a predicted clean image; the loss function then compares this prediction to the true clean reference image (which is available during training because noise was added computationally) and computes a single scalar error value. The training algorithm adjusts all of the model's many millions of numerical parameters by tiny amounts in the direction that would reduce this error value. After tens of thousands of such update steps, the model learns to produce predictions that are systematically close to the true clean image.

The specific mathematical form of the loss function defines what ``close'' means: MSE punishes large pixel-value deviations; SSIM additionally punishes loss of spatial structure; NLL learns to predict not just the clean image value but also how uncertain it is about that value at each pixel.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}[
  box/.style={draw, rounded corners=4pt, minimum width=2.8cm, minimum height=1.1cm,
              align=center, fill=white, font=\small},
  sbox/.style={draw, rounded corners=4pt, minimum width=2.0cm, minimum height=1.1cm,
              align=center, fill=blue!6, font=\small},
  lbox/.style={draw, rounded corners=4pt, minimum width=2.4cm, minimum height=1.1cm,
              align=center, fill=orange!10, font=\small},
  arr/.style={->, thick, >=Stealth},
  >=Stealth
]

\node[box] (noisy) at (0,0) {Noisy input\\$\tilde{x}$};
\node[sbox] (model) at (3.5,0) {Model\\(millions of\\parameters)};
\node[box] (pred) at (7.0,0) {Predicted\\clean image\\$\hat{\mu}_y$};
\node[box] (ref) at (7.0,-2.5) {True clean\\reference\\$y$};
\node[lbox] (loss) at (11.0,-1.2) {Loss value\\$\mathcal{L}(\hat{\mu}_y, y)$\\(single number)};
\node[box] (update) at (7.0,-4.5) {Gradient update\\(adjust all\\parameters)};

\draw[arr] (noisy) -- (model);
\draw[arr] (model) -- (pred);
\draw[arr] (pred) -- (loss);
\draw[arr] (ref) -- (loss);
\draw[arr] (loss) -- node[right, font=\footnotesize] {backpropagation} (update);
\draw[arr, dashed, gray] (update) to[out=180, in=270] node[below left, font=\footnotesize, gray] {next step} (model.south);

\node[font=\footnotesize, text=gray] at (9.3, 0.3) {compare};
\draw[arr, gray, dashed] (pred.east) to[out=0, in=90] (loss.north);
\draw[arr, gray, dashed] (ref.east) to[out=0, in=270] (loss.south);

\end{tikzpicture}
\caption[Loss computation diagram]{How a loss function is used in training. The model maps a noisy input to a predicted clean image; the loss function compares this to the true clean reference (available because noise was added computationally) and produces a scalar error value. Backpropagation\protect\footnotemark\ uses this error to adjust the model's parameters. The specific mathematical definition of the loss determines what aspects of image quality are penalised.}
\label{fig:methods:loss_diagram}
\end{figure}
\footnotetext{\emph{Backpropagation} (short for backward propagation of errors) is the algorithm that computes, for every parameter in the network, the partial derivative of the loss with respect to that parameter. This derivative tells us in which direction each parameter should be adjusted to reduce the loss. The chain rule of calculus allows this computation to proceed layer by layer from the output back to the input. Backpropagation makes training deep networks with millions of parameters computationally feasible \cite{lecun_efficient_2002}.}

\subsection{Arm A: Mean Squared Error}
\label{sec:methods:loss:mse}

Arm A uses the standard mean squared error (MSE) loss, which minimises the average
squared difference between the reconstructed image $\hat{\mu}_y$ and the clean target $y$:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:methods:mse}
\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{MSE}} = \frac{1}{HW}\sum_{i=1}^{HW}\left(\hat{\mu}_y(i) - y(i)\right)^2
\end{equation}
This is the same loss used by DnCNN \cite{zhang_beyond_2017} and Hformer
\cite{zhang_hformer_2023}. Arm A therefore benchmarks those state-of-the-art denoisers
under the same training conditions as PP-VAE-Hformer. Because MSE treats every pixel as
equally uncertain, it provides no training signal for the aleatoric head $\log\hat{\sigma}^2_a$.

\subsection{Arms I, J, and Q: L1 and Charbonnier Pixel-Norm Variants}
\label{sec:methods:loss:l1charbonnier}

Arms~I, J, and Q extend the pixel-norm family by replacing the squared penalty of MSE with alternative distance measures. These three loss functions are compared with MSE below; readers primarily interested in the headline comparison may refer directly to Section~\ref{sec:results:curriculum:charb} for the quantitative outcome.

\paragraph{Why compare pixel norms?}
A pixel-norm loss trains the network to produce a denoised image whose individual pixel values are numerically close to the clean reference. The mathematical form of ``close'' is what differs between L2, L1, and Charbonnier. The choice determines two things: (i) what statistical summary of all plausible clean images the network learns to output, and (ii) how harshly large individual pixel errors are treated during training. For readers without a machine learning background: the loss function is like a scoring rule for an exam -- different scoring rules incentivise different answer strategies, even when the correct answer is the same.

\paragraph{L1 / MAE -- Arm I.}
The L1 (Mean Absolute Error) loss penalises errors \emph{in proportion to their size}: an error of $2$ contributes twice as much as an error of $1$.
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:methods:mae}
\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{L1}} = \frac{1}{HW}\sum_{i=1}^{HW}\left|\hat{\mu}_y(i) - y(i)\right|
\end{equation}
The network minimising L1 is driven toward the pixel-wise \emph{median} of all plausible clean images rather than the mean. Under noise distributions that are approximately symmetric -- as Poisson-Gaussian noise is at moderate photon counts -- the median and mean coincide, so L1 and L2 produce near-identical reconstructions in practice \cite{zhao_loss_2017}. \textbf{Advantages over MSE}: (i) slightly more robust to a small number of extreme pixel outliers; (ii) in some imaging regimes produces marginally sharper textures because outlier pixels do not dominate the gradient. \textbf{Disadvantages}: the absolute-value function has a non-differentiable kink at zero error, requiring a subgradient approximation; in practice this causes no instability at standard learning rates but must be handled carefully for very small learning rates late in training.

\paragraph{L1+SSIM+FFL composite -- Arm J.}
Arm J combines L1 with the SSIM and FFL structural terms defined in Sections~\ref{sec:methods:loss:ssim} and~\ref{sec:methods:loss:ffl}, replacing the NLL-headed composite of Arm~D with an L1-headed one. This tests whether the perceptual and frequency-domain benefits of SSIM+FFL are preserved when the base pixel norm is L1 rather than NLL. Because L1 does not supervise the aleatoric variance head, Arm~J is a fully deterministic arm with no uncertainty output.

\paragraph{Charbonnier -- Arm Q.}
The Charbonnier penalty \cite{charbonnier_two_1994} is a smooth approximation to L1:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:methods:charbonnier}
\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{Charb}} = \frac{1}{HW}\sum_{i=1}^{HW}\!\left(\sqrt{\left(\hat{\mu}_y(i)-y(i)\right)^2+\varepsilon^2}-\varepsilon\right), \quad \varepsilon = 10^{-3}
\end{equation}
For large errors ($|\hat{\mu}_y - y| \gg \varepsilon$), the Charbonnier loss behaves like L1: proportional penalty, robust to outliers. For small errors ($|\hat{\mu}_y - y| \ll \varepsilon$), it approximates L2: quadratic penalty, smooth gradient, stable optimisation. The result is a loss that inherits the edge-robustness of L1 and the differentiability of L2, without the kink at zero that makes pure L1 technically inconvenient. SwinIR \cite{liang_swinir_2021} adopted Charbonnier as its default training objective. \textbf{Advantages}: differentiable everywhere; smooth, well-conditioned training gradients; preferred in architectures with variance heads where gradient discontinuities could couple poorly with other loss terms. \textbf{Disadvantages}: introduces the hyperparameter $\varepsilon$ (fixed at $10^{-3}$ throughout this study, following \citeauthor{charbonnier_two_1994}); under approximately Gaussian noise, performance differences from L1 and L2 are negligible.

\paragraph{Expected equivalence under this noise model.}
Zhao et al.\ \cite{zhao_loss_2017} showed that composite objectives (SSIM+L1) outperform pure MSE by 0.5--1.0\,dB on natural images. However, the relative ranking of L2, L1, and Charbonnier alone is expected to be statistically indistinguishable for Poisson-Gaussian noise with approximately Gaussian pixel marginals, because the three estimators converge to the same posterior mean/median under this distribution. The ablation results in Section~\ref{sec:results:curriculum:charb} confirm this prediction empirically for a 200-epoch training loop.

\subsection{Arm B: Heteroscedastic Negative Log-Likelihood}
\label{sec:methods:loss:nll}

Arm B replaces MSE with a heteroscedastic negative log-likelihood (NLL) loss, following
Kendall and Gal \cite{kendall_what_2017} (see Section~\ref{sec:lit:losses:nll} for the
theoretical motivation). The loss simultaneously trains the network to predict the clean pixel
mean and a per-pixel noise variance. For predicted mean $\hat{\mu}_y(i)$ and
log-variance $\hat{s}(i) \equiv \log\hat{\sigma}^2_a(i)$:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:methods:nll}
\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{NLL}} = \frac{1}{HW}\sum_{i=1}^{HW}
  \left[\frac{\left(y(i) - \hat{\mu}_y(i)\right)^2}{2\exp\!\left(\hat{s}(i)\right)}
  + \frac{\hat{s}(i)}{2}\right]
\end{equation}
The two-term structure (reconstruction error scaled by predicted variance, balanced by a
variance regulariser to prevent degenerate solutions) is analysed in
Section~\ref{sec:lit:losses:nll}. In the Foi model context: dark regions where $ay+b$ is
large should elicit a correspondingly large predicted $\hat{\sigma}^2_a$.

\subsection{Arm C: Multi-Scale Structural Similarity}
\label{sec:methods:loss:ssim}

Arm C adds a Multi-Scale Structural Similarity (MS-SSIM) penalty to the NLL loss. At each
spatial scale, SSIM \cite{wang_image_2004} compares local patches of the prediction
$\hat{\mu}_y$ and clean reference $y$ along three perceptual axes -- luminance (mean
intensity), contrast (local variance), and structure (cross-correlation) -- via:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:methods:ssim_loss}
\mathrm{SSIM}(\mathbf{p},\,\mathbf{q})
  = \frac{(2\mu_{\mathbf{p}}\,\mu_{\mathbf{q}} + C_1)\,(2\sigma_{\mathbf{p}\mathbf{q}} + C_2)}
         {(\mu_{\mathbf{p}}^2 + \mu_{\mathbf{q}}^2 + C_1)\,(\sigma_{\mathbf{p}}^2 + \sigma_{\mathbf{q}}^2 + C_2)}
\end{equation}
where $\mu_{\mathbf{p}}$, $\mu_{\mathbf{q}}$ are the mean intensities of local patches
$\mathbf{p}$ (from $\hat{\mu}_y$) and $\mathbf{q}$ (from $y$); $\sigma_{\mathbf{p}}^2$,
$\sigma_{\mathbf{q}}^2$ are the patch variances; $\sigma_{\mathbf{p}\mathbf{q}}$ is the
patch cross-covariance; and $C_1 = (0.01)^2$, $C_2 = (0.03)^2$ are numerical stability
constants for $[0,1]$-normalised images \cite{wang_image_2004}. The value lies in $[0,1]$,
with 1 indicating a perceptually perfect match.

MS-SSIM \cite{wang_multiscale_2003} extends this across $M = 5$ spatial scales by
progressively downsampling the image pair by a factor of 2 before each comparison and
combining the results:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:methods:ssim}
\mathrm{MS\text{-}SSIM}\!\left(\hat{\mu}_y,\,y\right)
  = \prod_{j=1}^{5}\mathrm{SSIM}_j\!\left(\hat{\mu}_y,\,y\right)^{w_j}
\end{equation}
where $\mathrm{SSIM}_j$ is the mean SSIM score (Equation~\ref{eq:methods:ssim_loss})
computed after $j-1$ downsampling steps; $w_j$ are the empirically determined scale
weights $[0.0448,\,0.2856,\,0.3001,\,0.2363,\,0.1333]$ from Wang et al.\
\cite{wang_multiscale_2003}, with higher weight given to mid-range scales; and $j = 1$
is the original resolution. The loss term is $\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{SSIM}} = 1 -
\mathrm{MS\text{-}SSIM}(\hat{\mu}_y, y)$, weighted by $\lambda_{\mathrm{SSIM}} = 0.5$
in the composite. Zhao et al.\ \cite{zhao_loss_2017} showed on natural images that
switching from MSE to an MS-SSIM-based loss improved perceptual quality by 0.5--1.0~dB
PSNR. The SSIM term provides structural supervision that ensures fine anatomical
detail -- rib margins, vessel outlines, the pleural line -- is preserved in the
reconstruction beyond what the pixel-level NLL alone enforces.

\subsection{Arm D: Focal Frequency Loss}
\label{sec:methods:loss:ffl}

Arm D adds the Focal Frequency Loss (FFL) \cite{jiang_focal_2021}. As discussed in
Section~\ref{sec:lit:losses:ffl}, spectral bias \cite{rahaman_spectral_2019} causes
pixel-domain-trained networks to underfit high-frequency components such as fine edges, vessel
margins, and consolidation boundaries, which are precisely the structures most clinically significant
in paediatric CXR. FFL addresses this by computing reconstruction error in the frequency
domain via the two-dimensional discrete Fourier transform, with adaptive weights that
concentrate gradient pressure on the frequency components the model currently reconstructs
most poorly:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:methods:ffl}
\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{FFL}}
  = \frac{1}{HW}\sum_{u=0}^{H-1}\sum_{v=0}^{W-1}
    w(u,v)\cdot\bigl|\mathcal{F}(\hat{\mu}_y)[u,v] - \mathcal{F}(y)[u,v]\bigr|^2
\end{equation}
where $\mathcal{F}(\cdot)[u,v]$ denotes the complex coefficient at frequency index
$(u,v)$ of the two-dimensional Discrete Fourier Transform (2D-DFT) of an image;
$\hat{\mu}_y$ is the predicted reconstruction and $y$ is the clean reference; $H$ and
$W$ are the spatial height and width of the image ($256$ and $256$ in this study);
$(u,v)$ are the discrete frequency coordinates spanning the full spectrum from DC
($(0,0)$, corresponding to the image mean) to the Nyquist frequencies; the magnitude
$|\mathcal{F}(\hat{\mu}_y)[u,v] - \mathcal{F}(y)[u,v]|$ is the spectral error amplitude
at frequency $(u,v)$, measuring how far apart the real and imaginary parts of the two
spectra are at that frequency; and $w(u,v)$ is the adaptive spectral weight, computed
from the current reconstruction error spectrum at each training step:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:methods:ffl_weight}
w(u,v) = \bigl|\mathcal{F}(\hat{\mu}_y)[u,v] - \mathcal{F}(y)[u,v]\bigr|^{\gamma},
\quad \gamma \geq 0
\end{equation}
where $\gamma$ is a decay hyperparameter (fixed at $\gamma = 1$ throughout this study,
following \citeauthor{jiang_focal_2021} \cite{jiang_focal_2021}). This self-guided
weighting scheme means that frequency components the model currently reconstructs poorly
-- those with large spectral error -- automatically receive a larger gradient signal at
the next update step. Because spectral bias causes early training to prioritise low-frequency
components, the weight matrix $w(u,v)$ concentrates gradient pressure on high-frequency
components (fine edges, vessel margins, consolidation boundaries) exactly when they are
most under-recovered. As training progresses and high-frequency errors are corrected, the
weights redistribute, providing a continuous adaptive curriculum over the frequency
spectrum. The FFL term is included in the composite loss with weight $\lambda_{\mathrm{FFL}} = 0.1$.

\subsection{Arm E: Full Composite Loss with KL Divergence}
\label{sec:methods:loss:composite}

\paragraph{Edge loss (Arms L, M, N, P).}
Several arms include a Sobel edge supervision term that penalises loss of fine anatomical boundaries independently of the SSIM and FFL terms:
\begin{equation}
\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{edge}} = \frac{1}{HW}\bigl\|\mathrm{Sobel}(\hat{\mu}_y) - \mathrm{Sobel}(y)\bigr\|_1
\end{equation}
where $\mathrm{Sobel}(\cdot)$ denotes the Sobel gradient magnitude computed by convolving the image with the horizontal and vertical $3\times3$ Sobel kernels and taking the root-sum-square of the two response maps. This loss is computed over \emph{all} pixels: the Sobel operator is applied to the full image and the $\ell_1$ difference is summed everywhere, not restricted to detected edge locations. It directly penalises blurring of diagnostic boundaries (consolidation margins, vessel walls, pleural lines) by matching the spatial gradient structure between the prediction and the clean reference. The edge loss is included in Arms~L, M, N, and~P with weight $\lambda_{\mathrm{edge}} = 0.1$.

Arm E adds the VAE bottleneck (Section~\ref{sec:methods:architecture:bottleneck}) and Kullback-Leibler (KL) divergence regularisation \cite{kullback_information_1951}.\footnote{The Kullback-Leibler (KL) divergence $D_{\mathrm{KL}}(q \| p)$ is an information-theoretic measure of how different one probability distribution $q$ is from a reference distribution $p$. For two Gaussians, it has a closed-form expression (Equation~\ref{eq:methods:kl}). In the VAE framework \cite{kingma_auto-encoding_2014}, minimising the KL term between the learned posterior $q(z|x)$ and the standard normal prior $p(z)$ forces the encoder to produce latent codes that are regularly arranged in latent space, rather than encoding each training image at an arbitrary isolated point. This regularity is what makes Monte Carlo sampling from the posterior meaningful: if the posterior is always close to $\mathcal{N}(0,I)$, drawing different noise samples $\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}$ produces latent codes that span a connected neighbourhood, and the pixel-wise variance across the resulting reconstructions is a genuine measure of epistemic uncertainty rather than noise from a poorly constrained sampler.} The KL term measures how far the learned posterior
distribution $q(\mathbf{z} \mid x) = \mathcal{N}(\mathbf{z}_\mu,\,\exp(\mathbf{z}_{\log\sigma^2}))$
deviates from the standard normal prior $p(\mathbf{z}) = \mathcal{N}(\mathbf{0}, \mathbf{I})$,
and has the closed-form expression:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:methods:kl}
\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{KL}} = -\frac{1}{2}\sum_{c,h,w}
  \Bigl[1 + {\mathbf{z}_{\log\sigma^2}} - {\mathbf{z}_\mu}^2
  - \exp({\mathbf{z}_{\log\sigma^2}})\Bigr]_{c,h,w}
\end{equation}
where the sum runs over all channels $c$ and spatial positions $(h,w)$ of the bottleneck
feature map. The KL penalty prevents the model from encoding a unique, perfectly
deterministic code for each training image. Instead it forces the posterior to remain broad
enough that sampling from it produces plausible reconstructions -- which is the property
that makes the Monte Carlo epistemic uncertainty estimate in
Equation~\ref{eq:methods:epistemic} meaningful.

To prevent posterior collapse (Section~\ref{sec:intro:existing:synthesis}) \cite{fu_cyclical_2019, bowman_generating_2016}, the KL weight $\beta$ is annealed from 0 to $\beta_{\max} = 10^{-3}$
over the first 20 training epochs:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:methods:beta}
\beta_t = \min\!\left(\beta_{\max},\; \beta_{\max} \cdot \frac{t}{T_{\mathrm{warm}}}\right),
\qquad T_{\mathrm{warm}} = 20
\end{equation}

Table~\ref{tab:methods:loss_summary} summarises the loss configuration for all 16 ablation arms spanning the full factorial design of this study.
