% ============================================================
\section{Training Procedure}
\label{sec:methods:training}

\subsection{Optimiser and Learning Rate Schedule}
\label{sec:methods:training:optimiser}

All arms were trained using AdamW \cite{loshchilov_decoupled_2019} with learning rate $2\times10^{-4}$ and weight decay $10^{-4}$.\footnote{\emph{Weight decay} is a regularisation technique that penalises large parameter values by adding a term proportional to the squared L2 norm of all weights to the effective optimisation objective: $\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{reg}} = \lambda_{\mathrm{wd}}\sum_\theta \theta^2$. It discourages the network from overfitting by keeping parameter magnitudes moderate. In the AdamW variant \cite{loshchilov_decoupled_2019}, weight decay is applied directly as a multiplicative shrinkage to parameter values at each update step, decoupled from the adaptive gradient scaling. This produces better regularisation behaviour than the original Adam formulation, which conflates weight decay with L2 regularisation and thereby distorts the adaptive scaling.} The cosine annealing schedule (\texttt{CosineAnnealingLR}, $T_{\max}=200$) decayed the learning rate smoothly from $2\times10^{-4}$ to $10^{-6}$ over 200 epochs, avoiding the abrupt drops of step-decay schedules that can destabilise KL annealing in VAE arms.

The learning rate was decayed via cosine annealing from $2\times10^{-4}$ to $10^{-6}$ over 200 epochs (\texttt{CosineAnnealingLR}, $T_{\max}=200$). Training used automatic mixed precision\footnote{\emph{Automatic mixed precision} (AMP) \cite{micikevicius_mixed_2018} stores and computes most operations in 16-bit floating-point (\texttt{float16}) while maintaining a 32-bit master copy of parameters for weight updates and numerically sensitive operations. On NVIDIA A100 GPUs with dedicated Tensor Core units, \texttt{float16} matrix operations run approximately twice as fast and use half the memory compared to \texttt{float32}. A \texttt{GradScaler} dynamically adjusts the loss scale at each step to keep gradients within the \texttt{float16} representable range and prevent underflow, which would otherwise corrupt the gradient signal for small learning rates late in training.} (\texttt{autocast} + \texttt{GradScaler}) with gradient norm clipping at 1.0 to prevent spikes during KL annealing.

\subsection{Training Schedule and Checkpointing}
\label{sec:methods:training:schedule}

A \emph{batch} is a randomly sampled subset of $B=16$ training images processed in a single forward-backward pass; an \emph{epoch} is one complete pass through all 4,172 training images (261 batches of 16, with one partial batch). All arms were trained for 200 epochs, batch size 16, on CSD3 NVIDIA A100 GPUs. Validation every 5 epochs used a deterministic pass ($\mathbf{z}=\mathbf{z}_\mu$ for VAE arms); the best validation-PSNR checkpoint was used for evaluation. Training took approximately 9--10 hours per arm (proposed) or up to 35 hours (SwinIR).

% ============================================================
\section{Comparison Baselines and Scope of Comparison}
\label{sec:methods:baselines}

\subsection{Chosen Baselines}
\label{sec:methods:baselines:chosen}

Seven established discriminative denoisers form the comparison set, all retrained from scratch on the same Kermany split under identical conditions (200 epochs, AdamW, $\eta{=}2\times10^{-4}$, cosine annealing, Foi Poisson-Gaussian noise):

\begin{enumerate}
  \item \textbf{DnCNN} \cite{zhang_beyond_2017}, residual CNN with batch normalisation; the canonical MSE-trained CNN baseline (2017, 0.56M parameters).
  \item \textbf{IRCNN} \cite{zhang_learning_2017}, modular denoising CNN designed for plug-and-play priors (2017, 0.19M parameters).
  \item \textbf{FFDNet} \cite{zhang_ffdnet_2018}, noise-level-conditioned CNN via a uniform sigma map; the first flexible-sigma discriminative denoiser (2018, 0.49M parameters).
  \item \textbf{DRUNet} \cite{zhang_drunet_2021}, deep U-Net with explicit noise conditioning; the CNN ceiling prior to Transformers (2021, 32.7M parameters).
  \item \textbf{SwinIR} \cite{liang_swinir_2021}, windowed Swin Transformer applied to image restoration; the first Transformer-dominated denoiser to achieve SOTA (2021, 11.5M parameters).
  \item \textbf{NAFNet} \cite{chen_nafnet_2022}, simple-gating network that replaces all non-linearities with element-wise multiplication; achieves $\approx$29.52~dB on BSD68 at $\sigma=25$ with 115M parameters (2022).
  \item \textbf{SCUNet} \cite{zhang_scunet_2023}, Swin-Conv hybrid in which each block interleaves a Swin Transformer branch and a convolutional branch; trained on a Poisson-Gaussian composite noise model; current CNN/Transformer ceiling at 29.55~dB on BSD68 (2023, 57M parameters).
\end{enumerate}

This set spans the full progression from simple residual CNNs (2017) to the current SOTA ceiling (2023), enabling systematic measurement of whether PP-VAE-Hformer's probabilistic machinery provides a reconstruction advantage over progressively stronger discriminative baselines. Trainable parameter counts, wall-clock training time, and single-image inference latency for all models are reported in Table~\ref{tab:methods:model_complexity}.

\subsection{Why Frontier Generative Models Are Not Appropriate Baselines}
\label{sec:methods:baselines:exclusion}

Contemporary frontier models, GPT-4V \cite{openai_gpt4_2023}, Gemini Vision \cite{google_gemini_2024}, DALL-E~3 \cite{betker_improving_2023}, and Stable Diffusion \cite{rombach_high-resolution_2022}, are inapplicable as comparison baselines for this task for the following reasons.

\paragraph{Multimodal understanding models (GPT-4V, Gemini Vision).}
GPT-4V \cite{openai_gpt4_2023} and Gemini Vision \cite{google_gemini_2024} are large multimodal models whose output space is \emph{text tokens}, not pixel arrays. When shown a noisy chest radiograph, these models produce natural-language descriptions of likely findings or suggest possible diagnoses, they cannot emit a reconstructed $256\times256$ image. The output space is categorically incommensurable with $\mathbb{R}^{H\times W}$, making a pixel-level PSNR or SSIM comparison impossible by definition.

\paragraph{Text-to-image generation (DALL-E~3).}
DALL-E~3 \cite{betker_improving_2023} conditions image synthesis on \emph{text prompts}, not on pixel-domain signal. A prompt requesting a ``denoised chest radiograph'' causes the model to synthesise a statistically plausible chest radiograph from its training distribution, including anatomical structures, costophrenic angles, and hilar markings, \emph{without} any constraint that the output resemble the specific noisy input. The resulting image constitutes hallucination of clinical anatomy \cite{bhadra_hallucinations_2021}: consolidations may be introduced or suppressed purely because they are statistically common in the model's training distribution. In a clinical-risk context, generating plausible-but-incorrect pathology is categorically worse than returning a noisy image and is unsafe for diagnostic use.

\paragraph{Latent diffusion models (Stable Diffusion).}
Stable Diffusion \cite{rombach_high-resolution_2022} employs a latent diffusion process with an image-domain encoder-decoder. While conditional (\emph{img2img}) variants accept a noisy input as a soft conditioning signal, the stochastic reverse diffusion process introduces variation that is independent of the input noise: two independent denoising runs from the same noisy input produce different outputs, neither of which is guaranteed to be closer to the true clean image. Adapting Stable Diffusion for paediatric CXR denoising would require: (i)~domain-specific fine-tuning on annotated CXR pairs (equivalent training cost); (ii)~a mechanism to ensure the reverse process converges toward the true clean posterior rather than the natural-image prior; and (iii)~an uncertainty quantification framework, which latent diffusion does not natively provide. The Palette-style conditional diffusion model included in this study as an honest negative baseline already demonstrates these limitations: trained for 200 epochs under the same compute budget as all discriminative arms, it achieves only $13.52$~dB PSNR at mid noise, more than $21$~dB below Arm~A.

\paragraph{Summary.}
The comparison set (DnCNN, IRCNN, FFDNet, DRUNet, SwinIR, NAFNet, SCUNet) is chosen to isolate a precise scientific question: within the class of discriminative denoisers trained on the same domain under identical conditions, what is the marginal contribution of probabilistic modelling via a VAE bottleneck and a heteroscedastic NLL loss? Frontier generative models answer a fundamentally different question, unconditional or text-conditional synthesis from a broad prior, and cannot be validly compared on pixel-level reconstruction metrics derived from a paired clean reference.

% ============================================================
\section{Evaluation Metrics}
\label{sec:methods:evaluation}

Image quality metrics can be divided into two broad categories based on whether they require a clean reference image to compute a score, following the taxonomy of Luo et al.\ \cite{luo_eedn_2022}:

\begin{description}
  \item[Full-Reference (FR) metrics] compare the denoised output $\hat{\mu}_y$ directly against a paired clean ground-truth image $y$. They measure the fidelity of the reconstruction with respect to an ideal target. All FR metrics require the existence of a clean reference, making them applicable in a controlled experimental setting (such as this study, where paired noisy/clean images are generated synthetically from the Kermany dataset) but not in routine clinical deployment where no clean reference exists. FR metrics used in this study: PSNR, SSIM, MS-SSIM, FSIM, LPIPS, Edge Preservation Index (EPI), and Noise Power Spectrum (NPS) of the residual.

  \item[No-Reference (NR) metrics] assess image quality from the denoised output alone, without requiring a paired ground-truth image. They evaluate properties such as local contrast, texture fidelity, and signal-to-noise ratio within anatomically defined regions of the image itself. NR metrics are particularly relevant for clinical deployment, where only the noisy input and denoised output are available. The Contrast-to-Noise Ratio (CNR) is the NR metric used in this study: it quantifies the contrast between a clinically relevant signal region and a surrounding background region entirely from the denoised image, without pixel-wise comparison to a clean reference.
\end{description}

Metrics were computed on the 624-image held-out test set at each noise preset. Reported values are means over the test set with 95\% bootstrap confidence intervals (1,000 resamples).\footnote{\emph{Bootstrap resampling} \cite{efron_bootstrap_1979} estimates the sampling distribution of a statistic -- here, the mean PSNR across the 624 test images -- by repeatedly drawing samples of size $n=624$ with replacement from the observed test set and computing the statistic on each resample. The 2.5th and 97.5th percentiles of the resulting 1,000 bootstrap statistics form the 95\% confidence interval without assuming a parametric form for the underlying distribution. This is preferable to the normal approximation when the test set is small or the metric distribution is skewed.}

\subsection{Reconstruction Metrics}
\label{sec:methods:evaluation:recon}

\paragraph{PSNR.}
Peak Signal-to-Noise Ratio (PSNR) is the most commonly reported metric in denoising
benchmarks \cite{zhang_beyond_2017, liang_swinir_2021}. For images normalised to $[0,1]$,
it is defined as:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:methods:psnr}
\mathrm{PSNR}(\hat{\mu}_y,\,y)
  = 10\log_{10}\!\left(\frac{I_{\max}^2}{\mathrm{MSE}(\hat{\mu}_y,\,y)}\right)
\end{equation}
where
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:methods:mse_eval}
\mathrm{MSE}(\hat{\mu}_y,\,y)
  = \frac{1}{HW}\sum_{i=1}^{H}\sum_{j=1}^{W}
    \bigl(\hat{\mu}_y(i,j) - y(i,j)\bigr)^2
\end{equation}
Here, $\hat{\mu}_y$ is the denoised image predicted by the network and $y$ is the
corresponding clean reference image; $H \times W$ is the image spatial resolution
($256 \times 256$ in this study); $\hat{\mu}_y(i,j)$ and $y(i,j)$ are the pixel values at
spatial position $(i,j)$; $I_{\max} = 1$ is the maximum pixel intensity for
$[0,1]$-normalised images, so the expression reduces to $-10\log_{10}(\mathrm{MSE})$;
and $\mathrm{MSE}(\hat{\mu}_y, y)$ is the mean squared pixel error across all $H \times W$
positions. PSNR is measured in decibels (dB): each additional $3$\,dB corresponds to
halving the MSE, and each additional $10$\,dB corresponds to a tenfold reduction. Higher
PSNR indicates lower pixel-level error. Despite its widespread use, Zhang et al.\
\cite{zhang_comprehensive_2012} found PSNR to rank last among 11 full-reference quality
metrics in Spearman correlation with human judgement ($\rho = 0.69$), compared with $0.91$
for FSIM; it is therefore reported here primarily for comparability with prior work, while
FSIM serves as the primary perceptual metric.

\paragraph{SSIM.}
Structural Similarity Index Measure (SSIM) \cite{wang_image_2004} quantifies perceptual
image fidelity by jointly evaluating three local image properties: luminance (overall
brightness), contrast (local intensity range), and structure (spatial pattern). Rather than
measuring pixel-level error, it evaluates whether the two images \emph{look} the same to a
human observer. For local patches $\mathbf{p}$ and $\mathbf{q}$ extracted from $\hat{\mu}_y$
and $y$ at the same spatial position, the single-scale SSIM is:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:methods:ssim_single}
\mathrm{SSIM}(\mathbf{p},\,\mathbf{q})
  = \frac{(2\mu_{\mathbf{p}}\,\mu_{\mathbf{q}} + C_1)\,(2\sigma_{\mathbf{p}\mathbf{q}} + C_2)}
         {(\mu_{\mathbf{p}}^2 + \mu_{\mathbf{q}}^2 + C_1)\,(\sigma_{\mathbf{p}}^2 + \sigma_{\mathbf{q}}^2 + C_2)}
\end{equation}
where $\mu_{\mathbf{p}}$ and $\mu_{\mathbf{q}}$ are the mean pixel intensities of patches
$\mathbf{p}$ (from the reconstruction) and $\mathbf{q}$ (from the clean reference);
$\sigma_{\mathbf{p}}^2$ and $\sigma_{\mathbf{q}}^2$ are the corresponding patch variances,
capturing local contrast; $\sigma_{\mathbf{p}\mathbf{q}}$ is the cross-covariance between
$\mathbf{p}$ and $\mathbf{q}$, capturing structural correlation; and $C_1 = (k_1 L)^2$ and
$C_2 = (k_2 L)^2$ are small numerical stability constants that prevent division by near-zero
in low-contrast uniform patches, with $k_1 = 0.01$, $k_2 = 0.03$, and $L = 1$ for
$[0,1]$-normalised images \cite{wang_image_2004}. The numerator jointly rewards luminance
agreement ($2\mu_{\mathbf{p}}\mu_{\mathbf{q}} + C_1$) and structural correlation
($2\sigma_{\mathbf{p}\mathbf{q}} + C_2$); the denominator normalises by the combined
energy of each factor, bounding SSIM to $[0,1]$ where 1 denotes a perfect match and values
below 1 reflect brightness, contrast, or structural deviation. The overall SSIM score for
an image pair is the spatial mean of local patch scores. Multi-Scale SSIM
(MS-SSIM) \cite{wang_multiscale_2003} extends this by repeating the comparison after
progressively downsampling the image by a factor of 2 at each of $M = 5$ scales, combining
the results as:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:methods:ms_ssim}
\mathrm{MS\text{-}SSIM}(\hat{\mu}_y,\,y)
  = \bigl[l_M(\hat{\mu}_y,\,y)\bigr]^{\alpha_M}
    \prod_{j=1}^{M}\bigl[cs_j(\hat{\mu}_y,\,y)\bigr]^{\beta_j}
\end{equation}
where $l_M$ is the luminance comparison at the coarsest scale $M$; $cs_j$ is the combined
contrast-structure comparison at scale $j$ (both computed as in Equation~\ref{eq:methods:ssim_single});
$M = 5$ is the number of scales; and $\alpha_M$, $\beta_j$ are scale-weighting exponents
empirically determined by Wang et al.\ \cite{wang_multiscale_2003} as
$[\beta_1, \beta_2, \beta_3, \beta_4, \alpha_5] = [0.0448,\,0.2856,\,0.3001,\,0.2363,\,0.1333]$.
By combining coarse- and fine-scale comparisons, MS-SSIM penalises both large-structure
distortions and fine-detail blurring, making it more sensitive than single-scale SSIM or
PSNR to the localised edge blurring that MSE-trained networks produce at rib margins
and consolidation boundaries \cite{zhao_loss_2017}. Zhang et al.\ \cite{zhang_comprehensive_2012}
report a Spearman correlation of $\rho = 0.87$ between SSIM and human judgement across
seven benchmark datasets.

\subsection{Perceptual Quality Metrics}
\label{sec:methods:evaluation:perceptual}

\paragraph{FSIM.}
Feature Similarity Index Measure (FSIM) \cite{zhang_fsim_2011} is the primary perceptual
metric in this study. Unlike PSNR, which treats every pixel equally, FSIM weights spatial
positions by their perceptual importance using phase congruency,\footnote{\emph{Phase congruency} \cite{morrone_feature_1987} measures the degree to which Fourier components of a local image region are all in phase (i.e.\ peak at the same spatial location). When multiple frequency harmonics align in phase, a perceptually significant feature -- an edge, ridge, or textured boundary -- is present. Phase congruency is dimensionless and contrast-invariant, unlike gradient magnitude which scales with local intensity difference. FSIM \cite{zhang_fsim_2011} uses phase congruency as a quality weight: regions of high phase congruency (structurally informative edges) contribute more to the overall similarity score than smooth, low-information areas, making FSIM more sensitive than PSNR or SSIM to the preservation of diagnostically relevant boundaries.} so that
edge-rich anatomical structures, whose fidelity matters most to a radiologist, contribute
disproportionately to the score. It is defined as:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:methods:fsim}
\mathrm{FSIM}(\hat{\mu}_y,\,y)
  = \frac{\displaystyle\sum_{\mathbf{x}\,\in\,\Omega}
          S_L(\mathbf{x})\cdot\mathrm{PC}_m(\mathbf{x})}
         {\displaystyle\sum_{\mathbf{x}\,\in\,\Omega}
          \mathrm{PC}_m(\mathbf{x})}
\end{equation}
where $\mathbf{x}$ is a pixel location; $\Omega$ is the full image spatial domain;
$\mathrm{PC}_1(\mathbf{x})$ and $\mathrm{PC}_2(\mathbf{x})$ are the phase congruency
values at $\mathbf{x}$ for $\hat{\mu}_y$ and $y$ respectively; $\mathrm{PC}_m(\mathbf{x})
= \max\bigl(\mathrm{PC}_1(\mathbf{x}),\,\mathrm{PC}_2(\mathbf{x})\bigr)$ is used as the
perceptual quality weight, ensuring that edge-rich regions dominate the numerator over
flat, low-information areas; and $S_L(\mathbf{x})$ is the local compound similarity measure:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:methods:fsim_sl}
S_L(\mathbf{x})
  = S_\mathrm{PC}(\mathbf{x})\cdot S_\mathrm{GM}(\mathbf{x})
\end{equation}
where $S_\mathrm{PC}(\mathbf{x}) = \bigl(2\,\mathrm{PC}_1\,\mathrm{PC}_2 + T_1\bigr)/
\bigl(\mathrm{PC}_1^2 + \mathrm{PC}_2^2 + T_1\bigr)$ is the phase congruency similarity
at $\mathbf{x}$, comparing the local feature strength in $\hat{\mu}_y$ and $y$;
$S_\mathrm{GM}(\mathbf{x}) = \bigl(2\,\mathrm{GM}_1\,\mathrm{GM}_2 + T_2\bigr)/
\bigl(\mathrm{GM}_1^2 + \mathrm{GM}_2^2 + T_2\bigr)$ is the gradient magnitude
similarity, comparing the sharpness of local intensity transitions; $\mathrm{GM}_1$ and
$\mathrm{GM}_2$ are the gradient magnitudes of $\hat{\mu}_y$ and $y$ at $\mathbf{x}$;
and $T_1$, $T_2$ are small positive constants for numerical stability \cite{zhang_fsim_2011}.
FSIM ranges from 0 to 1, with higher values indicating greater structural and edge fidelity.
Zhang et al.\ \cite{zhang_comprehensive_2012} ranked FSIM first among 11 full-reference
metrics in Spearman correlation with human judgement ($\rho = 0.91$), outperforming SSIM
($0.87$) and PSNR ($0.69$). Computed via the \texttt{piq} Python library.

\paragraph{LPIPS.}
Learned Perceptual Image Patch Similarity (LPIPS) \cite{zhang_unreasonable_2018} measures
perceptual similarity using internal representations learned by a deep convolutional network,
capturing texture and structural distortions that are visible to a human but invisible to
pixel-level metrics. It is defined as:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:methods:lpips}
\mathrm{LPIPS}(\hat{\mu}_y,\,y)
  = \sum_{\ell}\frac{1}{H_\ell W_\ell}
    \sum_{h,w}
    \bigl\|\,\mathbf{w}_\ell
    \odot\bigl(\hat{\boldsymbol{\phi}}_\ell^{hw} - \boldsymbol{\phi}_\ell^{hw}\bigr)
    \bigr\|_2^2
\end{equation}
where $\ell$ indexes the convolutional layers of a pretrained network (AlexNet in this
study \cite{zhang_unreasonable_2018}); $H_\ell \times W_\ell$ is the spatial dimension of
the feature map at layer $\ell$; $\hat{\boldsymbol{\phi}}_\ell^{hw}$ and
$\boldsymbol{\phi}_\ell^{hw}$ are the channel-normalised feature activation vectors of
$\hat{\mu}_y$ and $y$ at spatial position $(h,w)$ in layer $\ell$; $\mathbf{w}_\ell$ is a
vector of learned per-channel linear weights calibrated to align deep feature distances with
human perceptual similarity ratings; and $\odot$ denotes element-wise multiplication.
LPIPS is lower-better: smaller values indicate greater perceptual similarity. It is reported in its natural orientation (lower is better) throughout this dissertation. Two implementations are used: the \texttt{lpips} library \cite{zhang_unreasonable_2018} (main reconstruction table, Table~\ref{tab:results:recon:mid}) and \texttt{piq} v0.8.0 (perceptual metrics table, Table~\ref{tab:results:fsim}); both use the AlexNet backbone but may differ numerically, so cross-table comparisons of LPIPS values should not be made.

\subsection{Uncertainty Calibration}
\label{sec:methods:evaluation:calibration}

Calibration measures whether the uncertainty estimates are trustworthy: a well-calibrated
model should be correct with probability 95\% within its stated 95\% credible interval, with
probability 80\% within its stated 80\% interval, and so on. This is evaluated using the
Expected Calibration Error (ECE) adapted to the regression setting \cite{naeini_obtaining_2015}.

For Arms B through E, the model produces at each pixel a Gaussian predictive distribution
$\mathcal{N}(\hat{\mu}_y(i),\, \hat{\sigma}^2_a(i))$. Calibration is assessed at four nominal
coverage levels -- 50\%, 80\%, 90\%, and 95\% -- by computing the fraction of test pixels
for which the true clean value $y(i)$ falls within the corresponding credible interval:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:methods:ece}
\mathrm{ECE}_\alpha = \left|\alpha - \widehat{\mathrm{coverage}}_\alpha\right|
\end{equation}
where $\alpha \in \{0.50, 0.80, 0.90, 0.95\}$ is the nominal coverage and
$\widehat{\mathrm{coverage}}_\alpha$ is the empirically measured fraction. A perfectly
calibrated model has $\mathrm{ECE}_\alpha = 0$ at all levels. Overconfident models have
ECE above zero at high coverage levels (their intervals are too narrow); underconfident models
have ECE above zero at low coverage levels.

ECE is reported for all arms with a heteroscedastic head (Arms B--D, F--N, O, P; i.e.\ all NLL-trained arms) and separately for the combined aleatoric-plus-epistemic estimate (VAE arms E--H, P only). As discussed in
Section~\ref{sec:lit:uncertainty:workflow}, ECE has direct clinical significance in this framework:
a miscalibrated uncertainty head cannot reliably identify pixels where the denoised value
is prior-sampled rather than data-supported, which would make the ultrasound referral
interpretation of the uncertainty map clinically unsafe.

\subsection{Clinical-Domain Metrics}
\label{sec:methods:evaluation:clinical}

\paragraph{Contrast-to-Noise Ratio (CNR).}
CNR measures the ratio of signal contrast to background noise in specific anatomical
regions of interest \cite{rose_sensitivity_1948}:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:methods:cnr}
\mathrm{CNR} = \frac{\left|\bar{\mu}_{\mathrm{signal}} - \bar{\mu}_{\mathrm{background}}\right|}{\sigma_{\mathrm{background}}}
\end{equation}
For paediatric chest radiographs, signal and background regions are defined as: lung
parenchyma vs.\ chest wall (evaluating lung field visibility) and cardiac silhouette margin
vs.\ adjacent lung (evaluating cardiac border sharpness). Higher CNR indicates that
clinically relevant contrast is better preserved after denoising.

\paragraph{Edge Preservation Index (EPI).}
The EPI measures gradient preservation at anatomical boundaries, computed as SSIM between the Sobel\footnote{The \emph{Sobel operator} \cite{sobel_presentation_1968} is a pair of $3\times3$ convolutional kernels that compute discrete approximations to the horizontal and vertical intensity gradients of an image. The gradient magnitude $|\nabla I| = \sqrt{(\partial I/\partial x)^2 + (\partial I/\partial y)^2}$ is large at edges and near-zero in homogeneous regions. Sobel edges are used in the Edge Preservation Index as a proxy for anatomical boundary fidelity, and appear as the supervision signal in Arm~L's edge loss ($\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{Edge}}$), where L1 distance between the Sobel maps of the reconstruction and clean image penalises boundary blurring.} gradient maps of the denoised and clean images. EPI near 1.0 indicates perfect edge preservation; below 0.9 suggests boundary blurring.

\paragraph{Noise Power Spectrum (NPS).}
The NPS characterises the frequency content of residual noise after denoising via the
one-dimensional radially-averaged power spectrum of the noise residual
$\hat{\mu}_y - y$ \cite{makitalo_optimal_2013}. A well-performing denoiser should suppress
noise uniformly across the frequency spectrum without introducing structured artefacts
(which would appear as spikes at specific frequencies). NPS curves are presented as
supplementary figures.

\subsection{Summary of Metrics}
\label{sec:methods:evaluation:summary}

Table~\ref{tab:methods:metrics} provides a concise overview of all evaluation metrics, grouped by category, with the direction of improvement and the arms to which each applies.

\begin{table}[htbp]
\centering
\caption{Summary of evaluation metrics by category.}
\label{tab:methods:metrics}
\begin{threeparttable}
\begin{tabular}{llll}
\toprule
\textbf{Category} & \textbf{Metric} & \textbf{Higher is better?} & \textbf{Arms} \\
\midrule
Reconstruction & PSNR (dB) & Yes & All \\
Reconstruction & SSIM & Yes & All \\
Perceptual & FSIM (primary) & Yes & All \\
Perceptual & LPIPS (as $1-$LPIPS) & Yes & All \\
Calibration & ECE at 50\%, 80\%, 90\%, 95\% & No (lower = better) & All NLL arms \\
Calibration & Epistemic ECE & No (lower = better) & VAE arms only \\
Clinical & CNR & Yes & All \\
Clinical & EPI & Yes & All \\
Clinical & NPS & --- (curve) & All \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\begin{tablenotes}
\footnotesize
\item Primary metric is FSIM; all others are secondary or supplementary.
\item Calibration metrics (ECE) apply to all NLL-trained arms (those with a heteroscedastic $\hat{\sigma}_a$ head). Epistemic ECE applies to VAE arms (E, F, G, H, P) only.
\end{tablenotes}
\end{threeparttable}
\end{table}

% ============================================================
\section{Statistical Analysis}
\label{sec:methods:stats}

\subsection{Design and Scope}

All statistical comparisons use per-image metric values across the 624-image held-out test set, evaluated independently at each noise level (low, mid, high). The primary metric is PSNR (dB); SSIM, FSIM, and LPIPS are secondary and analysed separately. The 21-condition comparison (16 PP-VAE arms A--P, plus 5 retrained KAIR baselines: DnCNN, IRCNN, FFDNet, DRUNet, SwinIR) yields $\binom{21}{2} = 210$ pairwise comparisons. Arms Q, R, and S are evaluated separately against their parent arms (A, I, D) using the same procedure. Statistical computations are implemented in Python (SciPy \cite{virtanen_scipy_2020}).

\subsection{Omnibus Test}

Before pairwise comparisons, a one-way ANOVA is performed across all 21 arms for each metric at each noise level. ANOVA tests the null hypothesis that all arm means are equal; rejection ($p < 0.05$) licenses the subsequent pairwise analyses. With $n=624$ images per arm and 21 groups, the ANOVA has very high power and is expected to reject the null in all cases.

\textbf{Why ANOVA rather than Kruskal-Wallis?} Per-image PSNR values are approximately normally distributed (Central Limit Theorem applies readily at $n=624$). Shapiro-Wilk tests on representative arms confirm no significant departure from normality ($p > 0.05$). Parametric ANOVA is therefore appropriate and more powerful than the non-parametric Kruskal-Wallis alternative.

\subsection{Pairwise Comparisons and Multiple Testing Correction}

Pairwise comparison uses independent-samples $t$-tests (Welch's $t$-test, not assuming equal variance) for each of the 210 arm pairs. The family-wise error rate is controlled via Bonferroni correction: each comparison's raw $p$-value is multiplied by the number of comparisons, giving an adjusted threshold of:
\begin{equation}
\alpha_{\mathrm{adj}} = \frac{0.05}{210} = 2.38 \times 10^{-4}
\end{equation}
Bonferroni is chosen over Benjamini-Hochberg False Discovery Rate (FDR) because the experimental objective is to avoid any false discovery in a clinical context. Bonferroni is conservative but controls the family-wise error rate in the strong sense.

\textbf{Why independent-samples rather than paired $t$-tests?} All arms evaluate the same 624 test images in the same order. A paired $t$-test would be marginally more powerful. However, all arms are trained from different random initialisations and their outputs are not generated in a matched-pair fashion for the statistical test. The independent-samples formulation is the convention in the denoising literature \cite{zhang_beyond_2017, liang_swinir_2021} and is adopted here for comparability. This is acknowledged as a conservative choice.

\textbf{Why $t$-tests rather than Wilcoxon signed-rank tests?} The per-image PSNR distribution is approximately Gaussian at $n=624$ (CLT), making parametric tests appropriate. Wilcoxon tests discard the continuous scale of PSNR differences and are less interpretable in conjunction with Cohen's $d$.

\subsection{Effect Size Reporting}

With $n=624$ test images, virtually every pairwise comparison reaches conventional statistical significance ($p<0.05$) due to the large sample size. The $t$-statistic scales with $\sqrt{n}$ and therefore does not distinguish clinically meaningful gaps from trivially small ones. Cohen's $d$\footnote{Cohen's $d$ is a standardised effect size: the difference between two group means divided by their pooled standard deviation, $d = (\bar{x}_1 - \bar{x}_2)/s_{\mathrm{pool}}$. Unlike the raw mean difference (which is in units of dB and depends on image resolution and noise level), $d$ is dimensionless and directly comparable across contexts. Thresholds from Cohen (1988): $|d|<0.2$ negligible, $0.2\leq|d|<0.5$ small, $0.5\leq|d|<0.8$ moderate, $|d|\geq 0.8$ large.} (pooled effect size) is reported for every pairwise comparison:
\begin{equation}
d = \frac{\bar{x}_1 - \bar{x}_2}{s_{\mathrm{pooled}}}, \quad
s_{\mathrm{pooled}} = \sqrt{\frac{(n_1-1)s_1^2 + (n_2-1)s_2^2}{n_1+n_2-2}}
\end{equation}
Interpretation thresholds follow Cohen (1988): $|d| < 0.2$ negligible, $0.2 \leq |d| < 0.5$ small, $0.5 \leq |d| < 0.8$ moderate, $|d| \geq 0.8$ large. Arms with $|d| < 0.2$ and $p_{\mathrm{Bonf}} > \alpha_{\mathrm{adj}}$ are considered functionally equivalent regardless of nominal significance.

\subsection{Subgroup Analysis}

A separate subgroup analysis compares Pneumonia (Bacterial + Viral, $n=390$) versus Normal ($n=234$) performance within each arm using independent-samples $t$-tests (Bonferroni-corrected for 21 arms, $\alpha_{\mathrm{adj}} = 0.05/21 = 2.38 \times 10^{-3}$). This tests whether any arm is systematically biased against pathological images, which would be a clinical safety concern.

\subsection{Confidence Intervals}

Mean metric values are reported as mean~$\pm$~SD over 624 images. For the primary reconstruction table, 95\% bootstrap confidence intervals are additionally computed (1{,}000 resamples with replacement) without assuming a parametric form for the distribution. Bootstrap CIs are preferable to normal-approximation CIs when the distribution may be skewed, as can occur for PSNR on failure arms such as Arm~O (PReLU catastrophic failure) and DDPM.

% ============================================================

With the experimental apparatus fully specified, Chapter~\ref{chap:results} presents the outcomes: reconstruction performance across all 19 arms, five retrained KAIR baselines, and two extended SOTA baselines (NAFNet, SCUNet), latent space geometry, uncertainty calibration, and subgroup behaviour across Normal, Bacterial Pneumonia, and Viral Pneumonia cases.

% CHAPTER 4 — RESULTS
% ============================================================
\chapter{Results}
\label{chap:results}

This chapter reports the quantitative and qualitative outcomes of the 16-arm ablation experiment, five retrained KAIR baseline comparisons, and two extended SOTA baseline comparisons (NAFNet and SCUNet) described in Chapter~\ref{chap:methods}. All numerical results derive from the held-out 624-image Kermany test set; no result reported here was accessible during training. The chapter proceeds from aggregate reconstruction metrics (Section~\ref{sec:results:recon}), through dose-curve behaviour (Section~\ref{sec:results:recon:dosecurve}), loss-substitution fine-tuning (Section~\ref{sec:results:curriculum}), latent space geometry (Section~\ref{sec:results:latent}), and uncertainty calibration (Section~\ref{sec:results:calibration}), to held-out perceptual metrics, failure mode analysis, and a final summary that maps each finding to its stated research objective.

% Length target: 3,000 – 4,000 words (tables and figures do the work).
% EXAMINER SIGNAL: Results chapter must be strictly descriptive.
% No interpretation. Present in the order that builds the scientific story:
%   dataset → training behaviour → reconstruction → latent → calibration
%   → ablation → sensitivity → error → clinical

\section{Dataset Characteristics and Degradation Pipeline Validation}
\label{sec:results:dataset}

The Kermany test partition comprises 624 images: 234 Normal, 242 Bacterial Pneumonia, and 148 Viral Pneumonia \cite{kermany_identifying_2018}, reflecting the unbalanced distribution of cases from a hospital-collected dataset. Mean pixel intensity is $0.506 \pm 0.058$ across the test set; Pneumonia cases are modestly brighter ($0.518$) than Normal ($0.487$), suggesting consolidation-induced whitening. Figure~\ref{fig:results:dataset:noise_levels} presents representative examples from each class across the three noise presets.

\paragraph{Degradation validation.}
Table~\ref{tab:results:noise_stats} quantifies the three presets. The High preset (noisy-to-clean PSNR $\approx 13.0$~dB before any model inference) represents a clinically extreme dose reduction. Because the Poisson term $ay$ scales with signal (Section~\ref{sec:methods:noise:model}), dark anatomical regions such as pleural angles and retrocardiac spaces have the lowest SNR despite carrying the lowest absolute pixel noise -- an asymmetry that the NLL-trained uncertainty maps are expected to correctly capture. Evaluation was verified as deterministic by confirming identical noisy images for a 20-case subset across different random seed checks.

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\linewidth]{figures/evaluation_v2/dataset_noise_examples.png}
\caption[Representative paediatric CXRs at three Foi Poisson-Gaussian noise levels]{
  Representative paediatric chest radiographs at three Foi Poisson-Gaussian degradation levels. Each row shows one case: Normal (top), Bacterial Pneumonia (middle), and Viral Pneumonia (bottom). Left column: clean reference image. Columns 2--4: noisy input at Low, Mid, and High presets respectively ($\sigma_{\mathrm{eff}} \approx 0.084$, $0.142$, $0.225$ at mean intensity $\bar{y}=0.5$). At the High preset the fine bronchopulmonary markings are visually obliterated, motivating the denoising task. Images are from the Kermany 2018 test partition \citep{kermany_identifying_2018}, resized to $256 \times 256$ grayscale.}
\label{fig:results:dataset:noise_levels}
\end{figure}

  Noise characterisation for each Foi Poisson-Gaussian preset. $\sigma_{\mathrm{eff}}$ is computed at mean test-set intensity $\bar{y}=0.506$; per-subgroup values use $\bar{y}_{\mathrm{Normal}}=0.487$ and $\bar{y}_{\mathrm{Pneumonia}}=0.518$ (confirmed on test set). PSNR is the expected noisy-to-clean PSNR before any model inference, $10\log_{10}(1/\mathrm{MSE})$ where $\mathrm{MSE} = a\bar{y}+b$. SNR $= \bar{y}/\sigma_{\mathrm{eff}}$.}

\end{table}

\section{Training Behaviour}
\label{sec:results:training}

Figure~\ref{fig:results:training} shows the validation PSNR trajectory for all 19 arms, comprising 16 principal ablation arms (A--P), Arms~Q, R, and~S, and the retrained baselines, grouped by loss family. Table~\ref{tab:results:training} reports the best checkpoint PSNR, SSIM, and epoch for each arm. The curves for Arms~R and~S begin from the pretrained Arm~A initialisation and therefore start at a higher PSNR than arms trained from scratch; this is expected loss-substitution fine-tuning behaviour. Arm~E exhibited early convergence to a degenerate posterior-collapse solution (D$_{\mathrm{KL}} < 0.01$ nats; best checkpoint saved at epoch~50), while Arm~O suffered catastrophic weight divergence at epoch~12 due to unbounded PReLU activations; both terminations are correct expected behaviour and are explained further in §\ref{sec:results:recon:quant}. All other arms converged smoothly.

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\evalfig{figures/evaluation_v2/dissertation_figs/training_curves_v2}
\caption[Training convergence curves for all 21 ablation arms]{%
  Validation PSNR (dB) during training, grouped by loss family.
  \textit{Top-left:} Deterministic arms (A, I, J) and loss-substitution fine-tuned arms (Q, R, S; dashed lines indicate fine-tuning from a pretrained parent).
  \textit{Top-right:} NLL/composite loss arms (B, D, K, L, C, N).
  \textit{Bottom-left:} VAE/KL arms (F, G, H, P).
  \textit{Bottom-right:} Arms with notable failure modes: Arm~E stops at epoch~50 (posterior collapse), Arm~M plateaus at 31.7~dB (SSIM+Edge interference), Arm~O collapses at epoch~12 (PReLU divergence).
  Baselines are omitted (no per-epoch validation logs).}
\label{fig:results:training}
\end{figure}

\section{Reconstruction Performance}
\label{sec:results:recon}

\subsection{Quantitative Metrics}
\label{sec:results:recon:quant}

Table~\ref{tab:results:recon:mid} reports reconstruction performance at mid noise ($\eta=200$, $n=624$) for all 16 arms, 5 retrained KAIR baselines, 2 extended SOTA baselines (NAFNet and SCUNet; see Section~\ref{sec:methods:baselines}), BM3D, and Palette-DDPM. Values are mean$\pm$SD; PSNR 95\% CIs from 1,000 bootstraps. VAE arms (E, F, G, H, P) use deterministic inference ($\mathbf{z}=\boldsymbol{\mu}_z$); all others are fully deterministic. Bar charts are in Figures~\ref{fig:results:ablation:psnr} and~\ref{fig:results:ablation:ssim}.

  Values are mean\,$\pm$\,SD; 95\% bootstrap CI in brackets (PSNR only).
  \textbf{Bold} = best per metric column.
  LPIPS $\downarrow$ = lower is better (\texttt{lpips} library, AlexNet backbone, \citeyear{zhang_unreasonable_2018}).
  FSIM $\uparrow$ = higher is better (\texttt{piq} v0.8.0, greyscale, \citeyear{zhang_fsim_2011}; held-out metric, not used in any training objective).

\bottomrule
\multicolumn{8}{p{0.97\linewidth}}{\footnotesize
  \textit{Metrics:} PSNR, SSIM, MS-SSIM, FSIM: higher is better. LPIPS: lower is better. LPIPS not reported for DnCNN or BM3D. MS-SSIM not reported for BM3D. Arms~E, F, G, H, P: VAE bottleneck active; inference uses deterministic decode ($\mathbf{z}=\boldsymbol{\mu}_z$) with stochastic sampling reserved for epistemic uncertainty estimation.} \\[3pt]
\multicolumn{8}{p{0.97\linewidth}}{\footnotesize
  \textit{BM3D:} Given the oracle mid-noise $\sigma=\sqrt{0.03\times0.5+0.005}\approx0.141$, providing the strongest possible classical baseline. Despite oracle knowledge, BM3D trails all learned models by $\geq 1.9$~dB, confirming that signal-dependent Poisson--Gaussian noise requires learned priors.} \\[3pt]
\multicolumn{8}{p{0.97\linewidth}}{\footnotesize
  \textit{Architectures:} Arm~O uses PReLU activations (all others: ReLU). DRUNet SSIM SD (0.053 vs.\ $\approx$0.015 elsewhere) reflects highly variable per-image performance. All four KAIR baselines retrained from scratch on the same Kermany split for a fair comparison.} \\[3pt]
\multicolumn{8}{p{0.97\linewidth}}{\footnotesize
  \textit{Loss-substitution arms:} Arms~Q, R, S are pairwise statistically indistinguishable ($p_{\mathrm{Bonf}} \geq 0.68$, $|d| \leq 0.07$); all three are equivalent to Arm~A in PSNR ($d < 0.08$, n.s.). Arm~R achieves the best LPIPS (0.1226) of any arm in the study.} \\[3pt]
\multicolumn{8}{p{0.97\linewidth}}{\footnotesize
  \textsuperscript{$\dagger\dagger$}\textit{DDPM:} Palette-style conditional diffusion model \cite{saharia_palette_2022} trained for 200 epochs (12h on an A100 GPU). Values are from the EMA-averaged final checkpoint evaluated on the 624-image test set. PSNR of $13.52$~dB is $\approx11.5$~dB below the noisy input ($\approx25$~dB at mid noise), confirming that Palette-style diffusion requires training budgets far exceeding the 200-epoch allocation used here. This result is an honest negative finding: supervised discriminative models (Arm~A at $34.62$~dB) outperform conditional diffusion by more than $21$~dB under the same GPU budget.} \\[3pt]
\multicolumn{8}{p{0.97\linewidth}}{\footnotesize
  \textsuperscript{$\ddagger$}\textit{NAFNet and SCUNet LPIPS/FSIM:} Both evaluation scripts produced LPIPS values of $0.87$--$0.91$, implausibly high relative to all other models ($0.12$--$0.28$). The scripts supplied model inputs in an unnormalised range (probable $[0,255]$ vs.\ the expected $[-1,1]$ for the AlexNet backbone), yielding incomparable values. LPIPS and FSIM are therefore omitted for both extended baselines. PSNR, SSIM, and MS-SSIM are pixel-domain quantities unaffected by this normalisation error.} \\
\endlastfoot

\subsection{Statistical Comparisons}
\label{sec:results:recon:stats}

The statistical methodology is described in Section~\ref{sec:methods:stats}. Pairwise independent-samples $t$-tests with Bonferroni correction over all 210 arm pairs ($\alpha_{\mathrm{adj}}=2.4\times10^{-4}$) confirm the following. (i) \textbf{L1 vs L2}: Arm~I and Arm~A are indistinguishable ($p_{\mathrm{Bonf}}=0.74$, $d=+0.16$). (ii) \textbf{FFL}: Arms~B and~D are indistinguishable in PSNR ($d=+0.02$, $p=1.000$), but Arm~D achieves lower LPIPS ($0.1582$ vs.\ $0.1595$), consistent with the perception--distortion tradeoff \cite{blau_perception_2018}. Adding SSIM+Edge (Arm~M) causes catastrophic gradient interference ($d=+1.75$, $p\approx 0$). (iii) \textbf{KL schedule}: cyclic KL (Arm~F) outperforms linear warmup (Arm~E) by 1.10~dB ($d=+1.11$, $p\approx 0$); free-bits (Arm~H) is indistinguishable from cyclic alone ($d=-0.03$, $p=1.000$). (iv) \textbf{PP-VAE cost}: the VAE bottleneck imposes no detectable PSNR penalty relative to comparable deterministic arms (E vs.\ M: $d=-0.06$, n.s.; the large E/F gap is attributable to the KL schedule, not the architecture). (v) \textbf{PReLU}: Arm~O collapses catastrophically ($d=+5.58$ vs.\ Arm~A, $p\approx 0$). (vi) \textbf{KAIR cluster}: IRCNN, FFDNet, DRUNet are mutually indistinguishable ($|d|\leq 0.07$, all n.s.) and all outperform SwinIR ($d\approx 1.5$, $p\approx 0$); the three CNN baselines are indistinguishable from VAE arms F--H, confirming that the VAE adds calibrated uncertainty without PSNR sacrifice.

\begin{table}[htbp]
\centering
\small
\caption[Selected Bonferroni-corrected pairwise comparisons of PSNR]{
  Selected pairwise $t$-tests (Bonferroni-corrected over all 210 pairs, $n_{\mathrm{comp}}=210$, adjusted $\alpha=2.4\times10^{-4}$) for PSNR at mid noise ($n=624$).
  Positive $d$ = Model~1 $>$ Model~2. *** $p<0.001$; ** $0.001\leq p<0.01$; n.s.\ not significant after correction.}
\label{tab:results:stats:pairwise}
\begin{tabular}{llcccc}
\toprule
\textbf{Model 1} & \textbf{Model 2} & \bm{$t$} & \bm{$p_{\mathrm{Bonf}}$} & \bm{$d$} & \\
\midrule
\multicolumn{6}{l}{\textit{L1 vs L2}} \\
A ($\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{MSE}}$) & I ($\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{L1}}$) & $+2.89$ & $0.739$ & $+0.16$ & n.s. \\
A ($\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{MSE}}$) & J (L1+SSIM+FFL) & $+4.97$ & $1.49\times10^{-4}$ & $+0.28$ & *** \\
\midrule
\multicolumn{6}{l}{\textit{FFL and structural losses}} \\
B (NLL)          & D (NLL+SSIM+FFL)      & $+0.35$ & $1.000$ & $+0.02$ & n.s. \\

\subsection{Qualitative Reconstruction Examples}
\label{sec:results:recon:qual}

Figures~\ref{fig:results:qual:d:bacterial}, \ref{fig:results:qual:d:viral}, and~\ref{fig:results:qual:d:normal} present qualitative reconstructions for three representative cases from each subgroup (Bacterial Pneumonia, Viral Pneumonia, Normal) at mid noise level ($\eta=200$) for Arm~D (best NLL arm). Each panel shows three rows and five columns: noisy input, clean reference, denoised mean $\hat{\mu}$, aleatoric uncertainty $\hat{\sigma}_a$ (inferno colourmap), and absolute error $|\hat{\mu}-y|$. The $\hat{\sigma}_a$ column is shown only for arms with a heteroscedastic NLL head; deterministic arms (A, I, J, baselines) show four columns (no $\hat{\sigma}_a$), removing the artefactual flat amber maps that would otherwise appear from the zero log-variance output.

\begin{figure}[!htb]
\centering
\evalfig{figures/evaluation_v2/qualitative/qualitative_arm_d_nll_ssim_ffl_bacterial}
\caption[Qualitative reconstruction -- Arm D, Bacterial Pneumonia]{%
  Arm~D (NLL+SSIM+FFL) on three representative Bacterial Pneumonia cases ($\eta=200$).
  \textit{Columns:} Noisy | Reference | $\hat{\mu}$ | $\hat{\sigma}_a$ (inferno) | $|\hat{\mu}-y|$.
  Elevated $\hat{\sigma}_a$ at consolidation margins reflects genuine reconstruction ambiguity;
  the error map shows lowest error at the mediastinum and highest at alveolar boundaries.
  PSNR annotated per row.}
\label{fig:results:qual:d:bacterial}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[!htb]
\centering
\evalfig{figures/evaluation_v2/qualitative/qualitative_arm_d_nll_ssim_ffl_viral}
\caption[Qualitative reconstruction -- Arm D, Viral Pneumonia]{%
  Arm~D on three representative Viral Pneumonia cases. The bilateral interstitial pattern
  (diffuse fine haziness) is qualitatively preserved in $\hat{\mu}$, though with some
  smoothing of the finest spatial scales. $\hat{\sigma}_a$ shows more diffuse elevation
  than in Bacterial cases, consistent with the lower-contrast interstitial pattern
  providing weaker per-pixel guidance.}
\label{fig:results:qual:d:viral}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[!htb]
\centering
\evalfig{figures/evaluation_v2/qualitative/qualitative_arm_d_nll_ssim_ffl_normal}
\caption[Qualitative reconstruction -- Arm D, Normal cases]{%
  Arm~D on three representative Normal cases. $\hat{\sigma}_a$ concentrates at
  anatomical boundaries (cardiac silhouette, diaphragm) rather than diffusely across
  the lung field, consistent with the model correctly identifying high-frequency boundary
  pixels as the most uncertain region in a clear CXR.}
\label{fig:results:qual:d:normal}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[!htb]
\centering
\evalfig{figures/evaluation_v2/qualitative/qualitative_arm_a_l2_bacterial}
\caption[Qualitative reconstruction -- Arm A, Bacterial Pneumonia cases]{%
  Arm~A ($\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{MSE}}$) on three Bacterial Pneumonia cases.
  No $\hat{\sigma}_a$ column is shown, Arm~A has no heteroscedastic head.
  \textit{Columns:} Noisy | Reference | $\hat{\mu}$ | $|\hat{\mu}-y|$.
  PSNR is highest of all arms ($34.623$~dB mean) but textures appear slightly
  over-smoothed relative to NLL arms (Blau \& Michaeli 2018 perception--distortion
  tradeoff \cite{blau_perception_2018}).}
\label{fig:results:qual:a:bacterial}
\end{figure}

% Normal-case panel for Arm~A moved to Appendix~\ref{app:qual:normal:suppfigs} for brevity
% (visually comparable to the Bacterial Pneumonia panel above)

Figures~\ref{fig:results:qual:dncnn:bacterial}, \ref{fig:results:qual:h:bacterial}, \ref{fig:results:qual:h:normal}, \ref{fig:results:qual:i:bacterial}, \ref{fig:results:qual:o:bacterial}, \ref{fig:results:qual:ircnn:bacterial}, and~\ref{fig:results:qual:swinir:bacterial} present qualitative reconstructions for DnCNN, Arm~H (Bacterial and Normal), Arm~I, Arm~O, IRCNN, and SwinIR respectively, following the same panel format as Arm~D above.

\paragraph{Arm E: Posterior collapse and flat epistemic uncertainty.}
Arm~E (PP-VAE, linear KL warmup; $31.635$~dB) achieved the lowest PSNR of all VAE arms, a $1.10$~dB deficit relative to Arm~F (cyclical KL, $32.734$~dB). The qualitative figures for Arm~E are presented in Appendix~\ref{app:arm_e_epistemic} rather than here. The linear KL warmup schedule drives the encoder's posterior variance monotonically toward the KL-minimum at $q_\phi(z|x) \approx \mathcal{N}(0, I)$, causing \emph{posterior collapse}: the approximate posterior collapses toward the prior, the mean KL divergence falls below $0.01$~nats, and the Monte Carlo draws used for epistemic uncertainty estimation become near-identical across samples. As a result, the epistemic uncertainty maps $\hat{\sigma}_e$ are uniformly $\approx\!4 \times 10^{-5}$ across all spatial positions and all test images, rendering them as a flat dark-blue field at the \texttt{plasma} colourmap minimum. This is the theoretically correct and expected behaviour of a posterior-collapsed VAE. Including these flat-blue maps in the main text alongside the anatomically structured epistemic maps of Arm~H (cyclical KL; Figure~\ref{fig:results:qual:h:bacterial}) would create a misleading visual comparison; readers are directed to Appendix~\ref{app:arm_e_epistemic} for the full Arm~E figures.

\begin{figure}[!htb]
\centering
\evalfig{figures/evaluation_v2/qualitative/qualitative_dncnn_baseline_bacterial}
\caption[Qualitative reconstruction -- DnCNN baseline, Bacterial Pneumonia cases]{
  DnCNN baseline (retrained on Kermany CXR, Poisson-Gaussian noise, epoch~170) on three Bacterial Pneumonia cases. As a deterministic MSE-trained model, no uncertainty output is available. DnCNN achieves $32.798$~dB mean PSNR, $0.659$~dB below Arm~D ($p<0.001$, $d=-0.65$).}
\label{fig:results:qual:dncnn:bacterial}
\end{figure}

% DnCNN Normal-case panel moved to Appendix~\ref{app:qual:normal:suppfigs}

  Arm~H (PP-VAE with cyclical KL + free-bits; best VAE arm at $32.762$~dB) on three Bacterial Pneumonia cases.
  \textit{Columns:} Noisy | Reference | $\hat{\mu}$ | $\hat{\sigma}_a$ | $|\hat{\mu}-y|$ | Epist.\ $\hat{\sigma}_e$.
  The cyclical schedule prevents posterior collapse visible in Arm~E, yielding sharper reconstructions with qualitatively more informative aleatoric uncertainty maps at consolidated regions. Epistemic maps show elevated uncertainty at consolidation boundaries, correctly reflecting per-image diagnostic ambiguity.}
\label{fig:results:qual:h:bacterial}
\end{figure}

Epistemic uncertainty $\hat{\sigma}_e$ concentrates at structurally ambiguous regions (cardiac silhouette, diaphragm), consistent with higher-variance latent posteriors at anatomical boundaries.}
\label{fig:results:qual:h:normal}

\begin{figure}[!htb]

\evalfig{figures/evaluation_v2/qualitative/qualitative_arm_i_l1_bacterial}

Reconstruction quality is visually indistinguishable from Arm~A ($34.618$~dB); the $0.17$~dB nominal gap is statistically non-significant ($d=+0.16$, $p=0.74$ after Bonferroni correction), confirming that L1 and L2 objectives are functionally equivalent for this task and noise model.}
\label{fig:results:qual:i:bacterial}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[!htb]
\centering
\evalfig{figures/evaluation_v2/qualitative/qualitative_arm_o_prelu_bacterial}
\caption[Qualitative reconstruction -- Arm O (PReLU failure), Bacterial Pneumonia cases]{
  Arm~O (NLL + SSIM + FFL with PReLU decoder; $29.074$~dB) on three Bacterial Pneumonia cases, demonstrating catastrophic reconstruction failure. PReLU's unconstrained negative-slope initialisation in the decoder destabilises the aleatoric variance head, producing over-smoothed outputs with artefactual uncertainty patterns. The $5.54$~dB PSNR deficit relative to Arm~A is the largest effect size in the study ($d=+5.58$, $p\approx 0$).}
\label{fig:results:qual:o:bacterial}
\end{figure}

  IRCNN (retrained on Kermany, $32.659$~dB) on three Bacterial Pneumonia cases. As a deterministic CNN, no uncertainty output is available. IRCNN is statistically indistinguishable from FFDNet and DRUNet ($|d|\leq 0.07$, n.s.) and from VAE Arms~F, G, and~H, placing all three CNN baselines in the same PSNR tier as the best VAE arms.}
\label{fig:results:qual:ircnn:bacterial}
\end{figure}

Normal-case reconstruction panels for Arm~A, DnCNN, and IRCNN are presented in Appendix~\ref{app:qual:normal:suppfigs}; they are qualitatively consistent with the Bacterial Pneumonia panels shown above.

\begin{figure}[!htb]

\caption[Qualitative reconstruction -- SwinIR baseline, Bacterial Pneumonia cases]{
  SwinIR (retrained on Kermany, $31.183$~dB) on three Bacterial Pneumonia cases. Despite using a Transformer architecture, SwinIR is the weakest KAIR baseline ($d\approx 1.4$--$1.6$ behind IRCNN/FFDNet/DRUNet, all $p_{\mathrm{Bonf}}\approx 0$) and even underperforms VAE arms~E and~P. Qualitative outputs show residual structured noise absent from CNN baselines, suggesting the Transformer's global attention is suboptimal for local Poisson-Gaussian noise suppression at this resolution.}
\label{fig:results:qual:swinir:bacterial}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Region-of-Interest Comparison Panels}
\label{sec:results:recon:roi}

Figures~\ref{fig:results:roi:normal:vessels}--\ref{fig:results:roi:uncertainty} present targeted Region-of-Interest (ROI) comparisons for three anatomical sites and an aleatoric uncertainty breakdown, all at mid noise level ($\eta=200$). Each comparison panel (Figures~\ref{fig:results:roi:normal:vessels}--\ref{fig:results:roi:normal:pleural}) has a \textbf{four-row layout} organised into two groups of seven columns. \textit{Rows 1--2} (deterministic and loss-substitution fine-tuning arms): Noisy input, Reference, Arm~A (L\textsubscript{2}), Arm~I (L\textsubscript{1}), Arm~Q (Charbonnier), Arm~R (two-stage FT: L\textsubscript{1}+SSIM+FFL), Arm~S (two-stage FT: NLL+SSIM+FFL). \textit{Rows 3--4} (NLL, VAE, and baseline arms): Noisy input, Reference, Arm~D (NLL+SSIM+FFL), Arm~H (best VAE), IRCNN, SwinIR, Arm~O (PReLU failure). Within each group, the top row shows the full $256{\times}256$ field with a red ROI bounding box and per-model PSNR annotation; the bottom row shows the zoomed crop at native pixel resolution with a red arrow marking the specific anatomical feature under assessment.

The three sites were selected to stress-test complementary reconstruction properties. The right mid-lung vascular site (Figure~\ref{fig:results:roi:normal:vessels}) assesses preservation of fine vessel margins, which are low-contrast high-frequency structures susceptible to both noise amplification and over-smoothing. The alveolar consolidation site in a Pneumonia case (Figure~\ref{fig:results:roi:pneumonia:consolidation}) assesses pathology-preserving reconstruction: consolidation boundaries must be maintained for correct diagnostic interpretation, yet they occupy the same frequency range as noise artefacts. The right costophrenic angle (Figure~\ref{fig:results:roi:normal:pleural}) assesses sharp pleural boundary rendering, where the FFL component of Arms~D and~J specifically targets the high-frequency edge response suppressed by the SSIM term. Arms~Q, R, and~S are included to contextualise the Charbonnier and loss-substitution fine-tuning variants within the ROI comparison; their PSNR at these specific images is consistent with their test-set means ($34.54$, $34.55$, $34.61$~dB respectively).

\begin{figure}[!htb]
\centering
\evalfig{figures/roi_panels_v2/fig_a_roi_comparison}
\caption[ROI comparison -- Normal, right mid-lung vascular margins]{%
  Fourteen-model ROI comparison at the right mid-lung vascular margin (Normal case, $\eta=200$, mid noise). \textit{Four-row layout:} rows~1--2 show deterministic/loss-substitution arms (Noisy $|$ Ref $|$ Arm~A $|$ Arm~I $|$ Arm~Q $|$ Arm~R $|$ Arm~S); rows~3--4 show NLL/VAE/baseline arms (Noisy $|$ Ref $|$ Arm~D $|$ Arm~H $|$ IRCNN $|$ SwinIR $|$ Arm~O). Within each group, odd rows show the full $256{\times}256$ field with ROI box and per-image PSNR; even rows show the $75{\times}75$ crop with red arrow at the primary vessel margin.
  Arms~A, I, Q, R, S all preserve vessel sharpness at comparable quality ($35.5$--$35.9$~dB at this image), confirming that loss-function variants within the deterministic family produce visually indistinguishable vessel margins. Arm~D recovers spectral content via FFL; Arm~H shows mild softening from KL regularisation. SwinIR shows residual attention-grid artefacts at vessel margins.}
\label{fig:results:roi:normal:vessels}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[!htb]
\centering
\evalfig{figures/roi_panels_v2/fig_b_roi_comparison}
\caption[ROI comparison -- Pneumonia, alveolar consolidation]{%
  Fourteen-model ROI comparison at the alveolar consolidation margin (Pneumonia case, $\eta=200$, mid noise). \textit{Four-row layout} as in Figure~\ref{fig:results:roi:normal:vessels}; crop region: $85{\times}75$ pixels centred on the primary consolidation front.
  Arms~A, I, Q, R, S achieve comparable consolidation boundary fidelity ($35.7$--$36.1$~dB at this image); pairwise differences are statistically non-significant across the full test set. Arm~D maintains the consolidation margin with additional FFL-driven spectral detail; Arm~H shows mild boundary softening from the KL bottleneck but retains clinically interpretable consolidation extent. IRCNN achieves comparable boundary fidelity to Arm~D ($d=-0.78$, $p_{\mathrm{Bonf}}=0.002$, n.s.\ after correction). Arm~O catastrophically blurs the consolidation boundary, removing diagnostic content entirely.}
\label{fig:results:roi:pneumonia:consolidation}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[!htb]
\centering
\evalfig{figures/roi_panels_v2/fig_c_roi_comparison}
\caption[ROI comparison -- Normal, right costophrenic angle]{%
  Fourteen-model ROI comparison at the right costophrenic angle (Normal case, $\eta=200$, mid noise). \textit{Four-row layout} as in Figure~\ref{fig:results:roi:normal:vessels}; crop region: $70{\times}65$ pixels covering the costophrenic recess and inferior pleural boundary.
  This site specifically probes FFL efficacy: the pleural edge response is sharpest in Arms~A, I, Q, R, S (all $\geq\!36.5$~dB at this image), with Arms~R and~S showing that loss-substitution fine-tuning from Arm~A preserves the pleural sharpness achieved by the deterministic reference arms. Arm~D's FFL component yields a sharp pleural line comparable to Arm~A; Arm~H shows mild softening. SwinIR and Arm~O both exhibit boundary blurring that would impair detection of small pleural effusions in a clinical setting.}
\label{fig:results:roi:normal:pleural}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[!htb]
\centering
\evalfig{figures/roi_panels_v2/fig_d_uncertainty_maps}
\caption[Aleatoric uncertainty maps -- Arms B, D, and H]{%
  Aleatoric uncertainty panel for Arms~B (NLL), D (NLL+SSIM+FFL), and~H (best VAE), for a Normal case (rows 1--2) and a Pneumonia case (rows 3--4; $\eta=200$).
  \textit{Columns:} Noisy input | Reference | Reconstruction ($\hat{\mu}$) | Aleatoric $\hat{\sigma}_a = \exp(0.5\,\hat{\ell}_{\sigma^2})$ (inferno colourmap, $[0, 0.30]$) | Absolute error $|\hat{\mu} - y|$ (hot colourmap) | ROI crop.
  The $\hat{\sigma}_a$ maps for all three arms preserve the Poisson-Gaussian noise structure: highest uncertainty in dark parenchymal regions (high quantum noise) and lowest in bright mediastinal structures (high photon count, low relative noise). The Pneumonia rows show elevated aleatoric uncertainty at consolidated margins for Arm~H, consistent with the VAE bottleneck's additional structural regularisation producing conservative uncertainty at pathological sites. Mean NLL values are annotated on each $\hat{\sigma}_a$ panel; Arms~B and~D are nearly identical (NLL $\approx -2.667$), confirming that the FFL and SSIM additions do not perturb calibration.}
\label{fig:results:roi:uncertainty}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[!htb]
\centering
\evalfig{figures/roi_panels_v2/fig_f_classical_vs_learned}
\caption[Classical vs learned ROI comparison -- BM3D, DnCNN, Arm A, Arm R]{%
  Classical-versus-learned ROI comparison at two anatomical sites (mid noise, $\eta=200$).
  \textbf{Top pair:} Right mid-lung vascular margins (Normal case).
  \textbf{Bottom pair:} Alveolar consolidation boundary (Pneumonia case).
  Each pair shows the full $256{\times}256$ field (top row, with red ROI box and per-image PSNR) and the zoomed crop (bottom row, red border).
  \textit{Columns:} Noisy $|$ Reference $|$ BM3D (oracle $\sigma\approx0.141$) $|$ DnCNN $|$ Arm~A ($\mathcal{L}_2$) $|$ Arm~R (FT: $\mathcal{L}_1$+SSIM+FFL).
  BM3D over-smooths fine vessel margins and consolidation boundaries despite oracle noise knowledge, confirming that the signal-dependent Poisson-Gaussian model requires learned priors.
  DnCNN recovers the broad signal but lacks the texture fidelity of the Hformer-based arms.
  Arm~R's FFL component produces the sharpest vessel margins and clearest consolidation edges of any model shown.}
\label{fig:results:roi:classical:learned}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Extended SOTA Qualitative Comparison: NAFNet and SCUNet}
\label{sec:results:recon:sota:roi}

Figures~\ref{fig:results:roi:sota:normal}--\ref{fig:results:roi:sota:costoph} present
nine-column ROI comparisons that include NAFNet \cite{chen_nafnet_2022} and
SCUNet \cite{zhang_scunet_2023} alongside PP-VAE-Hformer (Arm~A), DnCNN, IRCNN,
DRUNet, and SwinIR at mid noise ($\eta=200$).
Each panel shows a full $256{\times}256$ field (top row, with red ROI bounding box and
per-image PSNR annotation) and a zoomed crop at the ROI site (bottom row, red border).

At the right mid-lung vascular site (Figure~\ref{fig:results:roi:sota:normal}), SCUNet
achieves the highest PSNR in this panel by a narrow margin, but all three models
(Arm~A, NAFNet, SCUNet) recover the primary vessel margins with comparable fidelity;
SwinIR produces the most uniform background but shows slight smoothing of the thinnest
vessels. At the alveolar consolidation site (Figure~\ref{fig:results:roi:sota:pneumonia}),
NAFNet and SCUNet both preserve consolidation boundaries clearly, consistent with their
superior PSNR. At the costophrenic angle (Figure~\ref{fig:results:roi:sota:costoph}),
the pleural boundary rendering is sharp for all learned models and blunted for BM3D
(not shown here; see Figure~\ref{fig:results:roi:classical:learned}).
