\subsection{Performance as a Function of Degradation Severity}
\label{sec:results:recon:dosecurve}

All models degrade monotonically across noise presets (Figures~\ref{fig:results:dosecurve:psnr}--\ref{fig:results:dosecurve:ssim}). The mid-noise ranking is largely preserved at low and high noise, with two exceptions: Arm~O (PReLU failure) is last at all levels, and Arms~M and~E show a crossover at high noise. SwinIR trails CNN baselines by $\approx 2$~dB consistently. Arms~A, I, J form a high-fidelity cluster ($>36.4$~dB at low noise); VAE arms cluster $\approx 2$~dB below. The SSIM dose curve (Figure~\ref{fig:results:dosecurve:ssim}) is largely consistent with PSNR ranking; Arm~O shows the largest SSIM deficit at high noise, consistent with PReLU-induced structural incoherence.

\begin{figure}[!htb]
\centering
\evalfig{figures/evaluation_v2/dose_curve_psnr}
\caption[Reconstruction PSNR vs noise severity, all 21 models]{%
  PSNR (dB, mean\,$\pm$\,SD, $n=624$) as a function of noise severity for all 21 models.
  Solid lines: pixel-norm arms (A, I, J); dashed lines: VAE arms (E, F, G, H, P);
  dotted lines: retrained CNN baselines. Colour coding consistent with
  Figure~\ref{fig:results:ablation:psnr}. All models degrade monotonically;
  the relative ranking at mid noise is preserved at low and high noise
  with the exceptions noted in the text.}
\label{fig:results:dosecurve:psnr}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[!htb]
\centering
\evalfig{figures/evaluation_v2/dose_curve_ssim}
\caption[Reconstruction SSIM vs noise severity, all 21 models]{%
  SSIM as a function of noise severity (same colour/style scheme as
  Figure~\ref{fig:results:dosecurve:psnr}). The SSIM ranking is largely
  consistent with PSNR; Arm~O (PReLU) shows the largest relative SSIM deficit
  at high noise, suggesting that structural coherence is most severely impaired
  by PReLU-induced instability under high degradation.}
\label{fig:results:dosecurve:ssim}
\end{figure}

% ============================================================
\section{Charbonnier Loss Variant and Two-Stage Loss-Substitution Fine-Tuning}
\label{sec:results:curriculum}
% ============================================================

This section analyses Arms~Q, R, and~S, which extend the principal ablation through two complementary strategies: (i)~substituting the Charbonnier robust norm for L1/L2 (Arm~Q), and (ii)~two-stage loss-substitution fine-tuning in which a pretrained Arm~A checkpoint is refined with a richer composite loss objective (Arms~R and~S), motivated by Zhao et al.\ \cite{zhao_loss_2017}'s finding that composite losses produce more complex optimisation landscapes than MSE and benefit from a pixel-accurate initialisation.

\subsection{Arm Q: Charbonnier Reconstruction Loss}
\label{sec:results:curriculum:charb}

Arm~Q substitutes the Charbonnier penalty \cite{charbonnier_two_1994} ($\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{Charb}} = \sqrt{\|\hat{x}-x\|^2+\varepsilon^2}-\varepsilon$, $\varepsilon=10^{-3}$) for L2. It achieves $34.535$\,dB, bracketed between Arm~A ($34.618$) and Arm~I ($34.444$). All three pixel norms are statistically equivalent ($p>2.4\times10^{-4}$, $d<0.2$), confirming insensitivity of reconstruction performance to the choice of pixel-norm exponent at this noise magnitude \cite{zhao_loss_2017}.

\subsection{Arms R and S: Two-Stage Loss-Substitution Fine-Tuning}
\label{sec:results:curriculum:ft}

Arms~R and~S implement a two-stage loss-substitution strategy. In Stage~1, Arm~A trains to convergence on the L2 (MSE) objective, which has a single global minimum and is easy to navigate \cite{zhao_loss_2017}. In Stage~2, the converged weights are loaded and a richer composite loss is substituted: Arm~R switches to $\mathcal{L}_1$+SSIM+FFL (100 epochs, $\eta=5\times10^{-5}$) and Arm~S switches to NLL+SSIM+FFL (100 epochs, $\eta=2\times10^{-5}$). This approach is motivated by Zhao et al.\ \cite{zhao_loss_2017}, who showed that composite objectives produce more complex loss landscapes with harder-to-escape local minima: starting from the MSE minimum provides a pixel-accurate initialisation that constrains Stage~2 optimisation to a well-behaved neighbourhood of parameter space, rather than navigating the full composite landscape from random weights. For Arm~S, the transition is additionally stabilised by a 20-epoch linear loss blend ($\alpha \cdot \mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{NLL+SSIM+FFL}} + (1-\alpha) \cdot \mathcal{L}_2$, with $\alpha$ rising from 0 to 1), which prevents NLL divergence while the variance head adapts from its near-zero initialisation. Table~\ref{tab:results:curriculum:summary} summarises outcomes.

\begin{table}[htbp]
\centering
\caption[Two-stage loss-substitution fine-tuning results]{Two-stage loss-substitution fine-tuning results at mid noise ($\eta=200$, $n=624$). Arm~A is the Stage~1 (MSE) initialisation for both R and~S; Stage~2 substitutes a richer composite loss \cite{zhao_loss_2017}. $\Delta$PSNR and $\Delta$LPIPS are relative to Arm~A. LPIPS values from the \texttt{lpips} library (AlexNet); lower is better.}
\label{tab:results:curriculum:summary}
\small
\begin{tabular}{lcccc}
\toprule
\textbf{Arm} & \textbf{Objective} & \textbf{PSNR (dB)} & \textbf{LPIPS $\downarrow$} & \textbf{$\Delta$PSNR vs.~A} \\
\midrule
A  & L2 (Stage 1 base)      & $34.618 \pm 1.071$ & $0.1287$ & --- \\
R  & L1+SSIM+FFL (Stage 2)  & $34.545 \pm 1.074$ & $\mathbf{0.1226}$ & $-0.073$\,dB \\
S  & NLL+SSIM+FFL (Stage 2) & $34.608 \pm 1.076$ & $0.1296$ & $-0.010$\,dB \\
\midrule
J  & L1+SSIM+FFL (scratch)  & $34.319 \pm 1.060$ & $0.1268$ & $-0.299$\,dB \\
D  & NLL+SSIM+FFL (scratch) & $33.435 \pm 1.026$ & $0.1582$ & $-1.183$\,dB \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\linewidth]{figures/evaluation_v2/curriculum_loss_dynamics.pdf}
\caption[Two-stage loss-substitution training dynamics]{%
  Two-stage loss-substitution training dynamics, visualised in the style of \citet{zhao_loss_2017}.
  \textit{Top:} Validation PSNR over 300 epochs. Stage~1 (blue, epochs~0--199) shows Arm~A converging under $\mathcal{L}_2$,
  which has a simple loss landscape with a single global minimum \cite{zhao_loss_2017}.
  At epoch~200 (dashed red line) the loss is substituted: Arm~R (orange) continues fine-tuning under $\mathcal{L}_1$+SSIM+FFL
  and Arm~S (green) switches to NLL+SSIM+FFL.
  A brief PSNR dip is visible for Arm~S at epochs~200--205, attributable to the 20-epoch linear loss blend warmup
  as the variance head adapts from its near-zero initialisation.
  \textit{Bottom:} Training loss components (log scale). The MSE/$\mathcal{L}_2$ component for Arm~A and the
  total $\mathcal{L}_1$+SSIM+FFL loss for Arm~R are shown on the left axis; Arm~S's NLL component
  (right axis, dashed) decreases monotonically as calibration improves.
  The discontinuity at epoch~200 -- loss magnitude jumps while PSNR remains stable -- reflects the
  loss substitution: the objective changes but the pixel-fidelity initialisation is retained,
  confirming that Stage~1 MSE pre-training provides a stable starting point for navigating the
  more complex composite loss landscape \cite{zhao_loss_2017}.}
\label{fig:results:curriculum:loss}
\end{figure}

Figure~\ref{fig:results:curriculum:loss} shows the validation PSNR trajectories and training loss components for Arms~A, R, and~S throughout both stages. Arm~R achieves the best LPIPS ($0.1226$) and FSIM ($0.9332$) in the entire study at only $-0.073$\,dB PSNR vs.\ Arm~A, outperforming single-stage Arm~J by $+0.226$\,dB PSNR, $+0.0026$ FSIM, and lower LPIPS. Arm~S achieves $34.608$\,dB ($\Delta=-0.010$\,dB vs.\ Arm~A) with calibrated aleatoric uncertainty (mean NLL~$-2.650$, sharpness~$0.0201$), which is $1.17$\,dB better PSNR than single-stage Arm~D at comparable calibration. The key lesson, consistent with Zhao et al.\ \cite{zhao_loss_2017}: the Arm~A pixel-fidelity initialisation sets a quality ceiling that single-stage composite-loss training cannot reach from random weights, because the composite loss landscape is sufficiently complex that gradient descent stalls at inferior local minima; substituting the richer loss from the MSE minimum approaches that ceiling from multiple quality dimensions simultaneously. Visual inspection of representative ROI crops confirms preservation of vessel margins, consolidation boundaries, and pleural edges at parity with Arm~A.

\section{Latent Space Analysis}
\label{sec:results:latent}

t-Distributed Stochastic Neighbour Embedding (t-SNE) and Uniform Manifold Approximation and Projection (UMAP) projections of the five VAE arm latent codes (Adjusted Rand Index (ARI)\footnote{The \emph{Adjusted Rand Index} (ARI) measures the agreement between a predicted clustering and a known ground-truth labelling, corrected for chance. ARI~$=1.0$ means perfect agreement; ARI~$=0.0$ is expected performance of a random assignment; ARI~$<0$ means worse than random. In the latent space analysis here, $k$-means clustering ($k=3$) is applied to the latent codes and its output is compared against the ground-truth diagnostic labels (Normal/Bacterial/Viral). A high ARI indicates that the encoder places diagnostically distinct images in different latent regions, which would imply that the bottleneck has learned semantically meaningful structure.} and Silhouette Score\footnote{The \emph{Silhouette Score} measures how well each point fits its assigned cluster compared to neighbouring clusters: $s = (b-a)/\max(a,b)$, where $a$ is the mean intra-cluster distance and $b$ is the mean nearest-cluster distance. Values near $+1$ indicate tight, well-separated clusters; near $0$ indicates overlapping clusters; near $-1$ indicates misassignment. Here it complements ARI by assessing latent space compactness independently of the ground-truth label matching.} vs.\ ground-truth class labels) assess whether the PP-VAE bottleneck imposes diagnostically meaningful structure. Latent codes are the global-average-pooled posterior mean $\bar{z}\in\mathbb{R}^C$ for $n=448$ test images (150 Normal, 150 Bacterial, 148 Viral).

\subsection{t-SNE and UMAP Projections}
\label{sec:results:latent:viz}

No VAE arm produces strongly separated class clusters: the KL term explicitly penalises the encoder from reserving distinct latent regions for distinct classes. Within this constraint, KL schedule dominates separation: Arm~E (linear, near-collapse) gives ARI~$=-0.002$; Arm~F (cyclic only) gives ARI~$=0.333$ (best); Arm~G (free-bits) $0.048$; Arm~H (cyc+fb) $0.210$; Arm~P (PReLU) $0.096$, degraded by decoder instability. Projections for Arms~E, G, H, and~P are in Appendix~\ref{app:latent:suppfigs}.

Figure~\ref{fig:results:latent:arm_f} shows the representative case (Arm~F, ARI~$=0.333$, the highest of all five VAE arms), illustrating the moderate diagnostic structure achievable under a well-tuned cyclical KL schedule.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
\centering
\evalfig{figures/evaluation_v2/latent_space/latent_arm_f_kl_cyc}
\caption[Latent space projections -- Arm F (PP-VAE, cyclical KL, ARI = 0.333)]{%
  t-SNE (left) and UMAP (right) of Arm~F latent codes ($n=448$; 150/150/148 per class).
  Points coloured by ground-truth label: Normal (blue), Bacterial (red), Viral (green).
  ARI~$=0.333$, Silhouette~$=0.113$ — the highest diagnostic structure of all five VAE arms.
  The cyclical KL schedule periodically releases the posterior constraint, allowing
  the encoder to retain class-discriminative variation. Moderate cluster separation
  between Normal and Pneumonia is visible, though substantial overlap remains as expected
  under KL regularisation. Arms E, G, H, P are shown in Appendix~\ref{app:latent:suppfigs}.}
\label{fig:results:latent:arm_f}
\end{figure}

\clearpage
\subsection{Quantitative Latent Space Metrics}
\label{sec:results:latent:quant}

Table~\ref{tab:results:latent:quant} summarises ARI and Silhouette Score (both higher = more class-structured).

\begin{table}[htbp]
\centering
\caption[Latent space clustering metrics -- VAE arms]{%
  Quantitative latent space structure for five VAE arms. ARI = Adjusted Rand Index
  between $k$-means ($k=3$) and ground-truth Normal/Bacterial/Viral labels.
  Silhouette Score computed on ground-truth labels. $n=448$ images (150/150/148 per class).
  Higher is better for both metrics; ARI = 0 corresponds to chance-level clustering.}
\label{tab:results:latent:quant}
\begin{tabular}{lcccl}
\toprule
\textbf{Arm} & \textbf{KL Schedule} & \textbf{ARI} & \textbf{Silhouette} & \textbf{Interpretation} \\
\midrule
E & Linear (collapsed)       & $-0.002$ & $0.020$ & Near-posterior-collapse; uninformative latent \\
F & Cyclical only             & $\mathbf{0.333}$ & $\mathbf{0.113}$ & Best class separation; periodic KL release \\
G & Free bits only            & $0.048$ & $0.030$ & Modest recovery; KL floor compresses code \\
H & Cyclical + free bits      & $0.210$ & $0.073$ & Good separation; free bits partially offsets cyc benefit \\
P & Cyc+fb + PReLU (full)     & $0.096$ & $0.069$ & Partial degradation; PReLU instability disrupts latent \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\begin{tablenotes}\small
\item Computed from 448 test images (150 Normal / 150 Bacterial / 148 Viral).
  $k$-means ARI: $k=3$, random\_state=42. Latent vectors: global average pool of
  $\mu_z \in \mathbb{R}^{64 \times 32 \times 32} \to \mathbb{R}^{64}$.
\end{tablenotes}
\end{table}

Arm~F (cyclic only) achieves ARI~$=0.333$ -- by far the highest -- despite Arm~H achieving better PSNR ($32.762$ vs.\ $32.734$~dB). The free-bits floor in Arm~H suppresses the periodic KL releases that allow Arm~F's encoder to encode class-discriminative information, producing a reconstruction-separation trade-off. Arm~E (linear, collapsed) gives ARI~$=-0.002$; linear annealing is insufficient for this architecture.

Figure~\ref{fig:results:latent:ari} shows the ARI and Silhouette comparison across all five arms.

\begin{figure}[!t]
\centering
\evalfig[0.72\linewidth]{figures/evaluation_v2/latent_space/latent_ari_comparison}
\caption[Latent space ARI and Silhouette comparison across VAE arms]{%
  Adjusted Rand Index (purple) and Silhouette Score (green) for $k$-means clustering
  of the latent space of all five VAE arms ($k=3$, ground truth: Normal / Bacterial / Viral).
  Both metrics are expected to be low due to KL regularisation discouraging class-discriminative
  latent structure; the comparison across arms tests whether cyclical KL schedules
  produce more structured posteriors than linear annealing.}
\label{fig:results:latent:ari}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Interpretation}
\label{sec:results:latent:interp}

The latent analysis confirms: (i) no arm produces strongly class-discriminative posteriors, as expected from ELBO theory; (ii) cyclic KL (Arm~F) maximises class separation at the cost of $0.028$~dB PSNR -- the practical choice depends on whether retrieval or reconstruction is the primary objective; (iii) PReLU instability (Arm~P) degrades latent structure alongside reconstruction quality, confirming failure on both axes.

\section{Calibration and Uncertainty Analysis}
\label{sec:results:calibration}

\subsection{Pixel-Level Uncertainty Maps}
\label{sec:results:calibration:maps}

NLL-trained arms (B--D, F--H, K--N, O, P) produce $\hat{\sigma}_a(x)=\exp(0.5\,\hat{\ell}_{\sigma^2})$ at each pixel; deterministic arms and baselines produce no uncertainty estimate. Figures~\ref{fig:results:uncertainty:arm_b}, \ref{fig:results:uncertainty:arm_d}, and~\ref{fig:results:uncertainty:arm_h} show uncertainty maps for Arms~B, D, and~H respectively on Bacterial Pneumonia cases. Three anatomically consistent patterns appear across all arms: highest $\hat{\sigma}_a$ at dark lung parenchyma (high quantum noise), near-zero at bright mediastinal structures, and elevated at consolidation margins (genuinely ambiguous boundary pixels).

\begin{figure}[!htb]
\centering
\evalfig{figures/evaluation_v2/uncertainty_arm_b_nll}
\caption[Pixel-level uncertainty maps -- Arm B (NLL)]{%
  Arm~B (NLL) uncertainty maps for four representative Bacterial Pneumonia cases ($\eta=200$).
  \textit{Columns:} Noisy input | Reference | $\hat{\mu}$ | $\hat{\sigma}_a$ (inferno) | $|\hat{\mu}-y|$ (hot).
  The $\hat{\sigma}_a$ map concentrates on dark parenchymal regions and at consolidation margins,
  consistent with the Poisson-Gaussian noise model. The NLL alone (without structural regularisation)
  produces smooth uncertainty maps with limited fine spatial structure.}
\label{fig:results:uncertainty:arm_b}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[!htb]
\centering
\evalfig{figures/evaluation_v2/uncertainty_arm_d_nll_ssim_ffl}
\caption[Pixel-level uncertainty maps -- Arm D (NLL+SSIM+FFL)]{%
  Arm~D (NLL+SSIM+FFL) uncertainty maps for the same four cases as
  Figure~\ref{fig:results:uncertainty:arm_b}. Adding SSIM and FFL does not
  degrade uncertainty calibration (NLL $\approx -2.667$, identical to Arm~B);
  the $\hat{\sigma}_a$ maps are qualitatively similar but reconstruction error
  maps ($|\hat{\mu}-y|$) show lower peak values, reflecting the improved
  spectral fidelity from FFL.}
\label{fig:results:uncertainty:arm_d}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[!htb]
\centering
\evalfig{figures/evaluation_v2/uncertainty_arm_h_kl_cyc_fb}
\caption[Pixel-level uncertainty maps -- Arm H (PP-VAE, best VAE)]{%
  Arm~H (VAE, cyclical KL + free bits) uncertainty maps. The VAE bottleneck
  introduces epistemic uncertainty via stochastic latent sampling; the $\hat{\sigma}_a$
  displayed here is the aleatoric component only. Arm~H shows marginally higher
  uncertainty at consolidation margins relative to Arms~B and~D, consistent with
  the KL bottleneck compressing reconstruction-relevant information and widening
  the predictive distribution at ambiguous boundaries. This behaviour is clinically
  desirable: higher uncertainty at pathological margins flags precisely the regions
  where radiologist attention is most needed.}
\label{fig:results:uncertainty:arm_h}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Metrics}
\label{sec:results:calibration:ece}
Arms~B and~D achieve NLL~$\approx -2.667$ nats/pixel at mid noise; Arm~H pays a small calibration cost (NLL~$\approx -2.470$) due to the VAE bottleneck. Sharpness is $0.0198$ (B/D), $0.0214$ (H), and $0.0201$ (Arm~S via loss-substitution Stage~2). Figure~\ref{fig:results:calibration:reliability} shows image-level reliability diagrams: Pearson $r\approx0.8$--$0.9$ across NLL arms confirms that $\hat{\sigma}_a$ is a reliable signal for reconstruction difficulty.

\begin{figure}[http]
\centering
\evalfig{figures/evaluation_v2/dissertation_figs/calibration_reliability_v2}
\caption[Uncertainty calibration reliability diagrams for NLL and VAE arms]{%
  Image-level reliability diagrams at mid noise ($\eta=200$, $n=624$).
  Each panel shows one NLL/VAE arm: images are binned by predicted aleatoric uncertainty $\hat{\sigma}_a$ (sharpness; $x$-axis), and the mean actual RMSE in each bin is plotted on the $y$-axis.
  The dashed line indicates perfect calibration ($\hat{\sigma}_a = \text{RMSE}$).
  Pearson $r$ (inset) quantifies the sharpness--error correlation: $r > 0$ confirms that higher predicted uncertainty corresponds to genuinely harder images.
  Arm~S (two-stage FT from Arm~A with NLL+SSIM+FFL objective) achieves calibration comparable to Arm~D despite the two-stage training.}
\label{fig:results:calibration:reliability}
\end{figure}


\section{Ablation Study Results}
\label{sec:results:ablation}
Figures~\ref{fig:results:ablation:psnr} and~\ref{fig:results:ablation:ssim} summarise the incremental effect of each loss term as horizontal bar charts, with models sorted by PSNR. Colour coding indicates arm type: blue (pixel-norm: L\textsubscript{2}, L\textsubscript{1}), orange (NLL arms), purple (VAE arms), green (retrained baselines). Table~\ref{tab:results:ablation:full} provides full numerical results across all three noise levels.

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\evalfig{figures/evaluation_v2/ablation_barchart_v2}
\caption[Ablation study PSNR -- horizontal bar chart]{%
  PSNR (dB, mean\,$\pm$\,SD, $n=624$) at mid noise ($\eta=200$) for all 21 models,
  sorted ascending by PSNR. Colours indicate arm type (pixel-norm / NLL / VAE / baseline).
  The pixel-norm cluster (Arm~A, I, J; blue) occupies the top tier.
  NLL arms (orange) trade $\sim$1.2~dB PSNR for calibrated uncertainty.
  The best VAE arm (H; purple) is statistically indistinguishable from IRCNN (green).
  Arm~O (PReLU; orange) is the clear outlier at $29.07$~dB.}
\label{fig:results:ablation:psnr}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\evalfig{figures/evaluation_v2/ablation_barchart_ssim}
\caption[Ablation study SSIM -- horizontal bar chart]{%
  SSIM at mid noise for all 21 models (same ordering and colour scheme as
  Figure~\ref{fig:results:ablation:psnr}). The SSIM ranking mirrors PSNR
  with one notable difference: FFDNet and DRUNet rank marginally higher on SSIM
  than on PSNR, consistent with their sigma-conditioned architecture providing
  better structural coherence at mid-noise magnitudes.}
\label{fig:results:ablation:ssim}
\end{figure}

\section{Sensitivity Analysis}
\label{sec:results:sensitivity}

\subsection{Sensitivity to Loss Weights}
\label{sec:results:sensitivity:weights}
Loss weight sensitivity was not systematically searched due to computational constraints ($\sim$10~h/arm on A100). The cross-arm ablation (Arms~B, D, L, N) indirectly probes sensitivity: SSIM+FFL are complementary; SSIM+Edge+Perceptual causes gradient interference (Arms~M, N). Full sensitivity surface estimation is identified as future work.

\subsection{Sensitivity to Latent Dimension}
\label{sec:results:sensitivity:latent}
The latent dimension $C_z=64$ was fixed to match the Hformer bottleneck for architectural consistency between VAE and non-VAE arms. Systematic variation of $C_z$ is identified as future work.

\subsection{Sensitivity to Degradation Severity}
\label{sec:results:sensitivity:dose}
Model rank ordering is preserved across all noise levels (Spearman $r_s>0.98$ between Low and High noise rankings). The NLL-vs-pixel-norm gap narrows slightly at high noise ($1.02$~dB at Low vs.\ $0.77$~dB at High), consistent with the calibration cost being proportionally smaller when SNR is already severely degraded.

\section{Subgroup Analysis: Normal, Bacterial, and Viral Pneumonia}
\label{sec:results:subgroup}

The test set comprises 234 Normal, 242 Bacterial, and 148 Viral Pneumonia cases. Bacterial disease (lobar consolidation) produces stronger local contrast gradients than viral disease (bilateral interstitial pattern), directly affecting per-arm PSNR.

Table~\ref{tab:results:subgroup:combined} stratifies PSNR by class at mid noise. The Pneumonia-over-Normal advantage ($\Delta\approx+1.0$~dB) is consistent across all 21 arms, confirming a data-domain property. Arm~O's smaller gap ($+0.75$~dB) reflects indiscriminate suppression from PReLU divergence. Bacterial cases consistently outperform Viral ($\Delta_{B-V}\approx+0.5$--$0.6$~dB; Table~\ref{tab:results:subgroup:viral}), with lobar consolidation providing more distinct reconstruction targets than diffuse interstitial haziness.

\begin{landscape}
\singlespacing
\footnotesize
\setlength{\LTcapwidth}{\linewidth}
\begin{longtable}{p{2.4cm}p{7.0cm}ccc}

\caption[Reconstruction PSNR by diagnostic class -- all 21 models]{%
  Reconstruction PSNR (dB) stratified by diagnostic class at mid noise level ($\eta=200$, mean\,$\pm$\,SD).
  All models evaluated on $n_{\mathrm{Norm}}=234$, $n_{\mathrm{Pneu}}=390$ test images.
  $\Delta$ = Pneumonia $-$ Normal PSNR\,dB. All differences significant at $p<0.001$
  (independent-samples $t$-test, Bonferroni-corrected).
  Data computed from \texttt{per\_image\_metrics.csv} (job 30793687).}
\label{tab:results:subgroup:combined}\\

Figure~\ref{fig:results:subgroup:all_arms} presents a quantitative summary of the Pneumonia-over-Normal advantage across all 19 ablation arms, confirming that Arms~Q, R, and~S follow the same subgroup pattern as their parent arms. The loss-substitution fine-tuned arms (R and~S, both initialised from Arm~A) show subgroup deltas ($\Delta \approx +0.92$~dB) consistent with their parent arms, confirming the subgroup advantage is a data-domain property independent of fine-tuning strategy.

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\evalfig{figures/evaluation_v2/dissertation_figs/subgroup_psnr_all_arms}
\caption[Subgroup PSNR by diagnostic class -- all 19 ablation arms]{%
  Reconstruction PSNR (dB) stratified by diagnostic class at mid noise ($\eta=200$, $n=624$).
  Blue bars: Normal ($n=234$); red bars: Pneumonia ($n=390$).
  Arms~Q, R, and~S (two-stage FT group) are included alongside all 16 principal ablation arms and DnCNN.
  Error bars: standard error of the mean. Shaded background bands indicate arm groups (Deterministic\,/\,FT, NLL/Composite, VAE/KL, DnCNN).
  The consistent Pneumonia\,$>$\,Normal advantage across all arms confirms a data-domain effect independent of training objective.}
\label{fig:results:subgroup:all_arms}
\end{figure}

Figures~\ref{fig:results:subgroup:normal}, \ref{fig:results:subgroup:bacterial}, and~\ref{fig:results:subgroup:viral} present qualitative comparison panels for Normal, Bacterial, and Viral subgroups respectively. Each panel shows four arms (Arm~A, Arm~D, Arm~H, IRCNN) across three representative cases from the respective subgroup. PSNR is annotated per cell. The key qualitative finding is that Arm~D and IRCNN produce visually indistinguishable reconstructions in Normal cases, while in Bacterial cases Arm~D shows marginally crisper consolidation margins. In Viral cases, all four arms struggle to preserve the subtle interstitial pattern without over-smoothing the fine bilateral haziness, with Arm~H (VAE) showing the most conservative preservation at the cost of slightly elevated NLL.

\end{tabular}
\end{table}

The PSNR rank ordering is largely preserved under both held-out metrics. DDPM (FSIM~$=0.804$, LPIPS~$=0.407$) falls below all models including BM3D, confirming insufficient convergence. BM3D trails all learned discriminative models ($\Delta\mathrm{FSIM}\geq0.015$), confirming that the Poisson-Gaussian model falls outside AWGN assumptions. Pixel-norm arms dominate (FSIM~$\in[0.9296,0.9317]$); NLL arms lie $\approx0.016$ FSIM below, consistent with the 1.2~dB PSNR gap. DRUNet achieves FSIM~$=0.9212$ despite PSNR~$32.17$~dB, suggesting its dilated architecture preserves structural frequency content efficiently. Arm~O (FSIM~$0.8757$, LPIPS~$0.4397$) is the worst arm.

Arm~R achieves the highest FSIM ($0.9332$) and lowest LPIPS ($0.2192$) in the study, surpassing Arm~A on both held-out metrics despite a $0.073$~dB PSNR deficit. Arm~J (same objective, single-stage) achieves FSIM~$0.9306$ / LPIPS~$0.2268$ -- inferior on both metrics while also losing $0.30$~dB PSNR -- confirming that loss-substitution fine-tuning resolves the perception-distortion tension that afflicts single-stage training.

\section{Error Analysis and Failure Cases}
\label{sec:results:error}

Three failure modes were identified.

\paragraph{PReLU decoder instability (Arm O).}
PReLU's learnable negative slope allowed the log-variance head to diverge towards large positive values early in training, propagating through the NLL loss and collapsing reconstruction to $29.07$~dB ($d=+5.58$ vs.\ Arm~A, $p\approx0$). Best checkpoint at epoch~12 vs.\ typical convergence at epoch~60--80. PReLU is not a safe replacement for ReLU in heteroscedastic decoder architectures.

\paragraph{VAE posterior collapse (Arm E).}
Linear KL warmup led to posterior collapse at epoch~$\approx120$ ($D_\mathrm{KL}<0.01$ nats), reducing the model to a deterministic decoder ($31.64$~dB, $-0.38$~dB vs.\ cyclical arms). The aleatoric head remained approximately calibrated (NLL~$\approx-2.42$) but epistemic maps lost meaningful structure, motivating cyclical annealing in Arms~F--H.

\paragraph{Loss objective interference (Arms M and N).}
SSIM and Sobel-edge supervision both pressure spatial gradients via conflicting pathways, preventing simultaneous satisfaction at high-contrast boundaries. Arm~M achieves $31.69$~dB ($-2.93$~dB vs.\ Arm~A), the second-largest deficit in the study.

\paragraph{Systematic spatial bias.}
Reconstruction error concentrates at anatomical boundaries (cardiac silhouette, diaphragm, consolidation edges) in all non-failure arms, consistent with the Poisson noise model assigning higher variance to dark boundary pixels. NLL-trained arms correctly flag these as highest-uncertainty regions.

\section{Summary of Key Findings}
\label{sec:results:summary}

\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{Reconstruction (RO1).} Among ablation arms, Arm~A leads PSNR ($34.62$~dB); among all models in the study, SCUNet achieves the highest PSNR ($34.73$~dB). Arm~R leads perceptual quality (FSIM~$0.9332$, LPIPS~$0.2192$); Arm~D achieves the best uncertainty-reconstruction trade-off ($33.44$~dB, calibrated NLL~$-2.67$).
\item \textbf{PP-VAE cost (RO2).} Arm~H ($32.76$~dB, $-1.86$~dB vs.\ Arm~A, $d=+1.14$, $p<0.001$) uniquely provides calibrated aleatoric and epistemic uncertainty at statistical parity with the best CNN baselines.
\item \textbf{Uncertainty calibration (RO3).} NLL arms achieve mean NLL~$\approx-2.67$ nats/pixel, with aleatoric uncertainty correctly concentrated at consolidation boundaries, cardiac silhouette, and diaphragm.
\item \textbf{KL annealing (RO4).} Linear KL (Arm~E) collapses at epoch~$\approx120$. Cyclic+free-bits (Arm~H) is the recommended default for spatial VAE architectures ($+1.13$~dB over Arm~E).
\item \textbf{Loss interaction (RO5).} L1/L2 are equivalent ($d=+0.16$, n.s.); SSIM+FFL are complementary; simultaneous SSIM+Edge (Arms~M, N) causes gradient interference ($-1.4$--$-1.8$~dB vs.\ Arm~D).
\item \textbf{Subgroup (RO6).} Bacterial~$>$~Viral~$>$~Normal PSNR ($\Delta\approx+1.0/+0.5$~dB) is a consistent data-domain effect; rank ordering is preserved across all arms.
\item \textbf{Baselines (RO7).} All five retrained KAIR baselines ($31.18$--$32.80$~dB) are outperformed by Arm~A ($+1.82$~dB above DnCNN); SwinIR trails all CNN baselines. Among the extended SOTA baselines, NAFNet ($34.39$~dB) is competitive with but below Arm~A, while SCUNet ($34.73$~dB) achieves the highest PSNR in the study, $+0.11$~dB above Arm~A, highlighting the reconstruction ceiling set by the current architectural SOTA when retrained on domain-matched data.
\item \textbf{Failure modes (RO8).} PReLU instability (Arm~O, $-5.54$~dB), posterior collapse (Arm~E, $-3.0$~dB), and loss interference (Arms~M/N, $-1.4$--$-2.9$~dB) are quantified in Section~\ref{sec:results:error}.
\end{enumerate}

These eight empirical findings are consistent and mutually reinforcing: the composite loss framework outperforms any single-term baseline, calibrated uncertainty is obtainable at no PSNR cost relative to the best CNN baselines, and three distinct failure modes reveal principled design constraints for future probabilistic denoisers. Chapter~\ref{chap:discussion} interprets these findings in the context of prior literature and the clinical mandate, asking not only what the numbers show but what they require of any system that seeks to deploy uncertainty-aware AI safely in paediatric imaging.

% ============================================================
% CHAPTER 5 — DISCUSSION
% ============================================================
\chapter{Discussion}
\label{chap:discussion}

The preceding chapter reported what the numbers are. This chapter asks what they mean -- for loss function design, for the practical value of probabilistic denoising, and for the clinical deployment of uncertainty-aware AI in paediatric radiology. The discussion is structured around the research objectives stated in Chapter~\ref{chap:intro}: first interpreting reconstruction performance and the role of each loss term, then addressing latent space geometry and KL schedule behaviour, then examining uncertainty calibration, and finally situating the findings relative to prior literature and the clinical mandate. Limitations and generalisability are addressed explicitly so that the boundaries of inference are clear.

\section{Interpretation of Principal Findings}
\label{sec:disc:findings}

\subsection{Reconstruction Performance and the PSNR-SSIM Trade-Off}
\label{sec:disc:findings:psnr}

At mid noise, PSNR and FSIM rankings are largely aligned: Arm~A leads both metrics ($34.618$~dB; FSIM~$0.9317$), suggesting that at this noise magnitude the perception-distortion tradeoff \cite{blau_perception_2018} is not yet binding. The NLL arms (B, D: $\approx 33.4$~dB) are $\approx 1.2$~dB below Arm~A, reflecting the capacity divided between estimating mean and variance; the cost purchases calibrated per-pixel aleatoric uncertainty unavailable from MSE architectures. The VAE arms (F, H: $32.7$--$32.8$~dB) lie a further $\approx 0.7$~dB below but remain in statistical parity with the best CNN baselines (IRCNN: $32.659$~dB, $d=-0.101$, n.s.), confirming that epistemic uncertainty is obtained at no PSNR sacrifice relative to comparable deterministic networks.

\subsection{The Role of Each Constraint}
\label{sec:disc:findings:constraints}

\textbf{Pixel norm.} L1 and L2 are statistically equivalent ($d=+0.164$, n.s.); Charbonnier is expected to be similarly equivalent. Under Poisson-Gaussian noise with approximately Gaussian pixel marginals, the Bayes-optimal estimator under L1 and L2 is the same posterior mean.

\textbf{SSIM and FFL as complementary spectral constraints.} Adding SSIM to NLL (Arm~C) causes a $-1.1$~dB loss attributable to spectral bias: SSIM penalises patch-level structure mismatches, suppressing the high-frequency learning signal. FFL (Arm~D) fully recovers this loss ($d=+0.020$, n.s.\ vs Arm~B) by correcting high-frequency errors in the Fourier domain, while also improving LPIPS ($0.1582$ vs $0.1595$). The SSIM+FFL combination is the correct design choice: neither alone is sufficient.

\textbf{Edge loss gradient interference.} Arm~M (NLL+SSIM+Edge+FFL) collapses $-1.747$~dB below Arm~D ($d=+1.754$, $p\approx 0$). SSIM and Sobel supervision both pressure local spatial gradients via distinct pathways, producing destructive gradient interference when applied simultaneously. Edge supervision should not be combined with SSIM in the composite loss.

\textbf{VGG perceptual loss.} Arm~N ties Arm~C statistically ($d=-0.065$, n.s.) with marginal LPIPS benefit. Domain transfer from ImageNet RGB to greyscale CXR is insufficient to capture anatomically relevant structures; a CXR-pretrained perceptual loss is required for larger gains.

\textbf{PReLU failure.} Arm~O collapses $-5.544$~dB ($d=+5.577$, $p\approx 0$) due to PReLU's learnable negative slope producing NLL gradient overflow in the variance head. The remedy is to constrain slopes to $[0,1]$ or replace PReLU with GELU in variance-producing layers.

\subsection{Latent Space Structure and the KL Schedule}
\label{sec:disc:findings:latent}

Arm~F (cyclic KL) achieves the highest latent class separation (ARI~$=0.333$) at a small PSNR cost ($32.734$ vs.\ $32.762$~dB for Arm~H), confirming the tension between KL collapse prevention and class-discriminative latent content. The free-bits floor in Arm~H continuously suppresses the periodic latent-code release that cyclic annealing provides, stabilising reconstruction but reducing class separation (ARI~$=0.210$). Arms~F and~H also achieve the best calibration (NLL $= -2.538$ and $-2.556$~nats/px respectively), consistent with Fu et al.\ \cite{fu_cyclical_2019}: cyclic schedules prevent posterior collapse, maintaining informativeness of $\hat{\sigma}^2_e$. If downstream tasks require class-separable latent codes, Arm~F is preferable; for reconstruction quality, Arm~H.

\subsection{Uncertainty Calibration}
\label{sec:disc:findings:calibration}

NLL-trained arms produce physically meaningful aleatoric maps: uncertainty concentrates in dark anatomical regions (pleural angles, retrocardiac spaces) where the Foi Poisson term $ay$ is lowest, exactly matching the model's prediction (Figures~\ref{fig:results:uncertainty:arm_b}--\ref{fig:results:uncertainty:arm_h}). Failure arms (E, P) produce spatially uniform elevated variance that provides no clinical guidance. The best-calibrated arms (F: NLL $=-2.538$; H: NLL $=-2.556$) achieve this at a PSNR cost of only $\approx 0.7$~dB relative to NLL deterministic arms and at statistical parity with the best CNN baselines: regions where $\hat{\sigma}_a(i)>\tau$ can be flagged for review or repeat acquisition, operationalising ALARA at the inference stage.

\section{Comparison with Prior Literature}
\label{sec:disc:lit}

Fair comparisons use the same Kermany corpus and Poisson-Gaussian noise model. BM3D with oracle $\sigma$ achieves $30.716$~dB, trailing every learned arm by $\geq 1.9$~dB, confirming that signal-dependent heteroscedastic noise falls outside AWGN assumptions. DnCNN retrained achieves $32.798$~dB; NLL arms B and D exceed this by $+0.66$~dB and $+0.64$~dB respectively ($d\approx0.65$) while simultaneously providing calibrated per-pixel uncertainty maps. Arm~A ($34.618$~dB) exceeds DnCNN by $+1.82$~dB attributable to Hformer's attention-based long-range modelling. The extended SOTA baselines (Section~\ref{sec:methods:baselines}) further contextualise these results: NAFNet \cite{chen_nafnet_2022} achieves $34.388$~dB, competitive with but below Arm~A; SCUNet \cite{zhang_scunet_2023} achieves $34.726$~dB, $+0.11$~dB above Arm~A, representing the ceiling of current discriminative SOTA when retrained on domain-matched data. Crucially, neither NAFNet nor SCUNet provides calibrated uncertainty output, and their training scripts exhibited a metric-normalisation error that precluded direct LPIPS and FSIM comparison. DDPM ($13.52$~dB) is an honest negative finding: 200 epochs is insufficient for Palette-style diffusion to converge; the trajectory was still monotonically rising at epoch~200, demonstrating that discriminative architectures are far more budget-efficient.

Contextually (Table~\ref{tab:disc:lit}), Arm~A exceeds RED-CNN on clinical CT ($33.11$~dB \cite{chen_low_2017}). The closest architectural prior is Xu et al.\ \cite{xuLowdoseComputedTomography2025} (RDN+Transformer for LDCT), which is fully deterministic and produces no uncertainty output. To the authors' knowledge, PP-VAE-Hformer is the first architecture to combine hybrid attention reconstruction with calibrated per-pixel epistemic and aleatoric uncertainty maps on paediatric CXR; however, the rapidly evolving literature means this claim should be interpreted as reflecting the state of knowledge at the time of writing rather than as an exhaustive survey of unpublished concurrent work.

\section{Theoretical Significance}
\label{sec:disc:theory}

\textbf{Loss engineering vs architecture scaling.} The three CNN baselines cluster within $0.076$~dB on the Kermany test set despite representing a decade of progressive architectural innovation. On this dataset and noise model, switching the loss alone from DnCNN to NLL (Arm~B) adds $+0.658$~dB without architecture change, consistent with Zhao et al.\ \cite{zhao_loss_2017}: this suggests loss function design may be an underexploited lever in this domain, though the effect size should be interpreted cautiously given the single-dataset evaluation. The composite objective can be interpreted as a constrained ELBO where each term encodes a distinct domain prior (Gaussian noise physics, latent smoothness, structural coherence, spectral completeness); the ablation validates each prior by showing that removing it degrades performance along the dimension it encodes. The NLL+SSIM+FFL objective is architecture-agnostic and transferable to any heteroscedastic encoder-decoder.

\section{Clinical Relevance}
\label{sec:disc:clinical}

\textbf{Dose reduction.} At high noise ($\eta=300$, $\approx 3\times$ reduced dose), Arm~A maintains $32.9$~dB, matching IRCNN at mid noise. This is consistent with the possibility that $\approx1.5\times$ dose reduction may be achievable on this dataset without quality sacrifice by this specific metric, though translating this into a clinical dose reduction claim would require prospective human observer studies with confirmed diagnostic accuracy outcomes, which are outside the scope of this work. VAE arms~F/H maintain $\geq 30.7$~dB while retaining calibrated uncertainty maps. Pneumonia cases are reconstructed $\approx 1.0$~dB better than Normal cases -- the population most frequently scanned at low dose benefits most.

\textbf{Uncertainty workflow.} A simple three-tier decision rule operates on $\hat{\sigma}_a$: below $\tau_1$ accept; between $\tau_1$ and $\tau_2$ flag for second reader; above $\tau_2$ request repeat acquisition. Threshold calibration requires a reader study with confirmed diagnostic outcomes. Arm~D (NLL+SSIM+FFL, no VAE) is the most deployment-friendly configuration at $<50$~ms inference per image.

\section{Strengths of This Study}
\label{sec:disc:strengths}

This study's principal strengths are: (i) the first systematic 16-arm composite loss ablation for probabilistic paediatric CXR denoising; (ii) 210 Bonferroni-corrected pairwise comparisons with Cohen's $d$ effect sizes; (iii) all five baselines retrained on the same data split to eliminate domain-transfer confounds; (iv) multi-metric evaluation (PSNR, SSIM, LPIPS, FSIM, NLL) across three noise levels; and (v) a physically grounded Poisson-Gaussian noise model calibrated to clinical paediatric dose conditions.

\section{Limitations}
\label{sec:disc:limitations}

Key limitations are: (i) simulated degradation (no structured detector artefacts, motion blur, or scatter); (ii) single-centre Kermany dataset with limited demographic variability -- generalisation to MIMIC-CXR or CheXpert is unestablished; (iii) no radiologist reader study to validate that uncertainty maps improve diagnostic decision-making; (iv) binary pathology labels only; (v) KL regularisation inherently suppresses class-discriminative latent content (best ARI~$0.333$, Arm~F); (vi) loss weight sensitivity not exhaustively searched.

\paragraph{Metric limitations.} PSNR and SSIM optimise for pixel-level fidelity and may not correlate with diagnostic accuracy. Zhang et al.\ \cite{zhang_comprehensive_2012} show that PSNR has the lowest correlation ($\rho=0.69$) with human perceptual judgement of the eleven full-reference metrics they evaluated. A reconstructed image that scores 35\,dB PSNR may be diagnostically inferior to one scoring 33\,dB if the former suppresses consolidation margins while the latter preserves them. No radiologist reader study was performed in this dissertation to establish whether PSNR and SSIM gains translate to improved diagnostic confidence or lesion detection sensitivity. FSIM is adopted as the primary perceptual metric (Section~\ref{sec:lit:iqametrics}) in partial mitigation, but the absence of a clinical ground truth remains the fundamental limitation of any quantitative image quality comparison study.

\paragraph{Hyperparameter sensitivity.} Loss weights $\lambda_{\mathrm{SSIM}} = 0.5$, $\lambda_{\mathrm{FFL}} = 0.1$, and $\lambda_{\mathrm{edge}} = 0.1$ were fixed by pilot experiments on a coarse grid rather than a systematic search. The catastrophic gradient interference observed in Arm~M (NLL+SSIM+Edge+FFL, $-1.747$\,dB vs.\ Arm~D) suggests that the optimal $\lambda_{\mathrm{edge}}$ in the presence of SSIM is substantially lower than $0.1$, and that a finer search over $(\lambda_{\mathrm{SSIM}}, \lambda_{\mathrm{edge}})$ could recover additional PSNR for Arms~M and~N without retraining from scratch. Similarly, the KL weight ceiling $\beta_{\max} = 10^{-3}$ and the free-bits threshold $\lambda_{\mathrm{fb}} = 0.25$ for VAE arms were set by reference to the literature rather than data-driven search; data-adaptive tuning may further improve calibration for clinical deployment.

\section{Potential Sources of Bias}
\label{sec:disc:bias}

The 62.5\%/37.5\% Pneumonia/Normal split inflates aggregate PSNR by $\approx 0.4$~dB (Pneumonia reconstructs $\approx 1.0$~dB better), though comparative rankings are unaffected. The clean reference is JPEG-compressed (not raw DICOM), so models learn to reproduce JPEG artefacts. Metrics were pre-registered; NLL metrics structurally favour NLL-trained arms over MSE baselines, which is acknowledged.

\section{Generalisability}
\label{sec:disc:generalisability}

The composite loss framework is architecture-agnostic, requiring only a (mean, log-variance) output decoder. It is applicable to any encoder-decoder for medical image reconstruction (CT, MRI, ultrasound). The Gaussian NLL is accurate for Poisson-Gaussian noise at moderate photon counts but requires Anscombe-transform adaptation for CT sinograms. Full-resolution CXR ($\geq 1024\times1024$) would require hierarchical windowed attention to manage memory overhead.

\section{Future Research Directions}
\label{sec:disc:future}

Ranked by clinical impact:

\begin{enumerate}
  \item \textbf{Radiologist reader study.} A blinded reader study (diagnostic confidence + lesion detection on Arms~A, D, H with uncertainty maps) is the only pathway to establish clinical utility and is prerequisite for regulatory submission.

  \item \textbf{Uncertainty hotspot aggregation.} Per-pixel $\hat{\sigma}_a$ maps can be thresholded and attributed to anatomical lobar zones (RUL/RML/RLL/LUL/LLL/Mediastinum) to generate actionable per-scan reports; Figure~\ref{fig:results:uncertainty:hotspot} demonstrates a prototype implementation on Arm~S. Calibrating severity thresholds against confirmed pathology labels is the immediate next step.

  \item \textbf{Full-resolution DICOM validation.} Real low-dose acquisitions ($\geq1024\times1024$) from clinical PACS (e.g., MIMIC-CXR-JPG \cite{johnson_mimic_2019}) are required for deployment credibility.

  \item \textbf{Disentangled VAE for class separation.} KL regularisation suppresses class-discriminative latent content (best ARI~$0.333$). $\beta$-TCVAE or a jointly trained latent classifier head would recover class separability without sacrificing reconstruction.

  \item \textbf{Domain-adapted perceptual loss.} Arm~N confirms VGG-16 features transfer poorly from ImageNet to greyscale CXR. A CheXpert-pretrained feature extractor would yield larger perceptual gains.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
  \centering
  \evalfig{figures/evaluation_v2/fig_uncertainty_hotspot}
  \caption[Uncertainty-guided clinical hotspot detection -- Arm S (NLL two-stage FT)]{%
    \textbf{Uncertainty-guided clinical hotspot detection across three diagnostic groups (Arm~S, NLL two-stage FT).}
    Each row corresponds to one representative test case; each column shows a distinct view of the same image.
    \textbf{(a) Noisy input:} Poisson--Gaussian corrupted chest radiograph ($\alpha{=}0.03$, $\beta{=}0.005$).
    \textbf{(b) Reference (clean):} Ground-truth uncorrupted image, included so the reader can anchor reconstruction quality and identify structures in the denoised output.
    \textbf{(c) Denoised $\hat{\mu}$:} Mean prediction from Arm~S; the model recovers fine vascular and parenchymal detail across all three groups.
    \textbf{(d) Uncertainty proxy:} Gaussian-smoothed reconstruction residual $|\text{noisy} - \hat{\mu}|$ ($\sigma{=}3$\,px), displayed on the inferno colourmap (brighter $=$ higher uncertainty); this is the spatial signal a perfectly calibrated heteroscedastic $\hat{\sigma}_a$ would approximate.
    \textbf{(e) Hotspot overlay:} Denoised image with faint anatomical zone grid (RUL/RML/RLL $=$ blue tones; LUL/LLL $=$ red tones; Med $=$ green) and thresholded high-uncertainty regions (top-20\% of proxy after morphological closing, $5{\times}5$ disk), coloured by severity: HIGH $=$ red fill; MOD $=$ orange fill.
    Hotspot labels (H1, H2, \ldots) mark bounding boxes; per-case clinical reports are placed \emph{below} each overlay panel (not stamped on the image) to avoid obscuring anatomical structures.
    \emph{Row~1 (Normal):} Hotspots concentrate diffusely along vascular margins and the pleural interface, consistent with signal-dependent quantum noise at vessel boundaries.
    \emph{Row~2 (Bacterial Pneumonia):} Hotspots shift to the lower lobes with irregular morphology, reflecting consolidation-driven local contrast gradients.
    \emph{Row~3 (Viral Pneumonia):} Bilateral hotspots with a more diffuse, ground-glass distribution, distinguishable from the focal bacterial pattern.
    The right-hand colourbar maps the shared inferno scale across all rows; the bottom legend identifies hotspot severity and anatomical zone colouring.
  }
  \label{fig:results:uncertainty:hotspot}
\end{figure}

\section{Implications for Clinical Translation}
\label{sec:disc:translation}

Clinical translation requires three sequential stages: (i) prospective reader study demonstrating diagnostic accuracy improvement; (ii) validation on real clinical DICOM data at full resolution with PACS-compatible deployment (INT8 quantisation, ONNX export); and (iii) EU MDR Class IIa or FDA 510(k) regulatory clearance, for which the calibrated uncertainty maps contribute to the explainability requirement.

The economic argument for deployment is quantifiable: a dose reduction of $1.5\times$ across paediatric CXR procedures at a medium-sized paediatric hospital performing $\sim5,000$ CXRs per year would reduce cumulative effective dose by $\sim1,500$~mSv per year across the paediatric population. At an estimated attributable cancer risk of $5.5\%$ per Sv for children~\cite{pearce_radiation_2012}, this corresponds to a reduction of approximately $0.08$ attributable cancer cases per year per site, a meaningful lifetime benefit that compounds across a screening lifetime.

In resource-limited settings (LMICs), where radiologist review capacity is severely constrained~\cite{world_health_organisation_classification_2020}, the uncertainty map serves an additional function as a \emph{triage signal}: cases with high reconstruction uncertainty can be flagged for review while cases with low uncertainty are cleared without specialist input, reducing the radiologist workload for the large majority of technically adequate scans.

Chapter~\ref{chap:conclusion} restates the principal contributions, answers each objective, and articulates the path to clinical deployment.

% ============================================================
% CHAPTER 6 — CONCLUSION
% ============================================================
\chapter{Conclusion}
\label{chap:conclusion}

\section{Summary of Work Undertaken}
\label{sec:conc:summary}

This dissertation designed, implemented, and evaluated PP-VAE-Hformer, a probabilistic denoising framework for low-dose paediatric CXR. The system combines a hybrid CNN-Transformer (Hformer) encoder-decoder with an optional VAE bottleneck and a four-term composite loss (NLL, MS-SSIM, FFL, KL). A 16-arm ablation study on the 624-image Kermany test set, with 210 Bonferroni-corrected pairwise comparisons against five retrained KAIR baselines (DnCNN, IRCNN, FFDNet, DRUNet, SwinIR), two extended SOTA baselines (NAFNet, SCUNet), BM3D, and DDPM, establishes which loss terms contribute to pixel fidelity, perceptual quality, and calibrated uncertainty quantification.

\section{Answering the Research Objectives}
\label{sec:conc:objectives}

\begin{enumerate}
  \item \textbf{Degradation modelling} (Objective~1) was met by the Foi Poisson-Gaussian pipeline (Section~\ref{sec:methods:noise}), which accurately models the signal-dependent quantum and read-out noise of paediatric CXR and provides three calibrated noise levels (low / mid / high) spanning a clinically relevant dose range.

  \item \textbf{Architecture and objective design} (Objective~2) was met by the 16-arm ablation study, which isolates the contribution of every loss term: NLL provides calibrated uncertainty ($-2.556$~nats/px in best arm); MS-SSIM+FFL together recover the NLL baseline's PSNR while adding perceptual benefit; cyclical KL prevents posterior collapse and enables VAE parity with CNN baselines.

  \item \textbf{Pathology preservation validation} (Objective~3) was met by the full quantitative comparison against five retrained baselines (DnCNN, IRCNN, FFDNet, DRUNet, SwinIR), the 210-pair pairwise statistical analysis, and the subgroup analysis demonstrating that all arms perform $\approx 1$~dB better on Pneumonia (pathological) than Normal images, confirming that the models are not biased against the pathology class.
  

  \item \textbf{Uncertainty quantification} (Objective~4) was met by evaluating aleatoric calibration via NLL and sharpness metrics across all NLL and VAE arms, and by demonstrating that the cyclical KL schedule produces better-calibrated uncertainty outputs (lower NLL, lower $\hat{\sigma}_a$) than the linear-warmup or free-bits schedules.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Principal Scientific Contributions}
\label{sec:conc:sci}

This work makes four contributions that were not established by prior literature at the time of writing:

\begin{enumerate}
  \item \textbf{Systematic ablation of composite loss design for probabilistic medical denoising.} The 16-arm study is the first to isolate the effects of NLL, SSIM, FFL, Sobel edge, and perceptual terms simultaneously in a single VAE denoising experiment on paediatric CXR, resolving their interactions rather than relying on literature-motivated assumptions.

  \item \textbf{Demonstration of the SSIM--edge gradient interference effect.} The $-1.747$~dB PSNR collapse of Arm~M (adding Sobel edge supervision to NLL+SSIM+FFL) is a novel empirical finding with direct implications for composite loss design: these two structural regularisers are mutually destructive when applied simultaneously.

  \item \textbf{Cyclical KL annealing as the critical VAE design variable.} The $1.10$~dB gap between linear-warmup (Arm~E, $31.635$~dB) and cyclical (Arm~F, $32.734$~dB) KL schedules confirms that schedule design, not the VAE architecture itself, determines whether the probabilistic bottleneck is a performance cost or a net-zero feature.

  \item \textbf{CNN inductive bias advantage over Transformers for Poisson-Gaussian noise.} The $\approx 1.5$~dB advantage of the CNN KAIR baselines over SwinIR ($d \approx 1.4$--$1.6$, $p \approx 0$) is a statistically unambiguous finding for this noise type and domain, informing the architecture choice for future low-dose imaging systems.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Clinical Implications}
\label{sec:conc:clinical}

The immediate implication of this work is that a simple change in training objective (from MSE to NLL) is sufficient to add calibrated per-pixel uncertainty maps to an existing denoising architecture, at a cost of $\approx 1.2$~dB PSNR relative to the MSE baseline and $0.66$~dB better PSNR than a standard DnCNN baseline. For paediatric CXR specifically, where repeat acquisition carries a measurable carcinogenic risk, an uncertainty map that flags problematic reconstructions provides a meaningful clinical safety benefit: it converts an implicit confidence in the reconstruction into an explicit, quantified signal available to the radiologist at the moment of reporting.

The longer-term translational pathway (reader study, full-resolution DICOM validation, regulatory submission) is outlined in Section~\ref{sec:disc:translation}. This pathway is technically feasible with the framework developed here; the principal remaining barriers are organisational (access to paired low-dose clinical data) rather than methodological.

\section{Future Work}
\label{sec:conc:future}

Three priorities emerge: (i) a blinded radiologist reader study on Arms~D and~H with uncertainty maps; (ii) disentangled VAE variants ($\beta$-TCVAE) to recover the reconstruction/class-separation trade-off exposed by Arm~F (ARI~$0.333$); (iii) validation on full-resolution real DICOM data to bridge the simulated-to-clinical domain gap.

\section{Closing Statement}
\label{sec:conc:closing}

A chest radiograph is often the first, and occasionally the only, diagnostic window into the lungs of a sick child. Every unnecessary dose of radiation represents a small but real addition to a lifetime cancer risk for a patient who has decades of life ahead. The framework developed in this dissertation demonstrates that it is possible to reduce that dose further while simultaneously providing the clinician with an honest, calibrated measure of confidence in the reconstruction, not by building a larger or more complex model, but by asking the training objective a better-formulated question. The question is not ``how closely does this reconstruction match the clean image?'' but ``how confident are you, pixel by pixel, that this reconstruction is correct?'' That shift in question is the central contribution of this work, and the answer it produces,a calibrated uncertainty map alongside every denoised image, is the mechanism by which machine learning can earn its place in the paediatric imaging pathway.

C  & NLL+SSIM              & 200 & 8.9 & \\
N  & NLL+Perc+SSIM+FFL     & 200 & 9.2 & \\
E  & PP-VAE, linear KL     & 149 & 6.6 & Stopped at posterior collapse \\
F  & PP-VAE, cyclical KL   & 208 & 9.3 & Extra cycle completed \\
G  & PP-VAE, free-bits KL  & 200 & 8.9 & \\
H  & PP-VAE, cyc+fb KL     & 200 & 8.9 & \\
P  & PP-VAE full, PReLU    & 129 & 5.7 & Stopped: instability \\
M  & NLL+SSIM+Edge+FFL     & 202 & 8.9 & \\
O  & NLL+SSIM+FFL, PReLU   & 142 & 6.2 & Stopped: catastrophic failure \\
\midrule
\multicolumn{3}{l}{\textbf{Total (ablation arms)}} & $\approx 160$ & \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

% ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\chapter{Additional Ablation Results}
\label{app:ablations}

Systematic sub-ablations of individual loss weights (e.g.\ a $5\times5$ grid over $\lambda_{\mathrm{edge}} \times \lambda_{\mathrm{perc}}$) were not conducted within this project due to computational constraints: each 200-epoch arm requires approximately $8$--$10$ GPU-hours on the A100, making a full sensitivity grid ($\sim$250 arms) infeasible within the project timeline. The cross-arm comparisons in Chapter~4 serve as a coarser sensitivity analysis: comparing Arms~B, D, K, L, and~C isolates the marginal contribution of individual NLL composite terms; comparing Arms~E, F, G, H, and~P isolates the contribution of KL schedule design. Full loss-weight sensitivity surfaces are identified as a priority for future work (Section~\ref{sec:disc:future}).

% ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\chapter{Supplementary Statistical Tables}
\label{app:suptables}

All statistically significant pairwise comparisons ($d \geq 0.2$, $p < \alpha_{\mathrm{Bonf}} = 2.4 \times 10^{-4}$) are reported inline within Section~4.3.2. Key comparisons selected for the main table (Table~\ref{tab:results:stats:pairwise}) include all cross-family pairs and the most clinically informative within-family pairs. A full 21$\times$21 pairwise comparison matrix is available in the project GitHub repository (\texttt{results/pairwise\_stats.csv}).

Experiments used NVIDIA A100 40\,GB GPUs on the Ampere partition under SLURM job scheduling. Table~\ref{tab:app:software} lists the primary software dependencies and Table~\ref{tab:app:compute:timing} summarises per-arm training times. \chapter{Reproducibility Checklist}
\label{app:repro}

% Structured checklist following Pineau et al. (2021) ML Reproducibility Checklist.
% Cover: data, model, training, evaluation.

\begin{enumerate}
  \item Dataset: Is the dataset publicly available? \textbf{Yes}, MedMNIST v2, ChestMNIST split (Kermany paediatric CXR). DOI:~\href{https://doi.org/10.17632/rscbjbr9sj.2}{10.17632/rscbjbr9sj.2}; MedMNIST v2 accessed via \texttt{pip install medmnist==3.0.1} (June 2024).
  \item Dataset: Is the version and access date documented? \textbf{Yes}, MedMNIST v2.1, ChestMNIST; accessed June 2024. Official train/val/test CSV splits used without modification.
  \item Dataset: Are train/val/test splits identical to official splits? \textbf{Yes}, the official MedMNIST v2 splits (4,172~train / 16~val / 624~test) were used as provided; no re-splitting was performed.
  \item Model: Is the architecture fully specified? \textbf{Yes}, Section~3.3 and Appendix~\ref{app:hyperparams}.
  \item Model: Are all hyperparameters reported? \textbf{Yes}, Table~\ref{tab:app:hyperparams:shared}, Table~\ref{tab:app:hyperparams:arms}, and Table~\ref{tab:methods:loss_summary}.
  \item Training: Is the random seed fixed and reported? \textbf{Yes}, seed~42 for PyTorch, NumPy, and Python \texttt{random} (Table~\ref{tab:app:hyperparams:shared}).
  \item Training: Is the optimiser and schedule fully specified? \textbf{Yes}, Adam, lr~$=2\times10^{-4}$, weight\_decay~$=10^{-5}$, constant schedule (Table~\ref{tab:app:hyperparams:shared}).
  \item Evaluation: Are metrics computed on held-out test set only? \textbf{Yes}, all reported PSNR/SSIM/LPIPS/NLL/FSIM values are computed on the 624-image held-out test set (Section~3.5).
  \item Evaluation: Are confidence intervals or standard errors reported? \textbf{Yes}, Tables~4.2--4.3 report mean~$\pm$ SD. Pairwise comparisons report Cohen's~$d$, 95\% CI, and Bonferroni-corrected $p$-values (Table~4.5).
  \item Code: Is the codebase publicly available? \textbf{Yes}, \url{https://github.com/teklesam/pp-vae-hformer}.
\end{enumerate}

\chapter{Ethical Considerations}
\label{app:ethics}

\section{Dataset Ethics}
\label{app:ethics:dataset}

The dataset used in this study, the Kermany paediatric chest radiograph collection,was assembled under ethics approval granted by the Guangzhou Women and Children's Medical Centre, China, as described in Kermany et al.\ \cite{kermany_identifying_2018}. The dataset is publicly available under a CC~BY~4.0 licence on the Mendeley Data repository (DOI: 10.17632/rscbjbr9sj.2). No personally identifiable information is present in the dataset: images are identified only by a sequential index and a class label (Normal or Pneumonia), with patient names, dates, and demographic details removed prior to public release.

This study uses a pre-existing, de-identified, publicly available dataset. No new patient data were collected. No clinical interventions were performed. No direct patient contact occurred. Under the University of Cambridge ethics framework for computational research using pre-existing anonymised data, no additional ethics approval beyond that obtained for the original Kermany collection is required for this study.

\section{Model Safety and Clinical Deployment}
\label{app:ethics:deployment}

The PP-VAE-Hformer model developed in this study has \textbf{not been validated for clinical deployment} and \textbf{must not be used to inform diagnostic decisions} in a clinical setting without prospective clinical validation against the applicable regulatory standards (e.g., UK MDR 2002 and IVDR 2022 as implemented post-Brexit, or FDA 510(k) clearance in the US). The following limitations are relevant to any future clinical translation:

\begin{itemize}
  \item \textbf{Evaluation dataset.} The model was trained and evaluated exclusively on the Kermany paediatric CXR dataset from a single institution (Guangzhou Women and Children's Medical Centre). External validation on images from other scanners, institutions, patient populations, and age groups has not been performed.
  \item \textbf{Noise model.} The Poisson-Gaussian noise simulation, while physically grounded, is an approximation. Real detector noise includes additional sources (scatter, beam hardening, patient motion artefacts) not captured by the Foi model. Performance on real low-dose acquisitions may differ from the simulated results reported here.
  \item \textbf{Uncertainty quantification.} The aleatoric and epistemic uncertainty maps produced by PP-VAE arms are research outputs intended to facilitate model interpretability analysis. They are not calibrated clinical risk scores and have not been validated as decision-support tools.
  \item \textbf{Pathology preservation.} While the pathology-preserving design objective is motivated by clinical concerns, preservation was assessed qualitatively (visual inspection of reconstructions) and quantitatively only via PSNR/SSIM/LPIPS. No radiologist reading study was performed, and pathology-preservation claims are therefore limited to the reconstruction fidelity domain.
\end{itemize}

\section{Responsible AI Principles}
\label{app:ethics:ai}

All computational work, model training, evaluation, and scientific conclusions in this dissertation are the sole work of the author. Standard scientific computing tools and libraries (PyTorch, NumPy, Matplotlib, \LaTeX) were used throughout. All code was written, reviewed, and verified by the author.

% ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\chapter{Mathematical Derivations}
\label{app:maths}

\section{ELBO Derivation}
\label{app:maths:elbo}

The evidence lower bound (ELBO) is derived from Jensen's inequality applied to the log-marginal likelihood. For a latent variable model with observed $x$, latent $z$, generative model $p_\theta(x|z)p(z)$, and approximate posterior $q_\phi(z|x)$:

\begin{align}
\log p_\theta(x) &= \log \int p_\theta(x|z) p(z) \, \mathrm{d}z \nonumber \\
&= \log \int \frac{q_\phi(z|x)}{q_\phi(z|x)} p_\theta(x|z) p(z) \, \mathrm{d}z \nonumber \\
&= \log \mathbb{E}_{q_\phi(z|x)}\!\left[\frac{p_\theta(x|z) p(z)}{q_\phi(z|x)}\right] \nonumber \\
&\geq \mathbb{E}_{q_\phi(z|x)}\!\left[\log \frac{p_\theta(x|z) p(z)}{q_\phi(z|x)}\right]
  \quad \text{(Jensen's inequality, $\log$ is concave)} \nonumber \\
&= \underbrace{\mathbb{E}_{q_\phi(z|x)}\bigl[\log p_\theta(x|z)\bigr]}_{\text{reconstruction}} -
   \underbrace{D_{\mathrm{KL}}\bigl[q_\phi(z|x) \| p(z)\bigr]}_{\text{regularisation}}
   \;\eqqcolon\; \mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{ELBO}}(\theta, \phi; x)
\label{eq:app:elbo}
\end{align}

The gap between $\log p_\theta(x)$ and $\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{ELBO}}$ is exactly $D_{\mathrm{KL}}[q_\phi(z|x) \| p_\theta(z|x)] \geq 0$. Maximising $\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{ELBO}}$ simultaneously maximises the log-likelihood and minimises this posterior gap.

\paragraph{Reparameterisation.}
The reconstruction term $\mathbb{E}_{q_\phi}[\log p_\theta(x|z)]$ requires gradients through a stochastic sample $z \sim q_\phi(z|x) = \mathcal{N}(\mu_\phi(x), \mathrm{diag}(\sigma^2_\phi(x)))$. Direct Monte Carlo estimation of $\nabla_\phi \mathbb{E}_{q_\phi}[\cdot]$ via the score function estimator has prohibitively high variance. The reparameterisation trick writes:
\begin{equation}
z = \mu_\phi(x) + \sigma_\phi(x) \odot \varepsilon, \quad \varepsilon \sim \mathcal{N}(0, I)
\label{eq:app:reparam}
\end{equation}
so that the stochasticity is moved to the noise variable $\varepsilon$, which does not depend on $\phi$. Gradients $\nabla_{\theta,\phi} \mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{ELBO}}$ can then flow through the deterministic function of $\varepsilon$ via standard backpropagation. In our spatial VAE, $\mu_\phi$ and $\log \sigma^2_\phi$ are spatially-indexed feature maps output by the encoder, and Equation~\eqref{eq:app:reparam} is applied pixel-wise across the spatial latent map.

\section{Closed-Form KL Divergence}
\label{app:maths:kl}

When both $q$ and $p$ are diagonal Gaussians, $q = \mathcal{N}(\mu, \mathrm{diag}(\sigma^2))$ and $p = \mathcal{N}(0, I)$, the KL divergence has a closed-form expression:

\begin{align}
D_{\mathrm{KL}}[q \| p]
&= \int q(z) \log \frac{q(z)}{p(z)} \, \mathrm{d}z \nonumber \\
&= \frac{1}{2} \sum_{j=1}^{J} \left(
    \mu_j^2 + \sigma_j^2 - 1 - \log \sigma_j^2
   \right)
\label{eq:app:kl}
\end{align}

where $j$ indexes the $J$ latent dimensions. This follows from direct computation of the difference of two Gaussian log-densities:
\begin{equation}
\log q(z) - \log p(z)
= -\tfrac{1}{2}\textstyle\sum_j \left(\log 2\pi + \log \sigma_j^2 + \frac{(z_j - \mu_j)^2}{\sigma_j^2}\right)
+ \tfrac{1}{2}\textstyle\sum_j \left(\log 2\pi + z_j^2\right)
\end{equation}
Taking the expectation under $q(z)$ and using $\mathbb{E}_q[z_j] = \mu_j$, $\mathbb{E}_q[z_j^2] = \mu_j^2 + \sigma_j^2$, $\mathbb{E}_q[(z_j - \mu_j)^2/\sigma_j^2] = 1$ yields Equation~\eqref{eq:app:kl}. In our implementation, $J$ indexes the $C_z \times (H/8) \times (W/8)$ elements of the spatial latent map, and the KL is summed across all latent dimensions and then averaged across the batch.

\section{Differentiability of Composite Loss}
\label{app:maths:grad}

Each component of the composite loss $\mathcal{L}$ (Equation~\ref{eq:intro:totalloss}) is differentiable with respect to the decoder output $\hat{\mu}$ and log-variance $\hat{\sigma}_a^2 = \exp(\hat{s})$:

\begin{itemize}
  \item $\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{MSE}} = \|\hat{\mu} - y\|_2^2$: differentiable (quadratic), $\nabla_{\hat{\mu}} = 2(\hat{\mu}-y)$.

  \item $\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{L1}} = \|\hat{\mu} - y\|_1$: differentiable a.e.\ (subgradient at zero; in practice, a smooth Charbonnier approximation $\sqrt{(\hat{\mu}-y)^2 + \epsilon^2}$ is used by SwinIR and is fully differentiable).

  \item $\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{NLL}} = \frac{1}{2}\!\left(\hat{s} + (\hat{\mu}-y)^2 e^{-\hat{s}}\right)$: differentiable jointly in $\hat{\mu}$ and $\hat{s}$:
    $\nabla_{\hat{\mu}} = (\hat{\mu}-y)e^{-\hat{s}}$;
    $\nabla_{\hat{s}} = \frac{1}{2}\left(1 - (\hat{\mu}-y)^2 e^{-\hat{s}}\right)$.

  \item $\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{KL}}$: differentiable via the reparameterisation trick (Section~\ref{app:maths:elbo}); $\nabla_{\mu_j} = \mu_j$, $\nabla_{\log\sigma_j^2} = \frac{1}{2}(\sigma_j^2 - 1)$.

  \item $\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{SSIM}}$: differentiable via the PyTorch differentiable SSIM implementation (windowed local statistics, no $\arg\max$ or hard thresholds).

  \item $\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{FFL}}$ (Focal Frequency Loss): differentiable via the DFT, which is a linear operation; PyTorch's \texttt{torch.fft.fft2} supports autograd.

  \item $\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{edge}}$: differentiable via Kornia's differentiable Canny edge detector, which replaces the hard threshold with a sigmoid-smoothed hysteresis step.

  \item $\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{perc}}$ (Perceptual loss via VGG-16): differentiable through the frozen VGG-16 feature extractor by standard backpropagation; the network weights are fixed, so no second-order computation is required.
\end{itemize}

The full composite loss gradient $\nabla_\theta \mathcal{L}$ is therefore well-defined for all parameter configurations in this study, and no subgradient approximations are required at any training step.

% ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\chapter{Arm E: Posterior-Collapse Epistemic Uncertainty Maps}
\label{app:arm_e_epistemic}

Arm~E (PP-VAE, linear KL warmup; $31.635$~dB) is the only arm in this study exhibiting full posterior collapse. Under a monotonically increasing KL weight schedule, the encoder's approximate posterior is driven toward the prior $\mathcal{N}(0,I)$ without any mechanism to resist collapse, resulting in $D_{\mathrm{KL}}[q_\phi(z|x)\,\|\,p(z)] < 0.01$~nats per spatial latent position and an encoder posterior variance $\hat{\sigma}^2_e \approx (4 \times 10^{-5})^2$. Monte Carlo sampling for epistemic uncertainty estimation draws $z^{(k)} = \hat{\mu}_\phi(x) + \hat{\sigma}_\phi(x) \odot \varepsilon^{(k)}$ with $\varepsilon^{(k)} \sim \mathcal{N}(0,I)$; when $\hat{\sigma}_\phi \approx 4\times10^{-5}$, all $K=30$ draws are numerically identical, and the empirical variance across draws is uniformly $\approx\!10^{-9}$. This produces the flat dark-blue field visible in the rightmost column of Figures~\ref{fig:app:qual:e:bacterial} and~\ref{fig:app:qual:e:normal} (plasma colourmap, $[0, \mathrm{p}_{95}]$ range floored at $10^{-3}$).

This is the theoretically correct and expected consequence of posterior collapse, not a visualisation error. The aleatoric maps $\hat{\sigma}_a$ (inferno colourmap, fourth column) remain structurally informative because the heteroscedastic reconstruction head is a separate output branch whose calibration is governed by the NLL reconstruction term, which is unaffected by KL collapse. The contrast between flat $\hat{\sigma}_e$ maps here and anatomically structured $\hat{\sigma}_e$ maps in Arm~H (cyclical KL; Figure~\ref{fig:results:qual:h:bacterial}) directly demonstrates the practical consequence of KL schedule design for epistemic uncertainty quality.

\begin{figure}[!htb]
\centering
\evalfig{figures/evaluation_v2/qualitative/qualitative_arm_e_ppvae_bacterial}
\caption[Arm E (PP-VAE, linear KL) -- Bacterial Pneumonia cases]{%
  Arm~E (PP-VAE, linear KL warmup; $31.635$~dB) on three Bacterial Pneumonia test cases.
  \textit{Columns:} Noisy $|$ Reference $|$ $\hat{\mu}$ (reconstruction) $|$ $\hat{\sigma}_a$ (aleatoric, inferno) $|$ $|\hat{\mu}-y|$ (error, hot) $|$ $\hat{\sigma}_e$ (epistemic, plasma).
  The rightmost column is uniformly dark blue ($\hat{\sigma}_e \approx 4\times10^{-5}$ everywhere), reflecting complete posterior collapse under linear KL warmup: all Monte Carlo latent samples are numerically identical, producing zero epistemic variance. The aleatoric head remains functional. Contrast with Arm~H (Figure~\ref{fig:results:qual:h:bacterial}), where cyclical KL prevents collapse and produces anatomically structured epistemic maps.}
\label{fig:app:qual:e:bacterial}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[!htb]
\centering
\evalfig{figures/evaluation_v2/qualitative/qualitative_arm_e_ppvae_normal}
\caption[Arm E (PP-VAE, linear KL) -- Normal cases]{%
  Arm~E on three Normal test cases. As in Figure~\ref{fig:app:qual:e:bacterial}, the epistemic column ($\hat{\sigma}_e$) is uniformly suppressed by posterior collapse. Boundary-localised $\hat{\sigma}_a$ (inferno colourmap) is preserved relative to Arm~D (Figure~\ref{fig:results:qual:d:normal}), confirming that the heteroscedastic head remains well-calibrated despite the latent-space collapse. The $1.10$~dB PSNR deficit relative to Arm~F and the flat epistemic maps jointly motivate the transition from linear to cyclical KL annealing in Arms~F--H.}
\label{fig:app:qual:e:normal}
\end{figure}
